BEFORE THE MIRROR
By “JEANNETTE.”
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and
all of us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow
over the lives of many of us women, and while perhaps a few—the more
stoical among us—can avoid worrying over past and prospective events we
may all, with a little care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results
of worry from being noticeable in our appearance. To these fortunate
few this little chat will, however, be as interesting and instructive as to
those who stand in real need of help in toilet matters, and may be the
means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown method of retain-
ing or regaining that beauty of face and figure which is the birthright of
every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present not
.generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Wrinkles, and How to Remove them.—Worry,
late hours or the passing of old Father Time
may cause those ugly little lines which so
detract from the freshness and charm of a
woman’s face; but the cure Is always the
same. Feed the tissues beneath the skin, and
massage them with a reliable skin iood, which,
while nourishing the fatty tissues, will not
coarsen the skin nor grow hairs upon the
face, as so many inferior creams do. To
remove the wrinkles, bathe the face with hot
water, wipe it dry, then, while it is still
warm, smear a little mercolized wax over the
whole face, carrying it well down on the neck,
and, with the tips of the fingers, rub it gentlv
into the pores, working in an upward and
outward direction. Wipe off any superfluous
wax, leaving a little on the skin all night.
In the morning wash off with warm water
and Pilenta soap. The use of the wax not
only feeds the skin, but removes the dead
outer cuticle, giving the fresh young skin
beneath an opportunity to breathe and show
itself.
Beautiful Hair—How to Shampoo.—Whether
the hair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky, depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the hair in good
condition it should be shampooed once in
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing,
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful of
stallax granules in a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which the ends
of the hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax
mixture into the scalp in the usual way.
Rinse and dry by fanning with a palm fan, or
in the open air, if the weather permits. Should
the hair be naturally very greasy, the oil
massage should be omitted. Stallax can be
bought at any chemist’s in quarter-pound
sealed packages; It keeps indefinitely, and one
package contains sufficient for 25 or 30
-shampoos.
A Greasy Skin and Blackheads—Blackheads
are absolutely fatal to beauty, for they give a
coarse, dirty look to the face which no cos-
metics can ever hide. The way to remove
blackheads quickly and effectively has only
recently been discovered. For many years those
who suffered in this way were restricted in
their diet, denied sweets and had to suffer
other privations. Modern science has, how-
ever, provided a simple, effective and quick
remedy. Dissolve one stymol tablet in a
glass of hot water, and when the effervescence
has subsided, dip a small, soft sponge into the
liquid and bathe the face. Leave for a few
minutes, then dry with a towel, and the black-
heads will come off. For a greasy skin bathe
the face three times a week with stymol and
spray with cold water every morning, using a
vulcanite throat spray.
To Improve the Eyebrows and Eyelashes.—
Long, curling eyelashes make the most in-
different eyes look attractive, and. while no
one can alter the color, shape or size of the
eyes, all may possess luxuriant eyelashes and
well-shaped eyebrows. To increase the growth
of the eyelashes rub a little mennaline into-
the roots very gently every night. Mennaline
is a perfectly harmless and delicate pomade-
like substance which may be obtained at any
chemist’s. For the eyebrows smear a little of
the mennaline on a small soft brush, and
brush into the eyebrows in a straight or
arched direction, according to how you wish
them to grow. A month’s treatment should
see a marked improvement.
ANSWERS
TO CORRESPONDENTS.
CAIRO.—No, I am not surprised at hearing
from a reader so far away, as I get letters
from every part of the world. I expect it is
the heat that has caused you to put on weight
so rapidly, and can quite understand how
much it adds to your age. Get a small quan-
tity of clynol berries and eat one after each
meal. They will permanently reduce your
weight, are quite harmless, and you need
make no alteration in your diet.
QUEEN BESS.—Thirty is far too young to
have grey hairs, and you must not allow the
trouble to increase. Get two ounces of tamma-
lite. mix it with three ounces of bay rum, and
apply to the hair with a small, soft sponge.
This will restore the grey and fading hair to
its original color.
EMMELINE.—For your muddy-lookiug skin
get a tin of mercolised wax and rub a little
well into the face and neck every night. Leave
on till morning, then wash off, using Pilenta
soap and warm water. This will remove the
dead outer skin and leave the complexion soft,
fresh and smooth.
JESSIE.—To remove the hairs from the lip
and chin get an ounce of powdered pheminol
and use as directed. Then apply tekko paste
several times daily for some weeks. This com-
bined treatment will permanently remove all
trace of liair from your face.
FRECKLED LASS.—To prevent the freckles
from appearing use a cleminite lotion. Get an
ounce of cleminite and mix it with four ounces
of warm water, allow to cool, then dab over
the face. This will not only protect the skin
from sunburn and freckles, but will give it a
dainty fresh look far superior to the bloom
imparted by the finest powder. [3

The Red Page
THE CONQUEROR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One aged man, however, expresses his individuality
remaining constant to the English Dictionary.
He never asks the librarian for anything else. His
standing order is ‘Another volume of the Dic-
tionary.’’’—Description of the South Yarra Braille
Library for the Blind.
The serried rows of dots spring into life
As down the page his lean white fingers pry ;
With noiseless clamor and with silent strife
The regiments of words troop slowly by,
Clad in the uniforms of earth and sky.
Their level ranks show bannerets which tell
Of valor, joys and laughter through the years,
And this one points to Heaven, and that to Hell,
While Birth and Death make mockery of tears.
He is their captain; he commands a band
.As vast, all-seeing, as the long-lost sun.
He counts his forces with a cunning hand,
And lingers on the value of each one.
Here treason may be feared, and these may run—
Riches, ambition, power, hate and dread.
He calls the roll of stauncher soldiers—rest,
And love, and beer, and sunshine, and white bread,
The wholesome syllables which fight the best.
And now he draws aside a pace and talks
With summer on the wideness of the skies ;
Or down the long, dim lane of memory walks
Beside the blue which once hid in her eyes,
Before the shadow came to make him wise.
When dawn glows on the page the misty night
Is shot with shafts of crimson, gold and pink;
The letters with the hordes of darkness fight,
With pauses now and then to sigh or wink.
This is no hermit telling wordy beads
And moping in the dust with head bowed low;
He is a captain strong whose firm hand leads
In search of continents where thoughts may
grow—
A conqueror whose march is safe but slow.
In his dark tent he sits alone with pride
To feel that earth and life are in his grip;
His soldiers bring him all things in their stride
Beneath the sceptre of his finger-tip.
VICTORIA.
DALE COLLINS.
Bathsheba do Medici of Franco -
Israel
She was an Old Testament lady who
diverted the whole course of the world’s his-
tory—Bathsheba de Medici. Catherine de
Medici was her reincarnation, these ladies
being the joint mother of Henry Solomon
111., otherwise Solomon de Valois.
David stands out conspicuously in Hebrew
history. Like Saul he was the creation
of Samuel, the stout, sour king-maker,
whose personal grab at hereditary power
had failed through the inefficiency of his
sons. He was nominated by the king-
maker when Saul proved not sufficiently
subservient. First he was a soldier in the
King’s army with a reputation because he
killed a Philistine with a long shot when
the Philistine, having only a spear, couldn’t
retaliate. He was the King’s son-in-law.
Later he was a suspect; then a brigand;
then a general in the enemy’s army. Next
he was a petty sub-king at Hebron reigning
as a Philistine deputy. Finally a furious
civil war made him King of Israel—the only
one who held down the people with a stand-
ing army of mercenary idolaters —the
Cherethites, Pelethites and Gittites. To
find work and pay for them he went out on
a career of conquest and built a little empire.
He got control of Damascus and lands be-
yond, with the royal estates of the previous
sovereign, also the land-tax, income-tatf, poll-
tax and dog-tax and all the customs revenue
on one of the great stock-routes of Western
Asia. He took Moab and Ammon and the
ports at the head of the Red Sea, and sat
in the custom-house there. For once poor
little agricultural Israel was a great Pow r er
and a bloated capitalist and was able to build
temples, palaces and a home for the Young
Men’s Jewish Association.
In the time of David’s greatness, when he
was marrying frequently, Bathsheba de
Medici washed herself in public by way of
doing a mash, and caught the loose and rov-
ing royal eye. The King sent for her and
she did 100 yards in even time. It isn’t
even hinted that she made objection to being
stolen or to the putting of her foreign hus-
band out of the way so that she might be
promoted from mistress to wife. It was
the unpardonable sin, and Israel had no use
for Bathsheba, the lady of swift character,
or for her son, Henry Solomon de Valois
of dubious legitimacy. But she stood to
her guns, or at least to her bow and arrow;
and the army of trained and armor-plated
idolaters was behind her. The mothers of
ttie King s elder sons fade off the story.
Possibly they were set to cooking or mend-
“lg - . The elder sons grew wild and alarmed
2 f sa w that Bathsheba’s kid was
destined for the throne.
Absalom made his bid for the succession,
and the people were with him. There is a
P f f dented, dissipated and helter-
skelter Psalmist fleeing across the Jordan to
touc *. w , lth his mercenaries as they
fU Urr T» « outh from Damascus, north from
the Red Sea ports, and west from Moab and
Ahithophel. Absalom’s friend,
urged the new monarch to follow with such
forces as he had and end the war. Hushai,
the old monarch’s hired spy, persuaded him
to wait till ho had collected the whole
national tumult of farmers armed with
®b a .de s a &d °f. shopmen with yardsticks.
Ahithophel, realising that that meant giving
time for the hired and disciplined unbelievers
to get together, wisely hanged himself. As
the event proved, the mercenaries made hay
of the tumult of Israel and of Absalom also.
David avoided the disgrace of slaughtering
his own people in Bathsheba’s cause by lurk-
ing in the background.
Bathsheba still forged ahead. She wore
the Crown jewels and painted herself every
afternoon. The family of Saul, which
might have provided a candidate for the
throne, was hanged in a row. The bones
of Saul were dug up and chucked about.
David grew old and imbecile, and doddered
about with a new wife, who was a cipher
and didn’t signify. Adonijah, apparently
the last of the elder sons, felt his head loose
on his shoulders, and reached out for the
kingship. Bathsheba rushed the position,
and her dubious son, a mere boy, was made
sovereign before the old man was dead. The
consent of the Popular Assembly, considered
essential in the cases of Saul and David,
wasn’t asked. It wouldn’t have been given
in any case. Bathsheba breathed heavily
in her son’s ear, and he murdered his half-
brother Adonijah, so that there were no more
claimants. So Bathsheba de Medici
appears first as a lady who lost her honor
and her husband, and last as a lady who
lost her stepson. She was a great loser.
Henry Solomon 111. reigned long and
expensively. He made friends by much out-
lay with the priests who wrote Scripture,
and they extolled his wisdom, of which they
could furnish only one sample—a judgment
about a disputed child that any detective
might have guessed at. Solomon was a
great loser like his mother. He lost his
father's new empire. He consequently lost
the revenue. He apparently lost the army
of hired idolators, for it drops out of history.
Possibly it drifted to some more enter-
prising' leader. He lost the goodwill of
his people because, when funds ran low, he
was the first ruler who taxed Israel. He
lost his religion. Judging by the Book of
Ecclesiastes, he also lost his hair and his
good spirits and all the joy of life. It is
assumed that he kept his sons locked up in
the harem lest they should rise against him
in his old bald days. Anyhow, only one
of them is mentioned, and that not till the
weary usurper was dead. Rehoboam looked
like the product of a harem.
Then the bulk of the people said that they
wanted no more of Bathsheba’s offspring to
reign over them. The little agricultural
State broke into two little agricultural frag
ments; and they fought with each other like
two little agricultural cats till not much re-
mained Ultimately what might have been
a great empire was stolen and scattered by
strangers. And its failure was the begin-
ning of the failure of the Semites, which
ended with the fall of Carthage, and it
caused the transfer of the sceptre to Europe
ana made Lenin and Trotsky possible.
It is said on poor authority that the ghost
of Bathsheba de Medici still washes itself
in Jerusalem on Saturday nights and shows
signs of great misery. Her son, and her re-
incarnation Catherine de Medici, and her
sons reincarnation, Henry de Valois, wash
there also m the spirit and look even more
wretched than the old woman.
JAMES EDMOND.
Edward Dyson
When Edward Dyson published his
“Rhymes from the Mines” all Australia
knew that a verseman of distinction had
arisen. They knew more than that; for
although only occasionally in that early
volume Dyson attempted loftier flights of
song, there was here and there a singing
quality in his verse, here and there an indi-
cation of finer and more intense feeling that
stamped him as a poet. That was many
years ago. Since then he has written much
in prosa and verse that was strikingly good,
and always there was the undercurrent of
poetry; but very seldom did his best and
finest gain its full expression. In his most
recent volume, Hello, Soldier! which he
describes as “khaki verse,” Dyson keeps
about his average. The work is almost uni-
oimly good, full of point and sometimes
lull or humor; it. shows the true craftsman
m To per cent, of the lines; but the genuine
poetry is rather hinted at than revealed
After all, war verse is not likely to be
specially poetical. The great struggle, first
and last, has produced very little song that
will live; but the stir and change of four
tremendous years did give great opportuni-
ties for journalistic verse—verse rude and
ready and fashioned to the needs of the
moment. To say that the bulk of Dyson’s
’haki verse” is of this order is to speak
n(^k IS iF raiSe ‘ I f e attem Pted valiant things
with his pen and he has succeeded as well
as any but the very best.
Here is a bit of fancy that would be hard
to beat:
Th e fl itting bullets flow and flock,
They twitter as they pass; *
Th ev re picking at the solid rock,
They re rooting in the grass.
A tiny ballet swiftly throws
Its gossamer of rust,
Brown fairies on their little toes
A-dancing in the dust.
And here, again, is a picture that lives and
moves;
The township woke, and the doors flew wide -
The women trotted their bovs beside '
Across the bridge on a single heel
The soldiers came in a golden glow
With throb of song and chink of steel.
The gallant crow of the piccolo.
Good and brown they were,
And their arms swung bare.
Their fine young faces revived in me
A boyhood’s vision of chivalry.
Or again:
There came the vibrant bugle note
N °ne cowered at the high alarm, '
* s , te ii dy , fleets were still afloat,
And England saw her children arm,
readily, with sober grace
The close-set ranks swung into ’place.
Not high poetry this, perhaps, but good stuff

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Autumn Evening.
Wild winds are shaking the blue wren’s
cover;
The sea has moods like a wearying lover;
The whole wide world is wet with rain,
As the wild-fowl stream to the north again ;
The paths are livid and dank with loam;
Ghostly hands on the panes go drumming;
The light streams out from the heart if
home.
Why do you linger? When are you com-
ing?
The air grows poignant with peewits call-
ing:
“The leaves are falling! The leaves are
falling!”
The twilight scarf of the wind-wan eve
Streams in tatters; her filmy sleeve
In rainy runnels trickles and clings
As she touches the heart with a clammy
finger
That opes the door for uneasy things.
When will you comet Ah, why do you
lingert
N.S.W.
NINA MURDOCH.
Brig o’ Balgownie.
Soft the pale feet of the morning pace toward
the yellow day,
To the lilting in the rushes where the fairy
pipers play;
To the singing of the river garmenting her
maiden loins
With a wondrous water-mantle like a mesh
of tiny coins;
Like a shower of silver music rinkling o’er
the little stones,
Wonderful upon the vision, marvellous with
many tones;
Yet mv fancy follows onward to the glade
around the turn.
Where the Auld Brig o’ Balgownie lightly
steps across the burn.
Voicing with the speech of beauty all the
dignity of Age,
As the glow of golden graving lights the mis-
sal’s mellow page.
Lo! the Auld Brig o’ Balgownie, chanting
there its magic ’rhyme,
Leaps the torrent of the Present from the
shadowed shore of Time;
Till I hear a richer cadence throbbing to a
measured march,
History in crimson trappings proudly rides
across the arch ;
All the banners of Tradition roll their folds
across the mind,
Billowing where the years rush backward
storming like a. mighty wind.
Gracing more the grace of morning tranquil
in the shaded place,
Bides the Auld Brig o’ Balgownie with its
heart upon its face;
Love-child of the master-builder, joyous his
white hand to feel,
Moving ’mid the patient granite at the tap-
ping of the steel;
Standing now like Galatea with her lover at
her knees;
Mingling still a wistful music with the flutes
among the trees;
Till the beating heart within me wakes to
melody and sings.
As the warm hand of Emotion sweetly sweeps
across the strings.
Held by all the bonds of Beauty, softly now
I surely feel
Saffron paths like tiny fingers tugging, tug-
ging at my heel;
Upward from the flashing waters they shall
bear my gipsy feet.
Growing ’neath the throbbing foot-beat to
the tumult of the street;
I must go to where the white winds wait
beside the leagues of sea,
Beckoning to the ruling skyline ; yet I leave
the heart o’ me
Where the Auld Brig o’ Balgownie bides
within a faery glade.
Gleaming like a richer jewel on the City’s
gown of jade.
SCOTLAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Flower-Gatherer.
I saw one gathering flowers within a glade,
And as he gathered them the fair things
died ;
And some were frail as Hope thaf dreamed
and sighed;
Others like the white bosom of a maid
Flushed with the warmth of Love, as half
afraid
She yields her all to him; some tender-
eyed
As Youth; and some like Joy that un-
denied
His heart against the heart of Sorrow laid.
At length the gatherer passed, and, the day
done,
The gloaming like a quiet mother came
And took the frightened flowers to her
breast.
Those that had ’scaped the gatherer in the
sun—
Quietly then, without a word of blame.
As one who knew the mystery of Rest.
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
The Fragrance.
Love came to me
When he was young;
No gifts had he
But neatly flung
Two roses. One caught in my hair:
One found my heart, but withered there.
♦ Love came to me
When he was old.
And clad was he
In cloth of gold.
I sought-mv heart and turned the key.
To find it filled with pot-pourri.
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. Gd. per annum you can have The Bulletin
sent regularly to you, every week for a year, to any
ADDRESS IN AUSTRALASIA, post paid.

all Business Communications to be addressed to the
manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or preserved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
•without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. Thh Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for th,e preservation or transmission of MS.

on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 29, 1919.
THE SEAMEN’S STRIKE.
Five years ago the imported serge that the
child’s frock is made of cost 4s. 6d. per yard.
The same quality now costs 18s. 6d., to the
stupendous profit of the capitalist at the
other end or the haughty importer or both.
Being maddened by this and a thousand other
imported steals of the sort —commonly de-
scribed as “the high cost of living”—a large
number of seamen have rushed into a strike
with a view to ruining or starving or ren-
dering miserable a lot of people who never
did them any harm, and who are suffering
quite as badly from the high cost of living
as they are. In fact, many of the victims
are suffering a great deal a orse, for the sea-
men are not an ill-paid crowd, though not
so well paid as the coal-miners. That is
to say, supposing the value of everything
produced and of all the irreplaceable minerals
dug out of the ground in a year is considered
as a wage-fund pure and simple, both these
classes get rather more than their share. If
the people who go on strike against their
Australian neighbors because of the high
price of imported goods were a shade more
superstitious they would probably pray for
cheap clothes just as some folk pray for rain.
If they were another shade more super-
stitious they would burn live children as
sacrifices to Chemosii outside Parliament
House, and beseech the god to turn away
his anger and reduce the general cost of
existence. Being what they are, the tend-
ency to give pain to the nearest or most
defenceless person when things go wrong
leads them to offer up the children as a
hunger sacrifice to Bosh instead of Che-
mosh —the Bosii of the Freetrade papers.
This is something akin to the case of that
officer in London who, being worried by
skin disease, shot his best friend. Of course
the intoxication of power must be taken into
account. The seamen, coal-miners, wharf-
laborers and railway men are all more or
less in a position to hold the country to
ransom when they feel that way. The
shipping companies—such as Australia
possesses —certainly can’t pay much ransom,
for the profits of their business, taking them
in the lump and averaging good times and
bad. are not gorsreous. But they can pass
it on to the community, which they will do
with enthusiasm.
One of the most prominent demands is for
u six-hour day in port, accompanied by im-
proved accommodation. The six- instead of
eight-hour day at sea may be demanded
later, but it is difficult, seeing that ships
can’t shut up at night like offices and ware-
houses and send the employees home. A
six-hour ship with all four shifts on board
would be more like a moving picnic-van than
a sea-carrier. Other claims are £4 a week
for one class of seamen and £3 10s. tdt
another, with, of course, board and lodging
added; better compensation for injured sea-
men and the dependants of those killed
(which is especially commendable in such a
dangerous occupation, though rather in-
definite) ; and 3s. per hour overtime. This
last item is loaded. Ships won’t carry four
shifts, but in port they will require four.
So they will employ the old crowd and work
the old hours, paying Os. per man per day
overtime. Considering liow -hard it is to get
rid of 18 hours of leisure per day this is
plainly what is aimed at. When all these
outlays are met, the cost of living will go
up again by a trifle.
* * * *
The demands, of course, don’t apply to
the oversea ships, all foreign-owned. Such
demands never apply to the men whom the
importers make the big profits for. The
proposed increases, necessitated by the figure
at which imported goods are sold, will help
to spoil any chance Australia has of becom-
ing its own shipowner, but then the steady
purpose of the roaring boys who are allowed
to run the Labor movement a great part of
the time is to destroy Australian industry—
to shut up its factories and its mines by
continuous strikes, yet never to strike against
the people who run up the cost of living by
adding 300 per cent, to the bill for goods
made by niggers and sundries. The row
when meat prices went up 60 per cent, in
five years, largely because the wages of
everybody concerned had gone up to balance
the cost of goods brought from abroad and
partly by reason of increased taxation,
moved the fiscal-sinkers of the Labor party
to a torrent of indignation against the local
butchers and farmers, and made them
almost call for their annihilation. The fact
that foreign serge has gone up from 4s. 6d.
to 18s. 6d. per yard doesn’t rouse them either
to anger or to the slightest interest in the
establishment of local industries. It only
moves them to stop carrying the rations of
the kid who finds her serge frock becoming
scarce. With them the fatted agent of
foreign cheap labor is sacred. Nor is there
any finality about the present demands.
Should the price of Japanese goods go up
some more the people concerned are liable to
renew their operations and institute another
effort to make the local population suffer
whatever misery they can bring about.
* * * •
The upheaval is supposed to have been
decided on at a meeting organised at a time
when, by reason of many vessels being hung
up in wayside ports through bad weather, a
small attendance might be expected. The
Bolsheviks or extremists or “narks” attended
in force, knowing that their attendance was
wanted, and held possession. The proposal
that a ballot should be taken was howled
down, for the loud-mouthed minority which
believes in general destruction has no use
for the ballot. Yet so completely have the
main body of unionists been enslaved by the
noisy minority that the refusal of a ballot
apparently counts for nothing, though if it
had been a matter of capitalists shutting
unionists out of the ballot at a general elec-
tion the row would have been past imagining.
If is a point of honor to humbly follow the
decision arrived at by a snatch vote taken
amid a tumult in a hall of no great size.
The result might, of course, have been the
same if a ballot had been taken, but the re-
fusal of it was an outrage on the rights of an
allegedly free and self-governing body of men.
If Labor had a clear idea of its rights, save
such rights as its bosses nermit it to have,
the men who were mainly responsible for
the refusal would be expelled from the union
at the shortest possible notice, but that would
be too much to expect. Laborites have been
expelled from the whole movement for urg-
ing that this country should put up, even by
compulsory service, . a whole-souled fight
against a pair of Hun despots who threat-
ened to reduce us to slavery ; though union-
ism is itself, of necessity, a compulsory ser-
vice movement, and the pacifist who doesn’t
serve in its ranks is regarded as a leper.
But if Labor has ever expelled a member
for being a notorious boodler or a stealer
of other men’s wives, or a greedy land-shark,
or being a party boss w 7 ho couldn’t see that
the common herd had any right to a ballot,
or a “scab” who scoffed at all the financial
and decentralisation planks of the alleged
platform, the episode has been overlooked.
THE GREAT DESTRUCTIVE
INDUSTRIES.
“In Australia it is a most urgent and necessary
thing that there shall be no further crowding into
the cities, but that, on the contrary, the people
shall spread themselves broadly over the continent
and engage, as far as possible, in primary indus-
tries.”—S. M. Herald (17/5/’l9).
It is time the community got a better
understanding of these primary industries
about w r hich they hear so much from
biased and often ignorant and ill-paid
phrase-mongers on the daily press. Any
cheap, glib man who has a smattering of
books and a habit of repetition and a cer-
tain kifid of job on a paper can lend all the
w 7 eight of that organ to tell the people
what little he knows about how to
run the country. And the w 7 ell-thumbed,
dog-eared saying about “primary industries”
is accessible to everybody and is to be found
in many school-books which mostly have it
all wrong, for school-book writers are too
often cheap hacks. As a matter of fact there
are three kinds of industry, not two, taking
the term industry to mean the making of
things that are intended for sale. (This
leaves out the bulk of the population, which
can’t be helped.) The three are primary,
secondary and destructive industries, and for
a period of Australia’s history the last was
the biggest of the trio. They may be defined
as follows: —
Primary industries include agricultural and pas-
toral enterprise of all sorts.
Secondary industries include everything in the
way of manufacture even in its most primitive stages.
The term covers all manner of improvements on raw
material, from the washing of the dirt out of wool
and the grinding of wheat and the salting of the
outer membrane of the late lamented on to the build-
ing of a 50,000-ton steel steamer with locally-
produced materials.
Destructive industries are the digging up of the
national capital in the way of coal and ores and oil
—-things that can’t be replaced. Also, it includes,
especially in a country that is short of forests, the
hacking down of valuable timber unless new trees are
planted. It doesn’t include fishing in a general way,
for the sea supplies new crops of fish, but it some-
times applies to pearl-shelling. This exhausting of
capital is necessary work, but it isn’t “primary” in
the proper sense. Or if it is the Prodigal Son and
the Jubilee Juggins were primary producers of a
sort.
* » * *
Primary industry is boomed by the shoddy
press as a great and desirable and profitable
thing because the profits of destructive in-
dustry are lumped in with it. At one
gorgeous period of Victoria’s history a hand-
ful of gold-finders, working easy surface
deposits on a mere fragment of the State’s
area, found w’ealth equal to £6 per week
for every family in the State, reckoning a
family at five persons. More recently, in
]9ol, the few gold-miners of Westralia went
one better and dug up irreplaceable assets to
the value of £7O for every inhabitant, and
did this mostly out of a bit of ground the
size of one big farm. They produced
nothing in the proper sense of the term,
but they found a lot of buried trea-
sure —not a pirate’s hoard but the hoard of
Providence. And the mining fraternity
is doing this sort of thing in a smaller way
all the time. Because of happenings like
Bendigo and Ivalgoorlie and a thousand
minor happenings the importing and anti-
manufacturing and anti-Australian press
seeks to cast a glamor over the struggle to
drag six or eight bushels of low-priced wheat
per acre out of some barren, rabbit-haunted
field which is too far out to give the settler
a chance. It is described as primary
industry, and when all are lumped together
the thing makes something of a show.
* * * *
The aim of every nation should be to
supply itself with everything it requires
before it lays itself out to be an exporter.
It is an unattainable ideal, but the com-
munity should get as near it as possible. The
achievement saves the cost of sending heavy
raw material to the ends of the earth and
dragging finished goods back, and paying
away half its substance to foreign middle-
men and profiteers w 7 hose exactions are quite
beyond its control. In Australia’s case
the distances are long, and because we
don’t own the ships the shipowner is an
extra and ravenous profiteer. The tem-
perate zone of Australia has far too many
people engaged in primary industries, or
rather in the principal varieties thereof,
and very many of the people engaged make
a wretched living by trying to export surplus
goods. The Governments are now endeavor-
ing to make the dead things appear alive by
pushing returned soldiers into the worst of
the primary business with loans of £5OO or
£7OO or £lOOO each, supplied out of money
which the Governments have themselves-
borrowed. Australia has far too few
secondary industries—the kind that pay
union wages and don’t ask the State for
loans of £5OO or £lOOO per worker. This
is evidenced by the fact that it buys almost
everything except the crudest objects abroad,
and it is the people abroad who have lately
put up their charges 300 per cent, or so, and
made living almost impossible, and have
often failed to deliver the goods at any price.
The country is miserably short of real cities.
Four or five of them to a continent nearly the
size of Europe is more like barbarism than
civilisation. At the same time it is shame-
fully over-citied. When 5,000,000 people
with 3,000,000 square miles to rove over put
nearly one-third of themselves into two
capitals down in one almost imperceptible
corner of their inheritance congestion is
overdone. There is no very great immedi-
ate need in temperate Australia for people
to spread more over the face of the country,
though there is in tropical Australia, where
the cotton industry has hardly even begun.
But the need to spread more towns over
the face of the country is obvious.
BOLSHEVIKS AND SOME STRANGE
IDEALS.
The most ueadable article in the Round
Table for March deals with “Bolshevik
Aims and Bolshevik Ideals.” The Bol-
sheviks really started with high ideals,
as did that crazed crowd whom William
Lane, the apostle, led from Australia
to Paraguay in 1893, but the ideals went
wrong. New Australia in Paraguay was
the great development, made under most
brilliant conditions, of the policy which some
professed Labor leaders are preaching to-day
as if it was something new 7 —the elimination
of the landlord, and the capitalist, and the
employer even if he is a very small, poor
employer, and even the replacement of State
enterprise by enterprise controlled by the
general tumult of the employees. It failed
miserably and the experimenters w r ent homo
to the old order of things. If the idea had
been practised generally in Australia, instead
of in a small way in a far land, the hungry,
disgruntled mob would have had no home to
flee to and would have had to battle through
with blood and tears as the Bolsheviks in
Russia are doing. The Russian people’s
failure isn’t finished yet, simply because they
haven’t been all killed, or all starved to
death, or been conquered by an outside force.
It is the difference between a prodigal son
with a charitable old man to return to and
a prodigal without.
* * * *
It is because of the awful failure in Para-
guay that a great many people shy away
from the One Big Union party which wants
to try the same device in all Australia with
none" of the Paraguayan experiment’s splen-
did advantages. It is even suggested that
the new men who preach the old men's
gospel should buy up or create a few indus-
tries and run them successfully as a sample
before they ask the community to go the
whole hog—the one whose dinner the youth
in the New Testament was reduced to steal-
ing.
The Russian revolutionists had grievances
enough. Among them were a loony
sovereign; a nobility of great hereditary
landowners like those who burden the British
Isles but more powerful and arrogant; a
grievous lack of education; a corrupt police
force ; a Parliament that was only a shadow ;
and an expensive war. Also they had a
bitter climate and bad liquor. The war
had been forced on them, and Russia didn’t
suffer through it the same ravages as France
and Belgium and Serbia, but the people were
too unlettered to know. When the Bol-
sheviks made a peace that was more disas-
trous than war there was a brief period of
something that looked like the ghost of pros-
perity. The paper money was abundant
and it was good for a week or two till it
became so abundant that it wasn’t good—in
fact, it wasn’t worth its weight in bread.
And it seemed that the taking over of indus-
tries by disorderly committees of workers,
who spent most of their time on stop-work
meetings, though it reduced the output in
many cases to a quarter or less of the usual
average, didn’t create famine. That was so
long as the stocks on hand lasted. So the
humblest Bolshevik —say the scavenger in
Petrograd—whose wages were raised to £l2
per week in paper got something like the
value of £l2—for a week or two. When
the coin disappeared, and the old stocks of
provender and clobber were exhausted, and
the new season’s goods didn’t come in at
anything like the old rate because of
stop-work meetings and incapacity, and £l2
of paper became worth something very
humble in real cash, the twelve-pounder
found that he was a poorer scavenger than
ever. It was necessary either to admit that
Paradise was a failure or to preach that the
capitalists were still plundering the people
and had to be suppressed. So the level at
which a man became a ravenous capitalist
was lowered by common consent as if it
was an income-tax exemption. At the be-
ginning the man with £5OOO worth of goods
might, if he chose, be accepted as one of the
crowd which chased and executed the man
with £500,000. But a time came when, the
supply of £500.000 men being quite used up,
the person with £2OO worth of goods might
be chased and executed himself.
* * * *
Then began the trouble between city and
country. The little cocky farmer, working
single-handed, couldn’t hold stop-work meet-
ings of himself, and in most cases he still
produced grain. He was suspected of
hoarding it and not yearning after paper
money. Anyhow, it was difficult to get
rid of it, as the railways had collapsed
through too many stop-work meetings. The
cocky was partially disqualified as a voter
from the first so as to keep the balance of
power in the cities —that is to say, he had
one member for 125,000 voters, while the
townspeople had one for 25,000 voters. All
employers, ana all who weren’t revolutionary
soldiers or sailors, or manual or domestic
workers were disqualified, which shut out
doctors, writers and the intellectual class
generally. The voter’s age was fixed at 18
(as proposed by Bolsheviks in Australia).
This voting arrangement was necessary to
keep the Lenin-Trotsky Government afloat,
for its power lay in the stop-workers of the
cities, but it led to divergence of opinion be-
tween town and country. By way of a
diversion, Poverty Committees were formed
of the cockies who hadn’t troubled to raise
a harvest, or had spent the proceeds, or had
had bad luck. Their job was to make war
on and collect contributions from the cockies
who had raised a harvest and were supposed
to have food on hand. The presumption
was that the poorer cockies would support
the Government and the richer cockies woe
be disqualified or dead. Doubts are enter-
tained about the next harvest; still the
schools teach the rising generation to have
hope for a bright future. Schools are far
more numerous than under the old regime,
but they teach little save Bolshevik prin-
ciples, just as many Mohammedan schools
teach only the Koran. The conscript army,
composed partly of Chinese, is said to be
faithful, and the hangman—-or rather the
hang-multitude—works overtime, for capital
punishment and torture have both been re-
stored.
* * # *
New Australia in Paraguay began differ-
ently and went home to mother before the
logical end came. The Queenslanders who
founded it had no hereditary nobility or
disastrous war to complain of. They had
still land available for settlement. Also they
possessed a real Parliament with a fairly,
though not excessively, liberal franchise.
But a polished imported crank or prophet
or impostor, one Lane from England,
preached a general exodus of the working
classes so that the rich might wash the
dishes themselves. He got followers. The
Paraguayan republic, which had wanted men
ever since it lost nine-tenths of its male adults
in the great war, gave a grant of rich land,
with rights of local self-government and
exemption from taxes and military service.
So the settlers started without landlords or
capitalists or employers or peers or any sort
of tyrants. They were carefully selected
folk, so they left the criminal element behind
them. They left their share of the public
debt behind them. And they all had money.
* * * *
The Rue Australian starved in the midst
of plenty. They enjoyed Liberty and
nothing else. When, recently, the Bol-
shevik day-wage men in State employ at
Cockatoo (Sydney) carried their protest
against the capitalist (themselves) so far
that thev only drove 73 rivets per day, the
nigger-driver (appointed bv the Government
made by themselves) bade them leave the job
and go to Hades or somewhere, or else
accept piece-work. Driven b’ - the fear of
hunger they accepted, and earned almost
double wages without any extraordinary
exertion. Consequently Freedom shrieked


a second time. The Rue Australians got
down, metaphorically, to driving one rivet
a week in their pursuit of Liberty, there
being no nigger-driver. The elected gang-
foremen were deposed for infringing the
rights of free men almost every time they
gave an order, and as it took a large public
meeting to depose one of them, it was a
long time between orders. The settlers sold
their cattle, and still grew poorer. They sank
so low that some English agricultural serfs
who came out to Paradise were shocked at the
tattered state of the angels who wouldn’t
work —or at least wouldn’t work except on
0.8. U. lines—lest they should again fall
under the yoke. They even cadged further
aid from the republic (population mostly
brown) which had already done so much for
them. And in the end they mostly got back
to Australia somehow.
Two tilings saved New Australia from tue
chance of becoming a slaughter-house like
Bolshevik Russia, but on a smaller scale.
First, the adventurers had a home to go to.
Secondly, the bayonets of the republic’s army
were available to prevent too violent dis-
agreement. The same experiment, spread
all over Australia, would have neither of
these advantages. It might not have even
so bright a leader as William Lane, who
was an upside down sort of genius and might
have been Lenin with larger opportunities.
THE VETERAN PASSES.
The Defence question, in most of its aspects,
has always been an embarrassment to the
politicians. It is more so now than ever.
President Wilson’s heavenly view regard-
ing a Millennial future in which there shall
be no more wars has strengthened the case
of the pacifists. There is a natural ten-
dency, now that the world is lowering its
guard, after nearly five years’ bloodshed,
towards laxness in Defence matters. And
yet Australia never had more need to be on
the alert. Its alien restriction policy is
going to keep the colored brother in a
chronic state of more or less suppressed fury ;
and if the tariff question is handled as it
should be the volume of foreign annoyance
will be considerably augmented. The poli-
ticians are striving furiously to face both
ways. To the local Wilsonians they explain
that the Army is a bagatelle; to Nationalists
that it is, and is going to be in the future,
an effective force. Neither statement is
correct.
* * * *
The Reserve, for (say) five years, at the
most, may be accepted as a serviceable arm.
It will consist of genuine soldiers expert in
every form of warfare known up to the end
of 1918. But one of the features, possibly
the feature, of the next war will be bombing
-—bombing, not only in the line and back
areas, but hundreds of miles to the rear.
The A.F.C. Reserve, on a four days’ training
basis, will have no hope of keeping abreast
of the times in aerial warfare. The 18-pr.
gun may become out of date any day now.
A new split trail has been devised, and a
recoil system, on the lines of that in use in
the French .75 gun, is being introduced. As
with the F.C. and Artillery, so with the
other arms. They must quickly become out-
moded. The Bulletin is entirely in favor
of the Reserve, ,per se; but it cannot remain
the first line of defence as it is at present.
To get the maximum of efficiency its per-
sonnel should be freely amalgamated with
the C.M.F. But that is exactly what the
Government and politicians generally don’t
intend to bring to pass unless they are forced
into it. The natural aim of the politician
is to soothe all hands and so keep his job.
The Army Reserve scheme is, amongst other
things, an expression of that tendency. One
of the ideas at the back of it is to offer a
sop to the expeditionary Cerberus who
shows an awkward anxiety to apply the
knowledge of the art of war which he has
gained at such tremendous expense, abroad,
to the service of his country. No patriot
member of the A.I.F. is able to claim that he
is altogether denied the right to serve. The
authorities can, and do, point proudly to
the Reserve, saying: “Your duty lies there.”
It is not enough for a keen young soldier.
This valuable type of citizen w r ants to keep
up to date in the profession which the war
has enabled him to add to whatever calling
he had before it, by work with the C.M.F. as
well. But that is exactly what he is de-
barred from doing, unless he was a trainee
before he joined the A.I.F.
* * * *
The absurdity of the system is most clearly
seen in the case of A.I.F. officers and n.c.o.’s.
Ninety per cent, of them have risen, by dis-
tinguished conduct in the field —that is to
say, by brains and character—and in the
face of keen competition. They are the pick
of an army which was the admiration of the
world. At the present time they are being
hurried into civil life by the hundred. There
is room for them neither in the permanent
forces nor in the Militia. It means that such
born leaders and soldiers as Colonel Murray,
Y.C., are blanketed, while men of the Per-
manent army who saw no service in this, or
any, war are kept on. And it further means
that the training of the Commonwealth’s
future first-line army is to be in the hands,
mostly, of officers who not only lack the
requisite knowledge but who, rightly or
wrongly, will be regarded by the boys under
them as “cold-footers.” It is true that a
large number of Militia officers went to the
war; but a large number, also, stayed at
home. Nominally they were “indispensable,”
or something similar; in practice most people
know that the inquiries instituted by the
State Commandants into their cases, half-
way through the war, were farcical. The
war wdiich is in its death-throes w r as almost
as easy to stay away from, with official
honor, as it was to get to. For every adult
trainee below commissioned rank who en-
listed at least one chose the safer way. This
means—unless the existing system goes
that the bulk of C.M.F. officers, a year or
so hence, will consist of young men who
heard the call and failed to respond to it
with the inevitable effect that, however sound
their reasons were, they must lose in author-
ity as well as in knowledge of the job. The
authorities in Melbourne recognise the
anomaly of the position—none better. They
know that the Commonwealth’s citizen army
would be immensely strengthened and
vitalised by the incorporation in it of all
those recommended officers and n.c.o.’s of
the Reserve who were willing to join. But
the change would necessitate an amendment
of the Defence Act, which at present lays
down that promotion in the C.M.F. can only
be got from the ranks of trainees. The Gov-
ernfnent doesn’t intend to amend the Act—-
if it can possibly help it. There is too
much sectional hostility involved. The Anti-
Conscription crowd would call it Militarism.
Another lot would see in it “a betrayal of
the cause of democracy.” The very con-
siderable portion of the C.M.F. which failed
to enlist would strongly object, as w’ould
their relatives, to any arrangement which
gave military preference to ex-members of
the A.I.F. All these sections represent
votes which neither political party is pre-
pared to lightly alienate. There are no
votes worth talking about in doing the right
and reasonable tiling—most of the electors
being too war-weary to interest themselves
in the question except insofar as it immedi-
ately affects them or theirs. Yet the basis
of an efficient army is skilled training, and,
as aforesaid, the cream of Australia’s best
for this purpose is being hastily pushed into
private life. Not till fresh trouble comes,
as it inevitably must, will the nation«appre-
ciute the insanity of the wastage which is
going on now.
PLAIN ENGLISH,
The Significance of Shangtung.
“The Pariah’’ : The question of Kiaochau
and Shangtung has been settled in favor of
the Japanese—that is, the Japanese inherit
all those rights and privileges that the Ger-
mans held in this rich province prior to the
outbreak of the war. That these were im-
portant is shown by the fact that the Big
Four only hurried the matter to a close after
twice adjourning. The Japanese made no
secret of their intention to keep their grip
whatever the Conference said, and the
Chinese said that they would be utterly sold
and disgraced if Japan were allowed to
usurp German concessions. To get at the
bottom of the question it is necessary to go
back to the time when there was a keen
interest in the exploitation of China, a time
when Peking was full of concession-seekers
of every nation, tricking, bribing and using
in every way the big money that wns at
their backs. Then, to make sure of a point
of support for her trade, Russia took over
Port Arthur, and Britain pursued the same
course at Wei-hai-wei. The French were too
busy in the south of China to trouble about
these matters in the north. Only one big
European nation was not represented in
China —Germany. Though late she was de-
termined not to be the least. In 1896 the
Chancellor made a vague statement that
might have meant much or nothing, and the
world was surprised when in the following-
year the Huns made the murder of two
missionaries an excuse for a daring grab.
At first it was said that Kiaochau was merely
a rallying point for German trade, but the
boastful Hun very soon made it appear that
he was playing- a deeper game. In August
1898, Lieut.-Col. Riieiniiold Wagner pre-
pared a memorandum on the harbors and
defences of China. Said he
No portion of the Chinese Empire can be so
easily acquired by an enemy as the Shangtung-
Peninsula. With Kiaochau undefended, nothing
would be easier than to land an army there and
take up a defensive position across the peninsula
....and thus sever it from the interior.... If Kiao-
chau were well fortified not only is the most exposed
position of the Shangtung Peninsula protected, but an
enemy could be easily expelled.... Kiaochau is in
such a position as to render Port Arthur and Wei-
hai-wei of no military value.
The American and British view was summed
up by two writers in the same words. “Ger-
many may therefore be said to have acquired
in Kiaochau the key to Northern China,” and
Krausse says that whoever holds Kiaochau
“possesses a controlling voice in all questions
affecting the fate of Peking.” It is easy to
see why China does not desire to have any
power holding such a vital strongpost at
her most vulnerable point. To the average
man, however, there is some mystification in
the antagonism shown towards Japan by
China. The reasons I set out fully in The
Bulletin of November 13, 1913 (in which,
by the way, I pointed the coming fate of
the Manchurian provinces). For 16 cen-
turies the literature of the countries shows
that a close friendship existed, only being-
broken when the Japanese began to western-
ise themselves. Then followed the Chino-
Jap war, with its conseouent Shiminoseki
treaty, then the very unpleasant Korean
business (a sort of lluns-in-Belgium affair
with special Oriental trimmings), some nasty
unpleasantnesses at the time of the Boxer out-
break, and several minor troubles that made
the Chinese people hate the Japanese. China,
just beginning to settle down after the revo-
lution that started back in 1911, fears to
have her strong neighbor firmly fixed near
her heart, and the Japanese are afraid of
what will happen to them if the Chinese
giant becomes strong, and is not disposed
to be friendly. There is no doubt that Japan
has tried her best to gain the good graces
cf China, but she has gone about it in a
manner that carries no recommendation.
One of the clauses of the treaty of 1896 pro-
vided for a mutual educational scheme. Stu-
dents from Japan were to spend some years
in China studying the language and philo-
sophy, while the same number of Chinese
students were to be in Japan. This “To a
Do-bun Kwai” was beautiful in theory, but
in the event all the advantages went to
Japan. She decided where the students of
each country should be trained, with the
result that her men went into all the pro-
vinces of China while the Chinese students
were all huddled in one place, and jealous
eyes saw that they did not learn too much
that was of vital importance. The result is
charminglv told by the Japanese publicist,
Dr. Kan-Ichi Asak'awa : “Japanese stu-
dents compiled monumental works on the
politics, geography and economics of China,
which, in their thoroughness and accuracy
are unrivalled in any language.” At the
moment of writing the latest cables say that
China threatens a boycott of Japanese goods.
This will be the third boycott undertaken by
the Chinese. In 1906 they tuned up the
Americans, and had Australia been awake
we should have collared a lot of trade. In
1908 they declared against goods from Nip-
pon, and the loss of trade to Japan was set
down by an official at over five million
pounds in a few months. The sight of
astute Chinese merchants piling up thou-
sands of pounds worth of Jap goods on the
bund at Canton and then having them burnt
was something to remember. Her effort on
this occasion will be none the less effective,
because China is now more united than she
was then. That is the position. What it
will be four years hence no one can say, but
if President Wilson lives long enough he
will see new wisdom coming out of the East.
A Cry from Abroad.
No. 1008 Private P. It. Lynch wrote to this
paper, from London, last mail: —
A soldier attempting to “bopst” his native coun-
try, Australia, comes a “crash” when newspapers,
having a great circulation throughout Great Britain
and Ireland, contain news items of which the cut-
tings enclosed are samples. The question arises,
Are the statements correct, or are they portions
of the propaganda of Bolshevism?
The statements were that profiteering
existed in Perth, and land-profiteering in
N. S. Wales, the respective authorities being
John Bull and London Daily Mail. The
Daily Mail quoted Holman as saying:—
It should be realised in England that, owing to
the vast land monopoly, Australia is unable to
place her own soldiers favorably, and that conse-
quently great discontent already exists.
That is so. So far as N. S. Wales is con-
cerned there is not a tenth of the land avail-
able which is necessary to accommodate the
A.1.F., let alone casual immigrants. A
reason, this, why Holman stated the other
day that the only immigrants he had any
use for were domestic servants. As regards
profiteering proper—meaning in goods and
chattels—it is rampant everywhere from
Perth to Cairns. Australia, in .point of
fact, is passing through a phase which has
been chronic in England, off and on, for
generations. With more land available per
head of the population than any white
country on earth it is suffering from land-
hunger. One of the most productive of
countries, it is paying through the nose for
almost every necessitv of life. The position,
in many parts of the Commonwealth, re-
sembles that of England in the forties of
last century. The Chartists agitated, nomi-
nally, for things like manhood suffrage and
payment of members. What they really
looked for from the Reform Bill was beer
at Id. per quart, as in the brave days of
old, and meat at fd. a pound. Labor, in
Australia, is chasing many ideals; but the
master one is, as always, everywhere, cheap
food and drink. A Sydney dinner-ale
which, shortly before the war, was 6d., is
now Is. in many hotels; fillet of steak, which
used to be Is a pound, or under, is now
Is. 6d. a pound, or over. Profiteering in
its real sense—that defined by the Welsh
Commissioners as “patent gambling in the
necessities of life” —has been checked in
England, even if it hasn’t been scotched.
Here it flourishes unhampered Never have
the retail traders done so well. To fly the
Jolly Roger over their shop-fronts would
perhaps look offensively flash; biff they get
there just the same. Practically every
article of food and clothing is from 75 to
150 per cent, dearer than it was five years
ago. People who would not have believed,
a few years back, that they cojuld cut things
any finer have found themselves accomplish-
ing marvels in the way of self-denial. In
short, Australia, the bountiful, which has
known no war, is everywhere in much the
same situation in regard to household sup-
plies as the United Kingdom was in after
two years of war. The present disa-
bilities which the Commonwealth is suffer-
ing from, owing, at least in part, to its
land-and-goods-profiteers, are restricted im-
migration and bloated cities. Unless the
situation improves the factors at the back
of those disabilities may breed an upheaval
in which the most unenviable man in the
community will be a profiteer.

Water Power.
A scheme is in the air, and also occasionally
in the daily papers, for damming the Clar-
ence River, in northern N.S.W. It is reck-
oned that the Gorge is such a favorable spot
that something 2ft times the size of Burrin-
juck can be produced at half the cost. The
idea isn’t so much to irrigate land and create
another settlement like Yanco, where the
State makes a big loss every year by per-
suading people to grow foodstuffs for the
One Big City that is too far away to allow
of great success, but rather to furnish
power. The possible supply of power is
almost unlimited. It can be used to work
railways with the coal bill eliminated—in
other words, to work them at much less than
present cost. At the same time, electricity
makes more difficult grades workable, and
thus payable lines can be built in mountain
regions where they wouldn’t pay if they had
to be flattened out to suit the locomotive
which carries its power along with it
like ballast. There is a vast reserve of
energy in the cables, which can be switched
on when the train starts to climb and
switched off again when the level is reached.
Therefore the idea opens a great prospect for
railways within the profitable carrying radius
of electric power which is at present sup-
posed to be about 200 or 250 miles from the
centre of supply, and if the State allows
lines to be built connecting the northern
tableland with any other ports than Sydney
an opportunity seems to be in sight. It is
also alleged that, with electric power on. the
spot, some of the local mines could be worked
at about half the present cost, for costs, when
fuel has to be fetched long distances, are
heavy. The supply of cheap force—far
cheaper than Sydney possesses and much
cheaper still than that of Melbourne—for
manufacturing purposes is also in the pro-
gramme, but the importing interest and its
friends of the Freetrade press will probably
squash manufactures and tell people to “go
on the land” and grow wheat. Anyhow, it
is contrary to the congestion policy to have
any manufactures in N.S.W. except in
Sydney. Also towns might be lighted at
very small expense if there are enough indus-
tries to make towns that are worth lighting.
It is reckoned that power and illumination
could be sold as far afield as Brisbane. The
idea isn’t new or experimental. Almost
everything in the north of England is now
run by electric force supplied from central
stations, though as the electricity is gen-
erated from coal it isn’t the inexpensive kind
that can be derived from water. And the
great elqptric-power business which is based
on its abundance of water is becoming one
of Tasmania’s greatest asets. The outstand-
ing feature of industry that relies on water
instead of coal is that it isn’t an eating-up
of the nation’s capital or a process of living
for the moment on assets that can never be
replaced. The fluid is harnessed in passing.
It is passing in any case for its own amuse-
ment, and it is made to work as it goes along.
After it has done its work il is available for
drinking, washing, irrigation, scenery or
navigation, just as it was before. Still, even
if the New England scheme is all that it is
cracked up to be or more, it isn’t likely to
be encouraged in any whole-hearted way.
Sydney is a coal-power city situated in the
centre of Australia’s greatest coalfield, anv
place with cheaper force at command may
expect to receive just enough encouragement
to make it a pale shadow of Sydney—not a
rival of Sydney. Its only hope is to secede
from the Ma capital as Brisbane and Mel-
bourne did. It was its possibilities in the way
of cheap water-power and prosperity and self-
assertion that were the ruin of the proposed
Federal capital at Dalgety. The old jealous,
grumpy push of importers’ tame politicians
never rested till they got, in its place, the
wonderful Metropolis in Two Pieces at Can-
berra, where coal, iron, water-power or other
advantages are not. Unfortunately this
isn't a very great time for big new enter-
prises. There will be unions to be con-
sulted at interminable length as in the ship-
building business, for when anything big and
fresh is proposed the old rules somehow
don’t apply, and protocols, negotiations,
treaties, ambuscades, conferences and tactical
operations are certain to be numerous.
There will be a special court to adjust dif-
ferences,. and a royal commission to search
for possible deep conspiracies on the part of
the billionaires who lurk behind every bush.
The question whether power should be sup-
plied for new industries unless they are all
State-owned will be argued at length. Th<>
day-work v. piecework question will be fought
out again. The necessary plant will be
imported at the foreign profiteer’s price.
The money will be borrowed at no end of a
price. Perhaps it would be good business
for the North to separate first and have a
little Government of its own to run the show
on the spot. The present affair is nearly
400 miles from the spot, and can only make
spasmodic visits by special train and blow
off speeches,
A Citizen as Well as a Soldier.
The salutary principle was established, last
week, in Sydney that soldiers in uniform
retain their civil rights. In the case of
Robert Kennedy, the G.M.P. who has just
been given a well-earned six months, the
military authorities fought desperately
for the privilege of walloping' the expedi-
tionary. That, in plain language, was the
issue involved. The original contention was
not that Kennedy was innocent of assault-
ing the invalid Anzac Williams, but that
he had a right to do so. Or, anyhow, that
Williams, being a soldier, had no civil re-
dress in the matter. The Crown, or the
Defence Department, or Victoria Barracks,
or whoever was the instigating party, went
to remarkable lengths to get the desired deci-
sion on this point. Major Bowie Wilson
admitted on oath that he, as O.C. A.I.F
troops for the metropolitan district, warned
Williams that if he proceeded against
Kennedy in the civil courts he would have
the resources of the Defence Department
against him, or words to that effect.
Williams’s version was that he had been
threatened that the case would be taken, if
necessary, to the Privy Council. The Judge,
in passing sentence, remarked that “to fight
it out as he [Kennedy], had done by false
evidence” only made the crime worse. The
defending counsel asked Williams if he had
not himself been convicted before a court-
martial. Being a lawyer, he knew well that
if it had been so it was irrelevant. It is
expressly provided, by Statute, that the Civil
Courts shall take no cognisance of court-
martial proceedings. An accused person con-
victed before a court-martial is still liable to
be tried by the civil courts; and an acquittal
in that low-comedy jurisdiction is equally
unrecogmsed by them. This unusual vigor
may, of course, have been in the ordinary
way of business; but in all the circum-
stances, it looks as if at least some of it was
inspired by the military authorities. It is
the more likely since the decision deprives
them, in one act, of a number of powers
which they have been using, and in many
cases abusing, ever since the War Precau-
tions Act came into force. One logical and
highly desirable outcome of it should be the
abolition of triql by court-martial for all
but members of the Permanent Forces.

lated to fill every patriotic Australian
with rage is these Canadian statistics.
Our contemporary is supposed to have
8,000,000 people to Australia’s 5,000.0‘>(>
Yet its biggest city isn’t much more than
half the size of Sydney, nor its second-
biggest much more than half as large as
Melbourne. It has a Federal capital like
Australia, but its Canberra is situated on a
big river and had 87,000 people when last
heard of, while Canberra and 000 square
miles of territory have 2422. Canada had
a Government note issue long before Aus-
tralia did, and it federated 33 years before
Australia did. But, above all, and the chief
cause of its activities, it began to have an

avowedly Protectionist tariff as far back as
1879, while Australia, in 1919, is still think-
ing over the matter. Our reptile contem-
porary’s tariff grew and grew as years went
on till it became a Sheol of a tariff. The
Dominion resolved that the “primary indus-
tries”—which N.S.W., a year or two ago,
discovered to mean getting 12s. worth of
wheat as the result of a year’s toil on an
acre of land —were not so very glorious after
all, so it resolved to be a manufacturing
country as well. It believed in making
shins, locomotives, engines of all sorts, agri-
cultural implements, clothes and sundries.
It even wanted to have manufactured goods
to export. When the war came it was able
to seize the great opportunity while low-
tariff Australia was so paralysed that it
couldn’t even keep the mice out of its unsold
wheat. In 1913 the exports of the two
countries were about equal (£78,000,000),
with the difference that about £15,000,0
of Canada’s exports were manufactured
goods, while Australia was the drudge that
furnished low-priced raw materials. In
1917, when Canada was even selling vast
quantities of ammunition to the Allies, its
exports were £315,000,000; Australia’s were
£97,000,000. In Canada’s little lot was
included £137,000,000 worth of manufac-
tured goods. Australia’s proportion was
more like a million. In 1918, when the
demand for war material was fallinsr on:,
Canada sold only £255,000,000 worth of
goods abroad, of which £113.000,000 were
the manufactured sort. Australia sold
£75,000,000 worth of raw materials. The
Shoddy press is never weary of stating
that trade is a great thing, and it is
when it consists largely of selling high-
priced finished stuff to somebody or other.
When it consists in buying high-priced
finished goods and selling wheaj: in return
at a rate which gives the toiler about £2 a
week net for the cultivation single-handed
of 100 acres, it isn’t quite the same thing.
The difference shows, for one thing, in the
amount of money the peoDle have in their
banks. The deposits in Australian banks,
apart from savings banks, at the end of the
war were about £240,000,000,. largely Gov-
ernment loan money. Those of Canada
were £530,000,000. They piled up wonder-
fully during the days when the Dominion
was rising to the occasion and selling expen-
sive munitions to the Allies. Australia’s
really notable products in the war-time were
the big strike of 1917: the Perth Labor Con-
ference ; the shipbuilding programme which
hasn’t finished a single ship; and the inven-
tion of new loan works to keep the people
employed. Australia has never acquired
the manufacturing instinct. No country
can which tries to be that weird anomaly,
a high-wage, short-hour land living on cheap
labor goods, also a white man’s land fed
largely with nigger produce. Our pros-
perous rival goes on the safe and simple rule
»f doing nothing that the Argus and Herald
and Telegraph say it should. If these
foolish, unpatriotic rags said anything more
't probably wouldn’t do that either —Yours,
etc., Henry Horsecollar.
“0.K.”: I propose an inquiry into the
cause of the persistent rise in the cost of
smoked fish, caught in Australasian waters,
cured by Australasian smokers, and sold,
wholesale and retail, by swarthy aliens, as a
rule. The price of M.L. smoked cod in Mel-
bourne shops last week was Is. Bd. a lb., and
the price of Australian smoked fish —Murray
cod, etc. —had risen in sympathy. When
the population of Australia was 75 per cent,
of the present figures the cost of cod never
exceeded a shilling. Lack of shipping facili-
ties was pleaded in the first place for each
rise in the price of sea-food from Maoriland ;
but. in the light of the present day, it seems
that the public is beset by a conspiracy of
profiteering. We carelessly paid more, and
now the fish-traders —“ring” men and com-
bined retailers—are charging us more still.
Meat-prices are declared to be governed by
the supply of live-stock due to scarcity (or
profusionV of pasturage. But feed for fish
is ever abundant, and the produce of the
mighty deep could be doubled next week if
the policy of the dealers were not restrictive.
It seems to me that the need for placing
thousands of returned soldiers on the land is
not greater than the need for embarking
hundreds on the sea in fishing-smacks.
The Allies ought to establish a department
for the inculcation of a sense of humor into
German mentality. It could only be
done by patient selection of Hun boasts,
brags, threats, announcements and Kaiserian
utterances during the war period, to be
issued in pamphlet form and scattered broad-
cast throughout the land of the unchastened
animal. The pamphlet would need to con-
trast all the early boasts with the later
whines and quote the latest German official
apologies re the sinking of the Lusitania in
the light of the fact that a Hun medal was
struck and a Hun holiday provided for school
kiddies, in celebration of the horror; it
should call attention to the dates of the
“public rejoicings” ordered by the Kaiser,
and set out the famous Hymn of Hate. It
would be a notable production.

“Broker” : The call for Australian wines in
England at the present time is unprece-
dented. For three months past Burgoyne
Ltd., a firm that controls most of the busi-
ness. has monotonously continued to inform
the trade that “the first post-war shipment
has now left Australia, and prospects of fur-
ther shipping facilities are favorable. Mean-
time we have no alternative but to ration
stocks.” One shipment only reached Eng-
land in 191 S (in January), and throughout
the year, with a growing demand, stocks
were never greater than one-fourth of nor-
mal. The average imports of three years
prior to the war period exceeded 750,000
gallons per annum, and figures for 1916-1918
are informing:—

goes, the end of the war was timely. Another
three months of the slaughter in Europe
would have closed the doors on an industry
which has taken 50 years to develop. Only

available for British consumption until the

That section of Melbourne’s police which
does its best to squeleh the sly-grog traffic
the other day flapped over another obstacle,
viz., that under the existing licensing law
anyone who isn’t licensed to sell tanglefoot
can trade as much as he likes, provided he
isn’t actually caught doing so and provided
it isn’t drunk on the premises. The police,
it seems, noticed a perennial stream of
slightly sober persons perambulating between
a Lonsdale-street lolly-shop and the lawns
down the middle of the street, where the
interiors of bottles were steadily con-
sumed. The Shop was searched and the
J. Hops found 111 quarts of ale planted.
The case looked lucid enough, but the Bench
upheld a smart lawyer who claimed there
was no case, inasmuch as there is no legal
offence in taking away beer from a shop or
a house —barring a public-house at certain
hours —-neither is it a crime to crook one’s
elbow in the main street. It had to be
nroved that the lush was intended for con-
sumption on the premises, and that hadn’t
been done in this case. So Parliament is
to be invited to further amend the blessed
Act —and when it has done so another smart
gent, one will probably push another hole
through it.
State-school teachers in Victoria, who
have long held aloof from the State Service
Federation, composed as it is of such humble
wage-earners as letter-sorters and the like,
are at last nosing around the bait of high
wages dangled before them by the manage-
ment of the S.S.F. Gordon Carter, gen.
sec. of the Federation, is helping the aloofers
to make up their minds by scattering the
seeds of upliftment at meetings held in coun-
try centres; and a sign of the times is the
fact that the usually sluggish Teachers’
Union is now showing unusual activity in
its various branches, which will presently be
called on to consider the question of affilia-
tion with the bigger body.
The Australian importing habit is now
blocking the settlement of South Aus. sol-
diers on the Murray, where there are other-
wise better prospects than anywhere else in
the State. A number of men who have
passed through the Training Farm, and
others who have qualified by previous farm-
ing experience, are waiting to take up land,
but the Irrigation Department can’t procure
machinery and materials. There is a fur-
ther difficulty about wire-netting. The De-
partment requires 100 miles of it, but it can
only be bought for forward delivery. Even
then the price is £6O a mile (treble the pre-
war rate), and fencing-wire is also from two
to three times what it was before Gallipoli.
And still there are some farmers who don’t
know what we want Protection for.
“Sol M.”: Sydney is a cold and profiteered
place these days. People who want elec-
tricity in bulk from the City Council for
heating purposes must buy a sort of clock
or meter to measure it with, the supply of
bulk lighting being short through lack of
imported machinery. The clock (imported)
isn't supplied by the City Council, but can
be obtained from a private firm. The article
looks as if it might be worth £8 at the out-
side, and the duty isn’t much. There ought
to be a Parliamentary inquiry as to what it
really does cost. It is sold to the public
at about £l7. Exactly who gets all the
difference I don’t know, but I would like to
know. Possibly if local manufacture com-
menced the price would go down. Australia
has all the materials for making electrical
machinery and electricity and clocks and
meters. It lias the coal and iron, and the
men, and the paper money, and the union
leaders to arrange the strike, and the
Arbitration Court to fail to settle the dis-
pute, and the go-slow habit, and the stop-
work meeting, and the loan at 54 per cent.
Also it has men like Gunner Yates, who
hold, whenever anybody in Australia tries to
make anything, that he is a bloated exploiter
who has accumulated £74.000.000 by oppress-
ing the poor. Meanwhile the poor—or at
least the cold —are being oppressed very
much by having to pay £l7 for an imported
ticker and then wait till the cows roost
high till they get it. Who benefits by all
that money?
