“Oueyn”: Memories of the Kyabram
(Vic.) political circus of 20-odd years ago
were revived by the death of Sam Lancaster,
nurseryman and fruit-grower. Carried on
the crest of the wave of emotion (or hys-
teria), Sam was washed into one of the
Rodney seats in the Assembly. Soon after
his arrival he began to worry the House to
do what he claimed members had been sent
there for. Peacock cackled, and in response
to Sam’s pained query as to the cause of the
hilarity, looked benignly on the newchum
and told him he would find Parliament a
very nice club before he had been there six
months. Lancaster thereupon started a party
to reform the clubmen, and met the usual
fate of those who oppose machine politics.
He went out at the next election.
W. J. Speight, who went west recently in
Auckland (M.L.), was the hero of one of
the toughest Parliamentary election fights
ever witnessed in that city, when he captured
the Auckland East seat in 1878 by only
eight votes, defeating one of Auckland’s big
merchants, J. McCosh Clark. Speight ran
on the Grey ticket, and the name of George
Grey was one to conjure with in Auckland
in those days. At the following election
Speight was a candidate for the Thames,
where he had run a paper for some years;
but this time he was beaten by the brilliant
Johnny Sheehan, at one time Native Minis-
ter, by only 17 votes. Speight was a force-
ful Irishman, born in Dublin 75 years ago.
After he went out of politics he became
manager in Auckland for the Government
Life Insurance Department.
The Afghan is a good non-manufacturing
soldier like the Australian. In the days
when men belabored each other with swords
or shot at each other with guns that were
good for 100 yards—in fact when one brave
man was much the same as another —he
buried a whole British army and was a holy
terror on the border. Now science has left
him behind as it has left the Persian and
the Arab and the Tartar, and he is just
what the Australian would be if he had to
depend on such arms and ships as he has
learned to turn out himself. The hook-
nosed folk who are suspected of being the
Lost Tribes of Israel went to war an hour
or two ago, and behold, their mountains
have become only a row of pins and their
Khyber Pass is a back number. The aero-
planes got above them, and they realised
that they were people fighting the air at the
bottom of a well, and having boulders
dropped on their skulls.
By Knibbs’s understudy :
Treasurer Fitzpatrick (N.S.W.) has done the in-
evitable thing. He explains that the complaint
against the tail end or sediment of the wheat of
19X6-17 (the mouse-plague year) is really engineered
by speculators who have sold more wheat certificates
than they can deliver and want to create prejudice
against the wheat in order to get out of the mess.
If they succeed the suffering farmer, it seems, will
lose £1,000,000. These certificates are worth about
6d. now, so the talk of £1,000,000 might imply that
the N. S. Wales farmers (N. S. Wales is the only
State concerned) have still certificates for 40,000,000
bushels on hand. It seems a lot, as the whole crop
was less than 37,000,000 bushels, of which less than
33,000,000 went into the pool and had certificates
issued against it. Even if Fitzpatrick assumes that
speculators have over-sold so badly that they would
have to pay the farmers 9d., it would appear that
the latter- still hold 30,000,000 out of less than
33,000,000 certificates —this after the speculator has
been battening on them for nearly 21 years. Also,
on the ninepenny basis, to make up the £1,000,000
Fitzpatrick talks of, the speculators would have to
buy the lot, which seems to imply that, in an hour
of madness, they sold 33,000,000 bushels of certifi-
cates when there were only 3,000,000 floating about
the market to make delivery with. Speculators must
be a childlike lot, and it is cruelty to let them
out without a nurse.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Mitchell is the third Premier that West-
ralia has had within a month. He is a cheer-
ful and genial character. Like Forrest, he
was born near Bunbury, and after leaving
school he spent some time working on his
father’s farm. Later, he was a junior clerk
in the W.A. Bank’s service, arid became
manager at Northam. He entered the State
Assembly as member for that district in
1905, and not long afterwards became Min-
ister for Lands in the Moore Government.
He held the same portfolio in the succeed-
ing Wilson Ministry, and had charge of the
Railway and Industries Departments in the
second administration formed by Wilson. On
LITTLE WRETCH!
MRS. ARGUS HERALDGRAPH: “Ah ! Ter at
that again, are yer!”'
the defeat of the Scaddan Government, when
the Nationalist party fired Wilson and
appointed Lefroy as its leader, two years ago,
Mitchell got the huff and gave up office.
His next move was to try and throw down
the Lefroy Government. He failed and then
retired into the background, from which he
was induced to emerge a few weeks ago to
become Minister of Lands in the Colebatch
Cabinet.
Federal affairs are quiet. Parliament
closed last December, and isn’t to meet till
July, when it has to get together for a few
days to blew the money. Then it is seri-
ously suggested that it should close again,
and let the tariff and all the other affairs of
the country slide till next year. Three Min-
isters are still away, and their understudies
say they can’t do anything worth mention-
ing till the expensive loafers come home
about the blankth day of Dunno in the year
Godknowswhen. The Acting Prime Minister
is ill and cross, and says he will never speak
to the papers again so long as he lives. A Fed-
eral “publicity officer” or billsticker or back-
scratcher is to be hired at £504 per annum.
The Government has spent £71.000.000 in
nine months—£26,ooo,ooo of its own money
and £45,000,000 borrowed. The expenses of the
Institute of Science and Industry and the
Bureau of Commerce and Industry still grow
bigger, though one of them has no legal
existence except bluff. Meanwhile Industry
consists chiefly of the news that more lines
of business have closed up, though Com-
merce, which is the art and mystery of im-
porting bad matches and women’s hats,
flourishes exceedingly. The Federal police
force, founded in a moment of frenzy when
an egg was thrown at Hughes, costs a bigger
bill this year than last. Furthermore, the
weary Government flatly refuses to inquire
further into the Shaw Wireless stench, about
which some people are very insistent, while
others aren’t.
As announced: —
Acting Prime Minister Watt is very angry with
the newspaper press of the Commonwealth. He has
decided, for the present, not to communicate anv
further Government news from the Prime Minister's
Department to any journal.
Which puts Watt’s Ministry in a nut-
cracker ; Less said, soonest ended.
Holman speaking proudly of the N.S.W.
Arbitration Act: —•
During the 12 months the Act has been in opera-
tion 204 industrial disputes have been settled; 204
grievances have been removed ; 204 running sores on
the body politic have been cured.
Running sores are things that break out on
the •unemployed poor, thus starving sailors
on a raft get them. Well-fed and satisfied
people who are busy earning big wages sit
making ships or engines or tweed clothes
don’t get so many sores. To have 204 of
them on one body in a year suggests that
there is very little skin left.
“Watt-Oh” : What’s the matter with Watt?
His health is not of the best, but it isn’t
that which has irritated him this week.
Some of the morning napers seem to have
riled him by chopping his “copy” about, and
so he says “Nothing for the papers.” What
he means is that there is nothing for the
press until the press gives him the guarantee
that it will not add to, subtract from, re-
vise, misuse or otherwise maltreat his official
utterances, but will print them in the form
in which they are supplied. Pressmen on
the Federal “rounds” in other days had to
stand up to similar outburst on the part of
the other Billy, but Watt is a different sort
of man. Usually he greets the
in hearty fashion ; now he is as vitriolic as
Hughes in his worst mood.

The Peake Government in South Aus.
seems to be riding for a fall. The National-
ist part of the coalition which put it into
power is getting very restive. Proportional
representation has taken the fancy of most
of the Nationalists, and to a deputation
which urged its adoption Peake gave a fight-
ing speech against it. He is a firm believer
in the party system, and puts his view this
way
“Under proportional representation, Lloyd George
would not have received such a majority as he
recently asked for and obtained.... The two
parties to the position to be faced are the Socialists
and their allies, against which are all who are
opposed to them, called by whatever name; there
should be no room for any midway standing.”
Heaven forbid ! Get the country stand on its
political head rather.
Mitchell is the thirteenth Premier of
Westrafia. The number is being added to
rapidly of late. Four of those who have
previously held the Premiership are dead—
Leake, Throssell. Wilson and Forrest —-six
are residing in W. Aus. and two find the Cold
Country agrees with them.
From the gallery:—
Tipped that Harry Morton and lawyer Colquhouri
will be the also-rans for the N. S. Wales Speaker-
ship. The former is said to have retired from the
contest —much as his Show-jumpers used to do on
topping the bar for the third time. P. B. Colqu-
houn is well liked and scholarly, but doesn’t seem
to “get the atmosphere.” As one of the temporary
Chairmen of Committees his low-spoken and judicial
obtuseness compared none too well with the work
of men like Osborne and Hickey from the Opposition
side. Anyhow, the Chairmanship of Committees at
£740 per annum isn't a bad consolation prize.

“Gallery”: The Federal Parliament will
probably reassemble about the beginning of
July, and almost immediately there should
be a disturbance anent the Government’s
refusal to inquire further into the Shaw
Wireless scandal. The subject is bound to
excite the immoderate eloquence of Bill Higgs
and other Laborites, and it is quite possible
that several disgruntled Nationalists _ will
howl their concurrence with the Opposition.
There is a nice wad of money that still re-
mains unexplained, and if only for the honor
of Jensen’s colleagues when the deal was
made, some attempt ought to be made to
trace the cash to its present address. The
current attitude of Watt and Co. suggests
that they almost smell something, and are
afraid that another inquiry might confirm
their noses in an overpowering nmnner.
The Hughes Government’s Institute of
Science and Industry is growing to be quite
a big frill, yet it exists purely on bluff. The
Bill for its creation hasn’t passed yet. If
it doesn’t pass, and it should be fought to
the last ditch as a protest against the in-
sufferable cheek of porfolio-ed persons, some-
one should demand that Ministers be held
personally responsible for whatever outlay
has been incurred, even if a bumbailiff has to
collect on their furniture.
On Friday some N. S. Wales Labor mem-
bers and one Senator from Queensland
gathered at Sydney to demand the Canberra
capital—the Metropolis in Two Pieces —•
immediately. Everybody concerned, it was
said, could be housed in temporary shacks
one-storey high inside nine months. Not one
of those present said that Canberra is the
best possible site in Australia, or even that
it is the tenth-best site in N. S. Wales, or
that it can be called a fairly good site. The
place—which only exists by Act of Parlia-
ment, after all —might be a family misfor-
tune or a defeat or a lost indemnity for all
its friends say to the contrary.
Back in 1853, when Sydney had only
Mayors, not Gaud Mayors—it began to have
Gaud Mayors in 1902 —the Government sus-
pended the municipal constitution for three
years. It dispensed on honorable terms with
all parties concerned and replaced them with
a trio of commissioners or tyrants, or audi-
tors. They might be called overhaulers. The
advantage of a move of this sort, especially
when it is done so suddenly that there isn’t
time to shift even the contents of the cup-
board where the liquor is kept and where a
skeleton might have trespassed, is that, if
there should be a dead body on the premises,
discovery is almost certain. And if there is
no such cadaver, everybody is pleased to
have an assurance to that effect. Unpaid
officials, like aldermen, have to serve for pure
love of their fellow citizens, and it is a small
thing to throw in a clear certificate of
character once in, say, 60 years.
Captain Konig, commander of the notori-
ous “commercial” submarine, U-boat
Deutschland, was recently elected chairman
of the Lower Weser Ger-
man People’s party, the new
name of the old National
Liberal Organisation. No
man should know the
“lower” business better.
The constitution of the
Vic. Farmers’ Union leaves
the tariff to the individual
opinions of members; but
the farmer’s natural ten-
dency towards black-labor
binder-twine and cheap im-
ported crockery has at last
driven the Bendigo branch
to pass a resolution, with-
out objectors, protesting
against any alterations in
(lie tariff until after the
next general elections, “or
until a duly constituted in-
quiry has shown the neces-
sity for such alteration.”
Evidently Hayseed didn’t
notice the establishment of
a Tariff Commission. Some
time ago, abandoning oppo-
sition to the union, the
Argus advised farmers to
join up and run the show in
the “right” direction, and
apparently Hayseed has
done so. Yet it is the
Argus outlook which leaves
the Vic. farmer to-day
mostly a discontented fiddler,
with 4d. to jingle in his
pants’ pockets and a long-
standing bill at the local
store, while his brother in
the U.S.A., who saves thou-
sands of miles of needless
haulage, is affluent.
Westralia’s latest Ministry contains Mit-
chell. Premier, Colonial Treasurer and Min-
ister for Lands and Repatriation, and J.
Scaddan, Colonial Secretary and Minister
for Railways—provided lie can secure the
Albany seat. They still call England
“Home”!
In the way of freedom from guile and base
suspicion the Postal Department takes the
bun. One annoyance, a while ago, arose
when two men, supposed to be postal officials
because they looked as much like that as
anything else, addressed the man in charge
of the railway van at a N.S.W. South Coast
station and demanded a bag which dido
belong there. It was. handed out as casually
as a sovereign might have been handed to
a complete stranger who offered in the dark
to put the giver on a sure thing for the Cup.
A recent story, as told by the postal officials
in court, was more complicated. A youth
said he took a bag of mail to Randwick P.O.
about 10.50 p.m., and locked it up. Then
a voice on the ’phone told his superior officer
something—apparently that he was to send
the bag out into the dead waste and middle
of the night so that it might be given to
somebody whom he hadn’t seen. By instruc-
tions from the voice he engaged a motor-car
at the Department’s 'expense, and in it a
youth and the bag, which had been- roused
from its slumbers, drove to a tram-shed,
where the bag was handed to a figure that
materialised out of the gloom. The figure
said it would pay for the car later on, and
was last seen walking with Webster’s pro-
perty towards Centennial Park. That was
the simple narrative which the prosecution
told against itself, and if it has got hold of
the real bag-taker it has captured a person
of infinite jest.
“Ess” : The first thing that was inscribed
on our local soldiers’ memorial was the
name of Aid. Wrangle, the Mayor, who
wasn’t within thousands of miles of the war
front. The names of the fallen will be in-
scribed on the side and back facets; but the
little mayor’s name is on the very front, where
nobody can miss it. I suppose little Bumbles
are pasting their advts. on the Digger’s coffin
all over the country. Surely a Govern-
ment that won’t allow a struggling suburban
cobbler to advertise himself as a Returned
Digger without the permission of the De-
fence Department could say something about
this greater scandal.
'OME AGAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I'm 'ome again—gassed, busted, gannny-legged.
I've done me bit, as 1 was urged to do,
F’r King an’ country. ’Ow they cried an’ begged
F’r me t’ go, an’ nobly “see it through” !
1 saw it through, orrite —an’ it was Hell!
I’m ’ome again. Now, what about it? Well?
The day 1 lef’ they smothered me with iiow'rs;
They cheered, an’ yelled, an’ ’owled till all was
blue;
Their “strong posts” sang, an’ waved their flags i r
hours.
One fat ole bloke ses: “Hoy, I envy you!’
But now I’m ’ome, there ain’t no cheerin’ mobs
T’ wclo’ be crippled blokes ’o’s lost their jobs.
There ain’t no flow’rs, confetti, wavin’ flags—
There ain’t no bonser tarts t’ kiss their 'ands
An’ sling us choc’lates, san’wiehes an’ fags;
There ain’t no choruses an’ brayin’ bauds.
The loud-mouthed patriots cursed the slackers, then;
But ’oo’s the slackers, now we’re ’oine again?
“Death to the Kaiser!” everybody yelled,
“We’ll never trade with Germany no more!”
But now, they kids “tli’ war-clouds is dispelled,”
So *"’Uns can come an’ find a open door.
An’ from ’is castle blood-stained Wilhelm sends
Undyin’ love t’ all ’is English friends! .
’Ave I gone ratty, balmy, up th’ pole?
Is this fair dinkum, that they’ve turned me down?
Ain’t these flagwaggers got no bloomin’ soul?
I ask f’r work, I want no laurel crown!
“'ls country’s saviour 1” w.’en I went they cried —
I’m ’ome again. Must I be crucified?
N.S.W.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

’['he figures far the Oct., 1918, half were
placed before shareholders in London in
March, and by that time Fritz had been
counted out and the ’Asia people had a fair
idea of immediate prospects. And they
looked so bright that, as one banker put it,
“the dreary process of writing down security
values seems to have ended for the time
being.” Disclosed net profits were there-
fore allowed to leap practically £25,000
ahead of other October term war-perform-
ances. But though war-time earnings have
been clipped in this “dreary process” of
providing for contingencies, gross profits
(less these unspecified clippings) have
bounded ahead :
Year to
Oct., Dross Profits. Expenses. Taxes.
1913.. £788,606 £299,818 £61,167
1914.. 836,549 307,623 74,520
1915.. 846.202 317,290 118.391

1917.. 9'2,206 349,751 196,810
1918.. 1,020,966 361,052 221,168
Who, in 191”, could have dared to forecast
that the ’Asia would emerge from four years’
experience of the world’s greatest war with-
out any scars and showing an increase of
almost 30 p.c. in its gross profits? After
providing additional props for its weaker
assets, increased taxes alone have prevented
the management disclosing record net profits.
Despite special war exes., the cost of earn-
ing the larger profits has, apart from taxes,
been proportionately smaller; but increased
taxes have lifted the total outgoings 01 p.c.
in five years.
Though there was always a doubt as to
what troubles might lie ahead, from a profit-
earning point of view the conditions during
the war period suited the big banks very
well. The inflation of the currency and
high prices for staples have given them
larger deposits to play with. This shows
how the ’Asia’s figures have moved :—-
Liabilities.
Oct., ’l3. Oct., ’l7. Oct., ’lB.

Bills, etc. . . 2,768,848 3,021,534 3,628,424

Premises . . 2,241 25,509 18,799
Shareholders’ funds five years ago amounted
to £4,890,109; in October last they were
£5,297,055 —an increase of £406,886. Liabili-
ties to .the public have meantime increased by
£5,267,268. So the management has had an
additional 5* millions to work for the bank.
Increased accommodation to customers alone
accounts for more than that. But a lot of
the advances on the books at the end of last
year were in lines returning comparatively
little profit—there has not been much in
wheat and War Loan advances, for instance.
Directly, when this money comes back again,
it will be free to flow in ordinary channels,
and promts should improve with the return
to normal conditions, even though taxation
is certain to be further increased, particu-
larly at the Australian end.
Besides being a very big concern the ’Asia
is in the Queen Liz. class in strength. It
holds £26.293,888 of the public’s money,
£8,448,724 of which is represented by liquid
assets, and about £2,862,000 of it by Govern-
ment securities “at market price or under,”
which hints that there is a substantial inner
reserve. To meet the £17,845,164 of liabili-
ties not represented by liquid assets there is
£23.123,421 of advances, on which the prun-
ing-knife has no doubt been busy during the
war years. Besides the advances, there are
premises, standing in the books at £18,799,
which is about the value of a foot or so of
the Sydney property alone. How much is
in this additional internal reserve, The
Bulletin has no idea; certainly hundreds
of thousands.
Shareholders have not suffered as a result
of the war. Even in the darkest patch they
got their 17 p.c. and left at least £30,000
of disclosed profit in the business! And
the 17 p.c. is free of income-tax, which makes
it equal to over p.c. on paid capital.
That dividend looks unreasonably large; but
since £3,127,055 of reserves have been
accumulated, while paid-up capital is two
millions, the div., free of income-tax, is
really equal to only a little over p.c. on
shareholders’ funds. Incidentally, the ’Asia
accounts will be made up yearly to October
in future, and the co.’s Charter is to be
altered to wipe, away the geographical re-
strictions which have so far kept it out of
four continents and the Pacific islands.

The Equitable finds present conditions good.
High rents have influenced people to set.
about acquiring their own homes, while the
artificial prosperity due to the war has given
many an opportunity to find the necessary
initial deposits to purchase property on t.-p.
During the war years the co. was cautious in
the matter of new advances, and 12 months
ago it had £44,000 piled up in “temporary in-
vestments.” Since the war-clouds lifted, how-
ever, the management has been lending more
freely, as shown in this comparison of eacli
year’s new advances :
1909.. £64,004 1915.. £82,093
1910.. 76,622 1916.. 71,907
1912.. 115,455 1917.. 85,482
1913.. 116,687 1918.. 75,797
1914.. 104,825 1919.. 97,918
There is now £330,534 out in mortgages,
compared with £336.553 five years ago. But
increased interest rates have made busi-
ness much more profitable: £36,940 was got
from interest earnings last year, compared
with £35,136 in 1914.
For shareholders the concern has been
very profitable. Nowadays they get a regu-
lar 10 p.c. p.a., and at the end of every three
years there is an additional distribution of
profits. Last year’s bonus of 6 p.c. meant
that for the three years 1916-18 shareholders
got 12 p.c. p.a. After paying divs. there
has always been a wide margin of profit left
in the business, and during the past 10 years
over £43.500 (or equal to nearly half the
paid capital) has been added to reserves.
Besides its own funds of almost £lBO.OOO.
the Equitable is working on £222.045 of
deposits; that is, for every £1 of its own
funds it holds £l 4s. Bd. of the public’s
money. The depositors are thus very well
protected, for after meeting all other liabili-
ties out of cash in hand, there are £330 h
of mortgages, £18,604 in freehold premises,
£6255 in bank stock and £46,633 in public
securities, deposits and cash, etc., to meet
their £222,045. So over 50 p.c. of the book
value of the mortgages and premises would
have to be lost before depositors were denied
a penny of their money.

That shows only an exact £4OOO tacked on
to reserves in four years; but the co. prob-
ably did better than shown by the figures
above. Nowadays a round thousand is
transferred to a dividend equalisation re-
serve, and the _ balance of net profits
after paying dividends is dropped into
a “rest account.” Apparently something
over • £14.000 has found its way into
this account since 1915, but how much
of the £14,000 could be regarded as true
reserves The Bulletin does not know.
Probably the fund is something in the nature
of a contingencies reserve, though the direc-
tors lump the account with ordinary trade
liabilities, and some of the £14,000 may
have disappeared altogether. But a glance
at the table shows that though over
£84,000 more is invested in the busi-
ness than 10 years ago, profits are
now smaller than they used to be. Mean-
time, from the point of view of consumption
everything has been bright in Queensland,
which was about the only State in the
Commonwealth which didn’t help to win the
war with early-closing of pubs. Apparently
there is now considerably less profit in beer
than there used to be; exes, have been climb-
ing steadily year after year, and it has been
difficult to pass the extra cost on. But all
the additional expenditure has not been in-
curred in the brewing, for administration
charges and taxes now take a much larger
cut, as these figures show :

The properties and plant item continues to

the days of fat profits have gone for good.

This co. is in a difficult business; it con-
tracts to clip squatters’ jumbucks over a
period of years, and results depend upon
erratic factors. If seasons are unfavorable
it is likely to be let in for a lot of “charity”
work and show losses; and also it has to
stand the racket of all labor troubles. The
loss of £9734 made on shearing 6,401,400
sheep in 1916 and the profit of £12,589 from
clipping 3,975,998 for the past year illustrate
the extremes of the business. The 1916
season was abnormally wet, and labor both
scarce and troublesome; in fact, conditions
were just about as bad as .they could be.
Last season was unusually good, and re-
turned a net profit of almost 56 p.c. on paid
caxiital. But, besides unfavorable seasons,
the co. has had other troubles. In 1912 it
was formed to take over the Federal Sheep
Shearing Co., Ltd., paying £12,000 cash and
10.000 fully-paid 10s. shares to the vendor
concern, and making available a further
10.000 fully-paids as a bait to get in con-
trib. capital, every four contribs. sub-
scribed being entitled to one bonus share.
Of the paid capital of £22,562 at December
31 last no less than £8631 consisted of good-
will froth and bonus-share bubbles. The con-
cern was thus raised on weak foundations. A
new crowd is in command nowadays, and is
doing the proper thing and blowing oft' most
of the froth —£7520 of the capital going to
reduce goodwill to £llll. Contributing
shareholders lose the value of the bonus
shares they got, plus 4d. per share, which
isn’t much. Paid capital will now stand at
£15.041 (25 493 fully-paid 6s. Bd. shares and
32,717 contribs. paid to 45., with a further
2s. Bd. callable). As the table shows, the
whole of the capital is now represented by
tangible assets, with £164 to spare—that is.
if the assets are worth every penny they are
shown at in the books. The new manage-
ment also soon decided that the control from
Sydney of operations in three States was not
satisfactory, so a local director in Queens-
land and another in Victoria are attending
to the co.’s interests in those States. The
concern is now on a sounder basis, and appa-
rently more efficiently managed. But its
results depend on factors beyond the control
of any management, profits will always be
erratic. Expenses are also necessarily heavy
owing to the wide field covered by the co.’s
operations—administration exes, last year,
for instance, took 10s. Bd. per 100 sheep
shorn ; and these figures are more likely to
increase than decrease irp difficult years.

The slump in profits is explained by this
line in the superintendent’s report: “Thirty-
seven days were lost at Stanford Merthyr
during the half-year by petty strikes.” So
receipts were only £80,246 last half, com-
pared with £121,684 for the previous six
months. It is not quite clear how the co.
will fare under the recent increase in wages
and prices, granted by the Commonwealth
Government without any serious considera-
tion of the justification of the miners’
demands for further pay or whether the 3s.
per ton increase in price was a fhir thing to
pass on to the consumer. However, the N.
S. Wales Royal Commission is digging deep
into coal cos.’ affairs, and though some of
the evidence and statistics forthcoming
doesn’t look very interesting. Commissioner
Campbell is being armed with a lot of infor-
mation which will enable him to show
exactly where the consumer and the cos.
stand as a result of the latest squeeze.
So far as East Greta is concerned, the
table does not indicate that the co. has been
able to make its shareholders unduly fat.
Since 1915 no new capital has been got in.
and net profits have averaged 7f p.c. p.a., of
which shareholders have had an average of
6| p.c. each year. Rut this gives another
view of the position:—
Mar., ’l5. Mar., ’l9.
Liquid assets.. .. £19,044 £75,537
Liabilities . . . . 34,642 70,190
So at March 31 last, before handing oui
the half-year’s div.. there was a surplus of
liquid assets over liabilities of £5347, while
four years ago there was an excess of lia-
bilities of £15.598. Meantime, the book value
of fixed assets has shrunk by about £5500.
Paid capital is £200.000. and £60,000 of the
liabilities shown above represents deben-
tures. Besides acquiring and equipping the
East Greta, Stanford Merthyr and Heddon
Greta collieries, the co. also put a lot of
money into the East Greta railway. This
line has now been taken over by the South
Maitland Railways, Ltd., and the balance-
sheet makes some interesting disclosures by
separating the railway and mining interests.
For its line, rolling-stock, etc., the East
Greta apparently received £149.798 in shares
in the South Maitland Railways. Mining
and house properties now stand in the books
at only £17.668. the Heddon Greta property
at £1937 (£6OOO was paid for it five years
ago), while plant and equipment represent
£76,877. The management is at present test-
ing a new area of 3507 acres near Cessnoc-k,
and boring has proved a seam lift, thick.
So far. £2302 has been laid out on probing
this Millfield property, and a further two
bores are now being put down.
When the Holman Government saw in the
tote an easy way to get money, there
was a flutter in Totalling Mechanisms, Ltd.,
the fully-paid £1 shares soaring from Bs. to
40s. Later, it was decided to reconstruct,
and the old co. of 1911, whose paid eanital
was £42,300. became Automatic Totalisators
Ltd., in 94,966 shares of £1 fully-paid. In
December last the Board secured the world's
rights of the Premier totalisator. but how
many shareholders knew that £5OOO was paid
in cash and £97,000 in shares? Were share-
holders consulted as to the advisability of
more than doubling the issued capital by the
acquisition of “rights” of an ent'rely un-
known value? However, a nice little model
of a Premier tote has been on view lately.

Manager Boyd, combating the A.W.U.’s
claim for a new award for Mt. Morgan, pro-
duced some interesting figures concerning
recent operations at the big show. At the
beginning of last month 3230 tons copper
remained unsold, and the best price offering
was £67 per ton. Each ton cost the co.
£135 15s. to produce in the half-year ended
June, 1918, and in the following half-year
£132 35., while for the 20 weeks ended April
20 expenses went up to £143 Is. 2d. per ton.
But as the copper contained an average of
12|oz. gold per ton. the actual cost per ton
copper was about £9O. So Mt. Morgan has
now about £370,000 »piled up. Copper
stocks held in England, France, U.S.A.
and Australia are estimated at about
671,330 tons, or enough to supply the pre-
war demand for nine months. As a result
of 44 years’ fighting it is reckoned that about
three million tons of copper are lying about
in France and Belgium, and of that about
a million tons are recoverable. A large por-
tion of it is in dumps which could be re-
smelted without much expense; and battle-
field salvage is exceeding expectations.
Dick Williams, of Edna May Battler
(W.A.). has been telling shareholders some-
thing about the co.’s new go, the Trafalgar,
out from Southern Cross. Williams gives
the mine a good record, and, inferentially,
suggests that the old crowd would not have let
go if they had not specialised in rich blocks.
But they didn’t let much slip past them at
the 130 ft. level, as they grubbed out 300 ft.
of a shoot, taking 3ft. to Oft. of ore the
length ot‘ the drive. They also went under-
foot 50ft. to water-level, raking out another
150 ft. of quartz. Where they stuck was
over the sinking of a shaft to 260 ft. This
let the Battler and Williams hop in with
£3OOO cash for the mine. The show has two
chances. One is the further opening up of
the West reef after shaft-sinking, and to test
the North lode, on which only pig-rooting
has been done to date. Out of the West
reef, apart from 2000 tons stone milled for
25000 z., Williams has left a lot of low-grade
material to worry over as soon as he gets
the mill to the mine from the old show at
Westonia. That ore, he concludes, will yield
a few bob a ton and help to save calls. With
all this in the mine’s favor, Williams ends
up with : “We will open up a profitable and
valuable mining property !” Perhaps.
The two local University professors, the
Alkali Commission and Brunner. Mond’s
visiting expert should not have much diffi-
culty in convincing the British Alkali Trust
that Westralia possesses all the requisites
for the manufacture of cheap soda and
its accessories. Lake Preston, 25 miles
long by one and a half wide, is a brine
of varying salt content from 4 p.c. to 12 p.c.,
according to the dryness of the season, and
another lake to the north, 12 miles long by
half a mile wide, contains millions of tons of
finely-granulated carbonate of lime as its bed.
Between the two lakes are natural depres-
sions, which could serve as brine concentra-
tors by solar evaporation. Collie Coal is
only 70 miles distant, from which the small
quantity of ammonia necessary in the pro-
cess could be very cheaply obtained either
from a carbonising and by-product installa-
tion on the coal or by utilising the Mond sys-
tem for heat and power generating at the
alkali works. Coke of high purity is the
other necessary ingredient, and blackboy coke
is the last word in pure fuels,giving nearly
15,000 B.T.U. per lb. and containing only ’
1 p.c. of ash. Altogether, for cheapness and
ready accessibilities of raw materials, this
small portion of Westralia is without an
equal south of the line, and needs only a few
miles of connecting rail to the Waroona-
Lake Clifton line (now under construction)
to complete the programme.
Nowadays Bullfinch Proprietary (W.A.)
just manages to keep going and hold off the
bailiff. There are plenty of faces of ore in
the mine, but the rich values of the boom-
time are now as poor as Lazarus. Big lodes
of 3dwt. to 4dwt. stuff and patches of better-
grade stone are visible in various parts of
the mine, but the average is not good enough
for profit-earning. The co. is still living in
hope; but at present it is having a very cold
time.
* * * *
Phillips River (W.A.) is again dead. For
some time returns from ore treated at the
local State smelter kept the place going, but
as the quantity furnished for treatment was
not sufficient to keep the plant going more
than a little while every now and then the
Government, after deciding that the loss was
not worth while, suddenly shut down. Min-
ister for Mines Robinson is now considering
the best ways and means of rendering assist-
ance to the stranded.

Hardly had the Duke Extended Board let
out the hews of a 2d. call to keep the wolf
from the door than Ballarat got out one of
its 'hot surprises into Alec Peacock’s office.
Shares dodged up in the market, and loud
was the howl for blood from those who had
rashly cut free from a call-making _ show
supposedly at the end of mining possibilities.
This impression arose because at the west end.
where the gold ought to be. the wash was
cemented stuff with not a color. At the east
end the gutter had bumped about so that it
was thought to be over the fence in the Duke
Consols ground. The story that got lan-
guidly into print was that bores put into the
wash at the west end had given good pro-
spects with gold of the right sample. At the
opposite end of the mine somethin? about as
tangible was said to be on the tapis.
Bryant’s luck was also in, for when things
were at their worst he was invited to come
along and help. Almost as he stepped out
of the car. up the shaft floated word of
good bores. But it is obvious that with all
its available wash stoped the Duke Extended
has to do a lot of development, which means
calls. So the luck of those who scraped in
Shares after the call-notice may not be so
great as is recorded.
When the sad fact of an empty till was
whispered in the Board-room of the Al, Gaff-
ney’s Creek (Vic.), it is said that the
“Money” Miller crowd quoted Omar: “Fill
me with the old familiar juice; But bury me
by some sweet garden side.” Of course, if
Lyell has to dodge divs., why shouldn’t Al?
But the trouble is that there had also to be a
swing on to the call-list. This misfortune
comes soon after the clever ones of the co.
got the bears in a red-hot squeeze. The
Bulletin gave a warning when oversolds
were buying back at 355. a share, with never
a soul to meet them half-way. Time has
brought its revenge, for who is there now
to take scrip at a quarter of that price?
The squeezed ones have stalked the stock
down as ruthlessly as (fid broker Vickery the
Bear Hill crowd, when he went chasing after
the £35,000 they made him pay for being
caught short in that stock. In both cases
the experience was that when those who
build up a corner want to 'sidle out they find
the banks very crumbly material. The expla-
nation of the call is that the mine must be
developed in accordance with the recommen-
dations of the manager, and that capital has
to be got. So the inside situation is that the
Al is as unreliable as most of the mountain
shows in the matter of permanence. The
floors rapidly cut in and out of gold—
mostly out.

Tyrconnel North, working on penny calls,
has burst through six reefs in the big tunnel
in the Sultan country at Blackwood (Vic.).
The working is in nearly half a mile, and at
2399 ft. driving is proceeding north on a lode,
the intention being to nick up a shoot of
stone that went underfoot in the old mine
overhead. Once that shoot is got the co. will
have 250 ft. of backs. Except in the case of the
Long Tunnel, Walhalla, every job like
that of the Tyrconnel North has seen cash
lost, but in country where the stone was as
rich as on the Sultan line, the gamble was
worth taking. Seeing that the water from
the old mines is draining off so slowly despite
the faulted country where driving is going
on, it looks as if the right lode formation
hasn’t yet been tapped. But with the mines
dry, what a share rustle there will be if the
old hands left a few blocks for the Tyrconnel
North.
“J.L.S.” : It has been suggested that Mor-
well (Vic.) raw brown coal should be wortn
355. a ton —or more. But comparison with
German experience is quite unwarranted, for
the simples reason that stoves, fire-boxes, fur-
naces and fuel-burning receptacles generally
are adopted, and have been constructed for
years past in that country, to burn com-
pressed dry lignite briquettes of about 10,000
B.T.U. maximum thermal value. In Vic-
toria, which has always relied on N. S.
Wales for black coal running 12-13,000
B.T.U. per lb., the fuel-burning receptacles
on the railways and for industrial and domes-
tic purposes are (with one or two recent ex-
ceptions) designed and built solely for black-
coalfiring. Morwell has one big advantage
over other low-grade fuels in Australia,
namely, cheapness of mining; and one or
two lesser advantages, such as the enormous,
quantities available (of which only a limited
amount at a time can be used), and its rather
high yield of oil by-products. Against this is
the fact that to utilise the fuel properly it
must be concentrated by carbonising and bri-
quetting the carbonised residue, and there the
binder difficulty obtrudes. The freight to
Melbourne from Morwell (ss. per ton) is
also an item in a low-grade fuel proposition.
From continuous work during the past three
years on the problems of successfully utilis-
ing Morwell brown coal commercially, I
have come to the conclusion that the so-called
untold wealth in brown coal is more or less
mythical when considered in conjunction
with the local conditions obtaining. It is for
future generations to reap the harvest. The
proposition of how best to treat Morwell coal
for the contemplated power-scheme and,
separately, as a source of supply of indus-
trial and domestic fuel to Victoria in com-
petition with Newcastle is not likely to be
settled until considerably more initial work
on briquetting, by-product recovery and
brown-coal firing generally have been carried
out. Fortunately the present Electricity
Commissioners and Messrs. Harper and Her-
man have no illusions on the potentialities of
brown coal as a short cut to untold wealth ;
and they are not likely to encourage fancy
schemes for treating the stuff unless backed
by adequate guarantees or demonstrated
successfully by large-scale trials.
The additional plant installed by King
Island Scheelite, in which the B. 11. Block 14
has such a big share-holding, resulted in the
mill in its last six months’ campaign getting
through over 12,000 tons of crush dirt. This
averaged .89 p.c. tungstic acid, and as the
resultant concentrates went practically 70
p.c. of that material, the recovery of ore con-
tents was a bit better than 77 p.c. In other
words, as the 11.0 i tons of concentrates are
valued at £20,261, then they were worth,
roughly, £lB3 per ton for a 70 p.c. product.
It took 114 tons ore to give a ton of con-
centrates, so that as costs approximated £75
per ton of concentrates, the working profit
made was £11,280. This permitted a divi-
dend of Is. per share, but, of course, the
price of the mine product was regulated by
war conditions. It is that fact that makes
the future of all scheelite and molybdenite
shows uncertain. Conditions at King Island
are ideal for cheap working, and Venn Brown
gets his costs down to 13s. G}d. per ton.
Developments on the West formation are
satisfactory, and those at the northern work-
ing are promising; so that there is more than
a kick left in the show, even above late
measured reserves, if the metal market keeps
kindly.
* * * #
The old argument re bringing the battery
to the ore or taking ore to the battery has
been revived on the molybdenite field near
Yetholme (N.S.W.). For months past the
Mammoth has had its plant at work on the
deposit on the mountain crest. The lifting
of the machinery in the first place was a big
job. The position of the plant also meant
a four-mile water pipe-line from a spring-
supplied dam, which fell below requirements
during the drought. The tapping of a
creek en route has since given enough water.
Mammoth has thus to meet transport charges
for good. The Mt. Tennyson co. has taken
the course of bringing the stone to the mill.
Its plant has been put at the foot of the
mountain, up the side of which about 1700 ft.
of aerial ropeway climbs 600 ft. The stone
is run into hoppers to the loading station,
and is moved along by its own weight, at a
calculated cost of less than 3d. a ton. The
water supply is close at hand —the creek
which the Mammoth has tapped further up
—while the main western line lies only a
mile off.
* * * *
Mt. Cuthbert, Cloncurry (Q.), is continu-
ing development work on several of its
widely separated leases. The parallel ore
body at the 150 ft. level in the Ivalkadoon
has been driven 139 ft. to the north, the lode
beina- sft. wide and showing 16 p.c. copper.
South, the drive is in 99ft. in country.
Prospects are good at the 250 ft. level, for the
north drive is in 60ft. and the ore broken is
going 10 p.c. A crosscut from the main
shaft will also reach the ore body at the
350 ft. level, which is the deepest in the
mine. A little work is also being done on
the Orphan and Little Wonder leases, and
a new hauling-shaft is down 28ft. at the
Mighty Atom.

The osmiridium scratchers in the Speck
have lately been up against an old trouble
—they cannot find a market. Nominally
the metal is quoted at £lO to £l2 per oz„
but no one seems to want to buy. Twelve
months ago the Speck was over-run with
agents, and fountain-pen manufacturers,
electrical manufacturers, etc., all anxious to
give up to £3O an ounce.
From R. H. Crozier :—•
“Wild Cat” (B. 15/5/’l9) remarks that because
the promoters of the Colossal Gold Mining Co.,
Ravenswood (N.Q.), are taking 90 paid-up shares in
a register of 340 shares, equal to 26.4 p.c., “N.
Queensland hasn’t forgotten the old trick's of float-
ing.” If this may be regarded as a trick it would
appear to be rather better known to Southern pro-
moters, as in a par. above The Bulletin gives par-
ticulars of a new Tasmanian venture in which the pro-
moters acquire no less than 37.5 p.c. paid-up interest
in the total register. However, apart from the leases,
on the acquisition and examination of which a con-
siderable sum has been expended, and which con-
tain a large tonnage of ore immediately available
without cost for development, the promoters of the
Colossal have obtained an option over a 35-head
battery, complete in every respect, with Wilflev
tables, cyanide plant and all auxiliary equipment,
which will be handed over to the co. without profit
for £2OOO. This plant would cost, under present
conditions, in the vicinity of £30,000 to erect, and
represents an asset of very considerable value. The
fact that this plant is available, that such a large
tonnage of ore can be mined by quarrying methods,
and that the supplies of fuel, water and labor need
cause no concern practically assures the success of
the venture, which has every prospect of developing
into one of the largest low-grade propositions in Aus-
tralia. Laboratory tests indicate that 85 p.c. of the
gold is recoverable by amalgamation and cyanidation,
reduced the residue to sd. per ton.

ANTICIPATED RESUMPTION OF
TRADE.
It is estimated that in August last Great
Britain held only two months’ supply of her
normal home consumption of raw cocoa, the
cause being the same that keeps Australian
wheat in Australia —shortage of shipping,
for there are areat accumulations of cocoa
in all the producing countries. Now that
the war is over, these conditions should not
obtain for long. The British Government
is bound, on every consideration, to remove
all obstacles to the resumption of trade at
the earliest possible moment. We may
therefore hope shortly to learn that the pro-
hibition of export of cocoa and chocolate
from Great Britain has been removed, when,
acting in concert. Australia will doubtless
remove such bar as exists at this end also,
and so far open the way for Cadbury’s and
Bournville Cocoas and Chocolates to take
their old positions in the shops aj^iin.
The latest issue to hand of the Boubn-
ville Works Magazine gives the total of
Bournville men on war service as 4603. of
whom about half remain on the firm’s books
as employees. They also refer to the
further grant by the firm of a capital sum
to provide pensions for the widows of pen-
sioners equal in amount to half those re-
ceived by the pensioner himself. The warm
reception given to this additional gift
( £60.000 in all) was the most striking
feature of the general meeting of the Bourn-
ville Works Pension Fund. One of the
speakers said that it meant the greatest
possible relief to the workers, one of whose
deepest anxieties in life had been relieved
by its provision, viz., that the wife, if surviv-
ing him. would be provided for in her old
age.— Advt.

GUSTAVE WAETHERS, appointed violin-
ist at Melb. University Conservatorium.
•C. N. Mackenzie, a son of Tom Macken-
zie, High Commissioner for Maoriland, is
the guest of Admiral Jellicoe on board the
warship New Zealand. He was in the first
Maoriland contingent, and lost the sight of
both eyes in action at Gallipoli. While in
London, in spite of his blindness, Macken-
zie edited and produced a paper which was
devoted to matters concerning the Maori-
land soldiers.
Hobart recently threw bouquets at a one-
man show of fine stuff produced by Jack Cato,
a Tassy boy recently returned from an eight
years’ drift around. He is the youngest
fellow of the Royal Photographic Society of
Britain, and is said to be the only one with
that coveted degree practising portraiture in
Australia. Cato has expressed Central
Africa in art as Cazneaux has pictured Aus-
tralia. He can also express another picture,
radiating with tropical heat, of the pest
and pestilence he encountered in the recent
German rebellion in that fever-stricken
country.
Septimus Burt, K.C., senior partner of
the ancient Perth law-firm of Stone and
Burt, died last week after an extended ill-
ness. In the days when the Leg. Council
was the only Westralian Par-
liament and open voting pre-
vailed for the election of such
members as were not nominees.
Burt held down a seat in the
House for several years. Two
members of the old firm went
to the Chief Justice’s seat —the
late Archibald Burt, father of
Septimus, who was his seventh
son, and Edward Stone, now
Lieut.-Governor. Stone, shortly
after his admission to the Bar,
in 1565, led the Reform League,
which had as its chief aim the
institution of Responsible Gov-
ernment. Burt, long afterwards,
had the portfolio of Attor-
ney-General in the first Ministry under the
new system, formed by Bigjolin Forrest.
“Billy T.”: A Melbourne barrister who
was in Cliidley’s confidence imparts a few
interesting facts in connection with that
quaint dresser. Chidley was at one time an
actor in Australia, and, under a stage-name
and in company with his wife, described as a
big, beautiful blonde, ran a show which
profited moderately in the smalls—about Vic.
mainly. Another fact that should give some
of our smart Alecks gravely to think is that
Chid, was a noble Roman of exceptional
strength, and a trained athlete who had
killed a man in Adelaide in fair fight and
had stood his trial for manslaughter there.
He came upon a man viciously maltreating
a woman, and his defence was that he had
every reason to believe the woman’s life was
endangered. This was supported by evi-
and Chidley was acquitted; but the
experience left its mark on him, and he said
to his Melbourne lawyer: “When I am gibed
at in the street by these young fellows, I
always call to mind that I have killed one. I
don’t want to kill another.” Any reader of
this who may have scoffed at the big fellow
in the little short shirt at close quarters can
now ruminate on the fact that he was in a
way toying with the tail of a tiger.
“A.F.C.”: Ifieut.-Colonel Oswald Watt,
who was in command of the Australian Inly-
ing Corps in the place where the war was,
is a Riverina pastoralist who was caught in
the whirlpool and joined up just where
he happened to be —which was France.
When the A.F.C. was formed he was per-
mitted to take his numerous FTench decora-
tions over to the Australians, and became
the first O.C. of No. 2 Squadron. There
was no more popular O.C. in France.
Filter, when he took charge of the Austra-
lian Training Wing, pilots were no longer
sent across as soon as they could tell the
joy-stick from the throttle, but were given
a thorough training and developed the habit
of bagging Huns from the jump-off.
Riverina will be thrilled if he brings back
his little “White Angel” Avro machine,
which was the pride of the Wing. With
his experience he is the man most fitted to
take charge of the newly-formed Australian
Air Force.
Lieut.-Col. B. C. Freyberg, the Maori-
lander who so greatly distinguished himself
in the field, winning the V.C. and the D.S.O.
(three times), has joined the Grenadier
Guards as a captain, with the brevet rank
of lieut.-colonel and temporary rank of
brigadier-general.
Dead of the ’flu at North Sydney, after
four years’ active service on various Fronts,
Dr. (Ifieut.-Col.) John Basil St. Vincent
Welch, D.S.O. He was second son of the
late J. St. Vincent Welch, for many years a
leading insurance man in Sydney. The
Colonel was one of the first to go to the
Front, and only returned a few months ago
to civil practice. Four Welch brothers were
in the medical line, three qualified physicians
and one a chemist. All three medicoes took
a turn in the war.
The most widely known victim of pneu-
monic so far in Adelaide is Archie Camp-
bell Magarey. one of the many doctors
in that notable medical family. He had a
brilliant career at St. I’eter’s school, the
University and in I.ondon, and saw war ser-
vice in New Guinea. Only 34 at the end.
As a Victorian Minister Peacock occup'ed
as many offices as Griffith, High Court C.J.,
did when in politics. Alec was Honorary
Minister. under Munro, and then in subse-
quent Cabinets held the Education, Chief
Sec-retary’s, Labor, Postmaster-General, Pre-
mier’s and Treasurer’s portfolios. At times
he acted for every Minister except the
Attorney-General and Solicitor-General. He
drew the line at law.
The appointment of Major I. Isaacson, of
St. Kilda (Vic.), as officer commanding on
the troopship which is to convey released
German internees back to the Faderland, has
aroused a good deal of adverse comment in
military circles. Although Isaacson left
Australia in command of a fighting unit, he
was engaged in administrative work while
he was abroad, and the reasonable growl
is that the O.C.’s position, which carries a
good salary and better “prospects,” should
have been given to a combatant officer.

“E.G.”: Brigadier-General G. Ramaciotti,
who looks much less vigorous than when he
was military commandant in N. S. Wales,
will shortly retire from the position of
Inspector-General of Defence Administration.
He will afterwards revisit his beloved Italy,
where he is almost as much at home as. in
Melbourne or Sydney. Since his appoint-
ment as Inspector-General, he has done good
work. He was formerly chairman of the
Commission which inquired into the question
of Home Service personnel. Substantial re-
ductions were effected as the result of that
Commission’s recommendations, many super-
fluous officers and men being ejected from
nice comfy billets.
M. M. Maughan, Director of Education
in S.A., has resigned his office, after a
long struggle with ill health. He is only 62
and much better than he was a little while
ago, but medical opinion is firm against his
getting into harness again. He was in'the
old Training College under Madley, and has
been, like that instructor of youth, a keen
soldier in his spare time. The successorship
to his billet seems a very open thing. The
Minister of Education has given the Adel-
aide dailies what is practically a guarantee
that he will not go outside the State for a
man.
(’apt. Keith Calvert, of the Middlesex
Hussars, is back at Dreeite, the parental
roost near Colac, with an M.C. on his chest.
Most of his war-work was done in France
and Belgium.
Although the late Sam Winter, boss of
the Melbourne Herald, did more than any
other man in the State to keep the A.N.A.
going in Victoria, his name was not men-
tioned the other night when the veterans of
the past were telling the young lions of the
present how hard they had worked. But for
Winter, Red Head Jones and other A.N.A.
enthuiasts on his staff, who puffed on the
embers with the bellow r s of publicity, the
organisation would have been in the ashpit, at
any time up to 1886.
“Bad Australian’s” friend:—
Jacques Lebaudy, “King of the Sahara,” shot re-
cently in U.S.A., was the queerest mixture
of artfulness, damfoolishness and plain cussed-
ness I ever struck. About three years ago* he
fled out of Chile after raising Cain in Santiago over
some Bolivian and Chilian mines in which he was
interested, and established his temporary headquar-
ters at a pub just across the Argentine frontier in the
heart of the Andes. He put in his time sending long
cables to more or less eminent people- in Europe and
the States and careering around the settlement on a
mule trailing a lasso after him with which
he amused himself trying to rope the casual way-
farer. One afternoon he came galloping into the
corral where a bunch of transport mules were kept,
and in his wild career nearly ran over one of the
arrieros kiddies. \\ hen I remonstrated with him he
cursed me with a great and comprehensive anathema
in four different languages, and when I threatened
to tear him apart from the other mule and deal with
him, he just went mad. “You tell me zat you put ze
boot into me. Me! Jack Lebaudy! By gar, I tell
you zat I can haf you keel in two meenit for talk lak
zat.” Then very venomously and slowly: “Aaa-haa,
you dam’ Inglis’ pore. I weel scr-r-rew your dam’
Inglis’ head off —so! And your bodee, I weel t’ro.v
lieem down ze montane ze same lak one dam’ skonk.”
Then he gathered up his lasso and fled up the moun-
tain-side to the pub. He came to me a few davs
later with a business proposition. “Zey do not treat
me too good, zese Bolivianos. You haf b’en long
tarn zere. You know zem all right. Zey are a lot
of dam’ indios. Me, I do not lak zem. You, Henree,
will get four or five liondred of your fr’en's—zese
Chilian bandidos—togezzer. I weel fin’ ze rifle an’
ze machine-gon an’ ze ozzer sings. Zen we go to
Bolivia an’ w’at you call play Hell. An’ me, I weel
be ze Presidente; an’ you, you weel be my Chief
Ministro an’ mak ze good time wiz your bandidos.
W’at you say?” 1 said: “No.” “But, by gar, 1
require you. I deman’ yon do zis t’ing. T pay you
w’at you lak—more zan you lak. Eet ees all
arrange.” It was not “all arrange.” So he galloped
off to think out a fresh scheme. Two days later he
came to me waving his arms and spitting like a
demented, tomcat. “I weel not stay in zis dam’
place. I weel get out. Why do I act lak zis? Ze
grub in ze hotel I cannot eat lieem. 1 am hongree,
veree hongree in ze night-tarn’. 1 get up. I go to zee
cookshop. Ze chef he ees not zere. I tak ze stek, ye
chop. I go down to ze salon. I light ze fire in ze
salon. I am cook ze chop. Ze managaire he come in
liees pyjam’. He ea’ch me by ze slack of ze pant
an’ he pelt me —me —Jack Lebaudy—Sacrc Dieu —he
pelt me out in ze patio, an’ 1 keek ze door all ze
night. By gar, I buy ze dam’ hotel an’ blow heein
in’ ze managaire to blazes.” Then he darted
olf to make arrangements for a special train to Chile,
which he got about four hours later; and the last
I heard of him was that he was grabbed for criminal
libel and clapped in the cooling-house immediately
on his arrival in Los Andes.

CROIX DE GUERRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"You Have heard, of course, that Eric got the
C roix de Guerre.”- —Extract from, letter.
A story comes from “over there,”
A story of red days that were —
My brother won the Croix de Guerre.
So in the household book of fame,
Where kindly praise outweighs the blame,
Is written now the soldier’s name.
The youth I knew was shy of dreams,
Afraid of what he thought extremes,
Upholding rather “is” than “seems.”
Hut here it is “for Valor” writ;
And valor is a dream, close-knit
With all the high extremes that flit
from God to man and man to God,
Wherever man goes triumph-shod
Along white paths before untrod.
My brother’s grandsons, too, will read
The written story of his deed,
His answer to the day’s black need.
And to the camp beneath the gums
from far away to me it comes,
Ail echo of the silenced drums.
By long sea pathways wide and free,
The story finds its way to me.
I look a little back and see
Tim boy with soul of honor rare- -
'I he tall, gay soldier debonair—-
Young Erie, with his Croix de Guerre!
x.s.vv.
HILARY LOFTING.

SOCIETY
Australia has had two great thrills with-
in the past week, although it shared one—
Hawker’s rescue—with the wide world. The
first was the sailing into the home ports of
the dauntless Australian destroyers and the
cruiser Melbourne from the far seas where,
for four years, they had been snuffling about
like terriers for the hidden rats. Great was
the welcome from Darwin to Port Phillip 1
Then followed the blow of the announce-
ment that the daring Victorian airman.
Hawker, and Grieve, his companion on the
flight across the Atlantic, had been given
up for lost. For days Australia grieved as
if each of her citizens had lost a son. May-
be Providence was touched, for. suddenly,
on Monday, news flashed through of the
rescue; and if Providence had been handy
the Commonwealth would have made it its
eternal President, with special permission to
live out of Canberra.
On the day the Australian destroyers
arrived in Port Jackson, a Digger,
giving the fleet a. welcome, rolled up George-
street. and bumped a general, or. maybe, it-
was an admiral. The soldier swung to atten-
tion (or thereabouts), saluted and pro-
ceeded to count the color ribbon-tags on the
officer’s tunic: “One. two, three {hid), four
—-and y’ earned every one of them,
Put it there, Digger!” ~
BILLJAC'K COMES HOME AND HAS A
DAY OFF.
“Hamer’s” communication: —
Discipline burst its bands on Armistice
Day, and no really serious attempt has been made to
re-establish it. The authorities, as delays in demobil-
isation occur, are getting very “windy” about the
possibility of outbreaks that arc more than disregard
to mere military order. “Orders” are full of
explanations of delays and appeals to the men to
“play the game.” In one camp morning parades,
which are only attended by men who wish to hear
the reading of any boat-rolls, are only a mob-like
muster; and, while the officer reads the orders-of-the-
day, the men form little rings to play two-up, crown-
and-ancbor and other national games. Generally
there is nothing over the odds in the men’s behavior,
but they simply will not recognise any necessity for
the continuation of military routine. To keep men
at work on the London Headquarters it was found
necessary to offer extra ration-,money, the rate now
paid being 6s. a day. Occasionally, when the food
in any camp is bad, or restrictions are put upon
leave, the threat is circulated that the camp will be
burnt down unless matters are improved; and this
usually brings about the visit of an agitated general
with more appeals about playing the game and pro-
mises of redress, which are generally kept. There
are the elements of disorder amongst all these im-
patient men, and the officers responsible for their
good behavior feel by no means happy.
The venomous legacy left behind by Budd.
the South Australian murderer lately
hanged, is not to have any effect, appa-
rently. After swearing throughout that he
knew nothing of the murder of the man
whose body was found buried under his
motor-shed, he left, at the last, a written
statement that another man whom he named
was equally guilty. S.A. Government put
detective Allchurch on to the matter. His
report, which will probably be taken as
final, is that after full and wide inquiry, lie
Cannot find “a tittle of evidence” connecting
the other man (whose name meanwhile has
been freely mentioned in the newspapers)
with the matter. On the other hand, he
has come across a lot of fresh evidence mak-
ing clearer than ever the guilt of Budd.

“Snell”: It's not true that the poor know
nothing of economics. I cot first-hand in-
formation on the subject from Mrs. Canty
and Mrs. Breen, who were having a heart-
to-heart talk in our right-of-way the other
morning. Mrs. Canty is about sft. 9in.
high, and as far round as a picnic-
marquee. Mrs. Breen is small, thin and
hen-like. Mrs. Canty commented with un-
due familiarity on Mrs. Breen’s black eye.
"Well.” she said. “I would let a dirty bit iv
a shrimp iv a husban’ like that wan iv yours
bate me, I would!” “And where’s yer own
husban’, Mrs. Canty?” asked Mrs. Breen.
“In bed. is lie? You beat him, you did, till
lie can’t go to work for a week. Breen beats
me and he’s off to his work on Monday morn-
in'. and not losing a shift at all. I’ll take
me own black eye, thankin’ you. Mrs. Canty,
with the high cost of livin’ and all.”

* * * *
“J.G.”: Maria Lavell, who succeeded in
divorcing her husband in Melbourne the
other day, is Victoria’s latest womamman.
She had for years passed as a male and did
very heavy graft. Unlike another of the
same type of woman, De Lacy Evans, of the
early Bendigo goldfields, Maria acquired a
real husband. Evans was “married” to and
lived for many years with another woman.
Marion Edwards, who created a stir
a few years ago, is, as far as is known, still
wearing the trousers. Another notable in-
stance was the Elmore district girl, Gorge a-
sen, who lived as a boy and man. She suc-
ceeded in bluffing a doctor and got into the
Victorian Mounted Rifles as a member of
the Elmore troop. She was a member of it
till she died.

home Mrs. Caudle will have to be patient
under an administration of some of her own
medicine. The war has loosened the nerves
of many soldiers, and developed the habit of
talking in their sleep in many war-stressed

conversation, which continues in fits ami
starts throughout the night, consisting of
sighs, exclamations, and scattered remarks
about girls, shells, tucker, officers and various
other important or unimportant details.
Probably the mutterings about girls will
occasion most trouble when the men get back
to the connubial couch.

“Uckle”: My Digger brother and a cob-
ber went into a watchmaker’s in France to
get a watch repaired. Asked if she under-
stood English the lady in charge replied,
“Verra leetle.” “Well,” said the cobber,
“me flamin’ turnip’s gone bung!” And then
she didn’t compree.
* * * *
The Man on the Seat’s yarn :
“I dunno what to think of that new bloke up et
the ’ouse,” said the one with the yellow feather.
“The feller they’re callin’ Pincher? He s awful
’ansom an’ perlite,” ;
“Oh, he’s that all right. Yesdee when me an
Mord was cornin’ back from the market, me with
two bags of vegetables, a parcel of meat an’ a new
’at shape, and Mord with more, we ’ad the luck to
get on a tram pack jambed, an’ o’ course we has
to stand. This chap is there afore us, standin’ too.
Up goes his lid like a nob, an’ sez he in his comic
way, ‘Tike my seat, ladies.’ ”
“That’s ’im all right. He's a trick.”
“Well, when it comes to payin’, me bein’ loaded
down, I has a job gettin’ at me purse, when Pincher
chips in agen: ‘ ’Low me,’ sez he; ‘l’ll pay it for
yeh.’ ”
“Didn’t I say he was perlite?” cried the girl in
the pink blouse, full of admiration for the gentle-
manly Pincher.
“Yes, you did, Sis. But wait. Hanged if be
don't take the purse out of me ’and. open it, dig
out a sprat, an’ sez he to the conductor: ‘Three
tupneys, boss.’ He perlitely pays fer us out of
me own purse, an’ ’as the brassy face to pay for
hisself too!”
Sis was obviously crestfallen. “’E shouldn’t ’a
done that,” she said: “that’s dirt.”
“Dirt, easy. When I gets ’ome you cud ’a’
knocked me dead with a feather. A arf quid I
’ad in me purse was gone. His jills ’ad pinched
it.”
Sis perked up; she looked quite radiant. Her
ideal had vindicated himself. “Ah,” she said,
“that’s not so paltry!”
* * * *
“Riverina”: In an estaminet in Steen-
wercfe at certain hours a blind boy pianist
used to play music that brought a reverent
hush over the assembled Diggers. After his
magic had soothed us and refined every
thought, he would burst into song and
bring us back to earth with a thud. That
boy had the most alarming repertoire of
trench ballads of any singer I met out there.
All the gems that the Tommies, Aussies
and Kiwis had perpetrated were stored in
his head; and., probably because of his blind-
ness. his memory had no holes in it. The
singing of those songs was the signal for a
"join-in” by the company, and as Marie and
Louise tripped round with the bock, tin
also occasionally lent a voice, and blithely
informed the boys that they "hadn’t seen the
Kaiser for the h—l of a time.”
Slightly under £4 a week is the screw
which the Vic. Government is offering to
any young lawyer in the service who is tit
to take on the job of conducting prosecutions
in courts of petty sessions. The maximum
of the class is £240; so that only a rise of
£36 can be looked forward to with confidence.
Of course there is the chance of getting valu-
able experience, as the young sawbones does
when he becomes associated with a first-rate
family doctor, but that’s an excuse all
sweaters can put forward.

EVERY PLACE HAS ITS POINTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Matches are made in Heaven —so declare
Parsons and caterers with fervor rare.
But, desolate amidst electric light,
I often wonder if we’ll find them there.
How awkward if the angels should forget
To have such needful trifles ready set!
Whereas in Hell, now, one would never lack
The wherewithal to light a cigarette.
VICTORIA.

Acting Minister for Defence Russell re-
ports that the famous cargo steamer River
Clyde, from which the troops landed while
she was beached under heavy fire at “V”
beach, Gallipoli, will have been salved and
despatched to England about the time this
edition appears. The distinctive features
of her transport equipment will be retained
and she will reach home with the high
bridge, derricks and gangways which made
her a conspicuous figure at the Landing.
Some years ago the River Clyde loaded
wheat in Australia, and if she rolls this
way again there’ll be a Digger or two who’ll
want a day off.
* * * *
“Peg-Leg”: Asked a big member of the
N. S. Wales Alliance about the million
pounds to be spent in forcing prohibition on
Australia. He laughed at me, and said
that it would be a simple matter to send
the place dry if they had that much, but the
entire fighting fund of the Prohibitionists in
the State was less than £lOOO. Their
only chance was if some big commercial con-
cern backed the movement for its own selfish
ends. The people who put up the money to
send U.S.A. dry were mainly manufacturers
of cordials and confectionery, with the Ilearst
papers to assist them ; but there are no simi-
lar concerns big enough to swing the pendu-
lum that way here.
* * * *
“A.R.”: It happened just before the
armistice. At one of the bases in France
there were three ribald Australians among a
big batch of Tommies. The corporal who
took charge addressed the men: “As the
Camp Commandant will be inspecting this
draft, when I call the roll answer smartly
to your name, saying ‘Here, Corporal.’
Understand?” Then the Commandant
arrived and the roll-call commenced. One
after another the Tommies sprang to atten-
tion and answered “Here, Corporal.” The
first Australian name came in stentorian
tones, “Driver Yarra!” The Aussie woke
from a daydream of lost beers: “Here!”
“Here what?” roared the Commandant in
his court-martial voice. “Here we are
again, hooray!” replied the cheerful repro-
bate.

“Silas”: Dennie, the younger son of the
family, had been away from the dairy-farm
on his first trip to Melbourne. He came
back displaying no enthusiasm. The elder
brother he had been staying with had broken
away from cow years before and become a
confirmed suburbanite, and Dennie had been
entirely out of his element. “Aw, I dunno,”
he said, “I don’t care fer
■ Melbin. An’ Jim he’s
too stuck up fer me. Too
stuck up altogether.
’Struth, he drives a
pramberlater!”

“Yal”: The average person (and it is sur-
prising how many are average persons) re-
gards an A.I.F. officer as a sort of Croesus.
In England this conception is general; yet
really the Tommy officer is much better off
than the Australian who looks like
a millionaire because he is free
with his money and hates bargain-
ing. The Aussie officer’s pay is
at a consolidated rate of 17s. 6d.
per diem plus 3s. 6d. field allow-
ance for 2nd-lieutenant and lieuten-
ant of all arms, whilst the Tommy
officer gets a specific rate of pay
per diem according to
the branch of the service
he adorns. On top of his
pay there are various
allowances —field, mess,
kit, fuel, light, quarters,
corps, command, separa-
tion, sustenance, special-
ist, instructional, or
staff; and they mount up.
A 2nd-lieutenant of
British infantry rising
from the ranks gets 2s.
per diem extra plus £5O
kit allowance; if married
with two children he
draws on active service
about 265. per day, be-
sides wound gratuities or
blood money, wound pen-
sions and a service gra-
tuity, the last named
amounting to 124 days’
pay for the first year and
62 days’ pay for every
additional year on ser-
vice. A lieutenant of English Engineers
averages about Bs. per day more than the
Australian lieut.—and more still if he is
in the army of occupation.

“Dial Sight”: Billjim has no superstitious
fear in his make-up. A well-soaked unit on
the way to the trenches halted alongside a
French farm for the night. Cover was
scanty, so two. Diggers, spotting a roofed-
in conveyance in an adjacent yard, crawled
in and spent a warm and comforting night.
The luxurious pair was even provided with
glass sides to the sleeping car, to gaze out
on the sodden landscapes. It was the village
hearse 1

“Sarum” : My own considerable experience
of the English shopkeeper is that the war
or something has turned him into a bowel-
less thief; and I found that he was readier
to rook the man who was fighting for him
than anybody else. Take Salisbury, for
example. For four years it has shamelessly
fleeced the A.1.F., until to-day it is one of
the wealthiest, towns in England per capita.
Our mess decided to be reasonably comfort-
able and have some furniture. Shylock of
Salisbury sold us some at a monumental
figure, explaining that when we left he would
repurchase at a price which would leave the
difference a reasonable hiring charge. When
we got our orders to go and approached Shy-
he offered to repurchase at a discount
of 87| per cent—after only three months’
wear!
* * *
By a man who has been in S’Africa:—
Now that the German menace has been removed
from their border—partly through the operations of
a n army that was largely Boer, with a Boer general
in the lead—it is in the invariable order of things
that there should be a recrudescence of the Boer
demand for the restoration of independence. When
the menace of the French armies in Canada was
removed from their border it took the United States
folk just 13 years to decide that they could do with-
out Britain. The awkward fact is that Britain was
the last country which wiped out a republic—two
republics, in fact—to swell the dimensions of a
monarchy. One of the features of South African
society not polite, evening-dress Government House
society, nor yet society in general at Capetown and
Durban, but of people near the old border land—is
that the Englishman hasn’t forgiven, and isn’t likely
to. He won in the end, but it was a fight of years
between the biggest empire on earth and an isolated
inland people who looked like a grain of dust, and
the Boers, though they were worn down, scored the
majority of the victories. The English feeling is a
mixture of anger, contempt (for the enemy’s alleged
uncouthness) and bitter mortification. It is a sign
of the times that there has been a fierce row in the
Transvaal Legislature over the proposal to use as a
school-book a Boer history entitled “A Century of
Wrong,” and ascribed to General Smuts, though
there is no author’s name on the title-page. School
history is a subject of many rows in annexed coun-
tries. Within my recollection—and I don’t know
that things have altered—it was prohibited in Irish
State schools, though there were scraps in the reading
books about Henry of Navarre, the massacre of St.
Bartholomew, the Spanish Armada, or such-like. Per-
haps the next world-war is getting ready. Korea is
making a new fuss on the plea that the surrender of
its independence to Japan in 1910 was a matter of
force. Certainly the Japanese had occupied the
country; and the empress, who was a troublesome
little brown woman, was murdered in her palace and
her body burnt in the adjacent park (opinion is divided
as to whether she was quite dead when put on the fire) ;
and some members of the futile Cabinet were tor-
tured before the treaty was arrived at. Whether
these doings amounted to compulsion is a matter for
lawyers to decide. Woodrow Wilson is a lawyer,
though I don’t know if he is a good one,
AT THE DIVORCE SHARP’S.
“So you have had no luck in your matri-

“Non, m’sieu, au contraire I have been

“D.F.”: The population of the Pacific
Islands seems to have been decreasing even
before the white man spread turn, new dis-
eases, clothes and other blessings amongst
them. Islands with no one on them when
they were discovered bore evidences of a
former population. Tbe first settlers on
Norfolk Island found stone axes and other
tools there, and thought they
saw signs that banana-plants
had once been set out in rows,
Fanning Island was deserted
when the Americans found it
in 1797, but Captain Maekay
a few years later found old
graves with stone tools and
trinkets buried with the re-
mains. So, too, Pitcairn
Island was empty when the
Bounty mutineers arrived, yet
showed evidences of ancient
settlement. And the stone
statues of Easter Island and
the megalithic monuments and
walls of the Carolines point to
a much larger ancient popula-
tion.
* * *
“H. 0.”: What innocent
lambs these shepherds are!
Archbishop Clarke, who wears
the gaiters of a blameless life
in Melbourne, the other day
made a speech suggesting that
“the sporadic outbreaks of
gun-play” which have lately
occurred were “in a great
measure attributable to the
effect on youthful and impres-
sionable minds of highly-
dramatic picture-plays.” The
“gun Dlay” to which the pre-
late referred is the result of
an organised warfare carried
on for months bv opposing
factions of “underworld” die-
hards, all well known to the
police and most of them old in years
and lawlessness. To anyone familiar
with the history of the vendetta, the
Archbish’s implication that but for
wicked picture-shows the leading characters
might, now be upright burghers is a matter
for mirth. It would be quite as reasonable to
suggest that had his Grace dipped into
“Deadwood Dick” in the days of his youth
he might now be the star garrotter of the
Flying Angels.”

“Pad Australian”: What impressed me
most in the recent march through London of
the 10.000 Guards just home from France
was the weird collection of horses. The
chargers, with a few exceptions, were very
l.gkt for their job. In one case a very
superior person was riding an Arab abolit
polo weight, and the cantankerous palfrv
ivould amble in spite of the rider’s best en-
deavors to persuade him to walk. As to
color; there was almost every shade except
green and sky-blue. I saw one slab-sided,
banjo-headed jazzier of a beautiful ripe-leinbn
color, and several quaint piebalds. Almdst
every animal looked as if its Owner had
started to “rat-tail’* it, altered his mind after
he had worked about 18 inches from the croup,
and let things go at that. The gun teams
were good, useful, active cobby sorts, but they
didn’t seem to have the weight for heavy
going, although they would probably do
fairly fast work on good carrying ground.
The men, too, were a rather uneven lot.
Bjit what a frantic reception they got! It
was just one wild, long-drawn howl of wel-
come from one end of London to the other.
