"Centhrion”» “ILM.*’ (B. 15/5/’i9) eom-
fnents on the bad luck of the Digger who
blisses his boat for Australia becahse lie
takes too long over the last beer. It isn’t
always bad luck. At the close of the first
year of the South African trouble I was one
of a contingent booked for home by the
old Orient, then doing trooping work. 1 had.
with others, applied for a commission, but
things dragged on indefinitely till the morn-
ing came for sailing. Strathcona’s Horse
were also going home that day by the Dur-
ham Castle to Canada, and with them we
came into Capetown from Maitland camp.
We were lined tip in a square to be addressed
by various Ibcal obscurities. It was a lint
day, and just behind us was a pub. I was
right flank man of the rear troop of my lot,
and a pal in Strathcona’s was left-flanker of
his, a yard away. We both looked at the
pub. then at each other. Presently the con-
tingents marched off without us. We had a
pleasant morning, and finally left for the
breakwater in a hansom with half a dozen
friends. There were two boats lying at the
jetty. One had two funnels, the other had
but one. I had travelled on the Orient some
years before, and knew she had twd fhhriffls.
On board the double-chimneved craft we went,
and immediately went to sleep. Next thing
I knew was being awakened by the Canuck.
Said he: “Say, this is th’ Gardam Durham
Castle! Yewer boat’s pulled out an hour
ago.” I watched the distant Orient, whose two
funnels had been changed for one since I had
seen her last, go out past Robben Island. Then

AN IDYLL OF JUNE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Plus tea and toast I take my ease
Draining sheer comfort to the lees
Where gas and firelight illume
And warm the cushioned, curtained room,
A well-worn volume on my knees.
Though showers lace the mournful breeze
That howls, 1 neither sigh nor sneeze;
Grey winter’s no such dreadful doom,
Plus tea and toast.
And much I love, in hours like these,
To read grim tales of polar seas;
Heroes, who dare a glacial tomb,
Piercing the blizzard’s icy gloom;
With them I strive and starve and freeze—
Plus tea and toast.
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

‘ The Hump”: Let me commend as a job
for the ex-Crown Prince mange-dressing camels.
Every time the accursed business had to be done I
swore that when I got back to Australia I’d shoot
on sight the man who cajoled me from the infantry
to the camel corps. After treating the ailing beast
it. took about a fortnight before you stopped tasting
the disinfectant with your meals, and by that time
the patient had begun to moult again. I recollect
one man from Glebe having a rough time with a par-
ticularly nasty beast, and during the performance he
got huffy with his officer. “Look here,” said the cap-
tain, “I won't crime you tor that, but I’ll kick your
behind.” The chap threw down his swab and turned
on the officer. “Right-oh,” he said ; “you kick first;
then I’ll kick you, and the loser mange-dresses this
camel.” There was no court-martial.
“Entomos”: The death's-head moth (B.
6/2/19) is not Australian. It has evidently been
introduced from the Cold Country by an enthusiastic
bug-man, and the capture of a specimen in N. S.
Wales is certainly interesting. Re the big grey
moth from dead timber, let me correct “Veronal.”
The caterpillars exist in living trees, but, in the
event of the wood drying, have the power, from
quite early youth, of completing their cycle and
becoming moths. Most juvenile caterpillars die when
their food supply fails, but Nurse Nature has arranged
that these usually long-lived things from our euca-
lyptus, wattle and other trees need not then pass
out. The abos. may eat these moths. A sin. one
is rather a pygmy, there being many much larger,
four species I know measuring from 9 to 9$ inches,
with a body as thick as an office ruler. But the
corpulent and juicy caterpillars are much more to
their taste, and, a bit browned on the coals, are
included in their menu as frequentLr as possible.
Many a white, schooled in bush secrets by his dark
brethren, has sampled these wrigglers and pro-
nounced them toothsome.
* * * *
“Gouger”: North of Cairns (Q.) there
grows a variety of tree-fern which sprouts forth a
crop of seeds each nearly the size of a billiard-ball.
The blacks pound them up and put the flour to soak
in running water for 24 hours. This takes out
the poison which is contained. The flour is then
dried and “beggars on the coals” are made out of
it. The only trouble with the fern is that when
the green fronds are eaten by cattle the banquet is
promptly followed by rickets. Cattle seemingly
healthy and in good condition collapse and have
to be shot. There are a lot of hearty greetings
from bank managers awaiting the man who can find
a sure cure for this ricket disase.
* * * *
“Dear Fellow”: The terms “clapmatch”
and “wig” were well enough understood in Hobart
Town a century or more ago, but now the first is
clean forgotten and the second survives only in
relation to horse- and other hair. A “clapmatch”
was a female seal, a “wig” the old male, and a
“pup ’ the young seal. No one seems to know why
the old bull-seal was called a “wig,” but “clap-
match” has a curious history. It is derived from the
Dutch “klapmuts,” a kind of hood worn by sailors,
and was first applied to a species of seal which had
a sort of gristly protuberance, supposed to resemble
a hood, on its nose. Clayton, writing in 1775, gives
clapmatch ’ as the name of the seal figured in
Anson’s “Voyage” under the name of the sea-lion In
bis day the word h’ad evidently no special application
to the females, but later it came to be confined to
them. “A ihip in sight is anything but good news
for the pups, wigs and clapmatches,” says a writer
in the old Sydney Gazette, referring to the “seal-
ing islands” of the far south.

“Pick : Though it came iu time to be
too late to save Norman Lindsay’s pet, the koala, the
Native Animals’ Protection Act has apparently given
the N.S.W. opossum another lease of life. That interest-
ing little Australian’s guttural argument with the
other fellow across Gumtree-street may once again be
a feature of the wilds. The pity is that in several
districts of N. S. Wales the restrictions against
possum- and kangaroo-killing are about to expire.
OUR VERSACULAR.
THE PARSON'S WIFE: "Well, how are things going, Mrs. Cowdry?”
THE COW-COCKY'S: “So good at all, mum. The milk's goin' sour on me, and two pigs
died on me; and note, when eggs is worth a bit, the fowls have all stopped laying
on me.”
Unless at once re-enacted, the very slowly-recovering
furry tribes will soon be again depleted. By the
way, why is it that Sydney skin-buying firms per-
sistently quote prices for the skins of animals that
come under the Animals’ Protection law? Have
they got a pull with the “National” Government?
“Mellisk Reef”: Faivo was a hefty
Malaita boy who was having a bad bout with bron-
chitis. He was “close-up finish.” Faivo was the
son of a chief, which made him more valuable in the
eyes of his cobbers, who formed our boat’s-crew. I
HIS SOFT HEART EDS ESS.
THE SPONGER: "Yer wouldn't stand there an' see me die o' thirst, would yer, mate?”
THE DRINKER: “No fear! I’d shut my eyes first!”
was doing the best I could for him, when the spokes-
man of the boys said: “This feller medicine you
g'ivim boy ’e all right along white man; or s’pose
one feller debil-debil, ’e stop; but boy ’e gotim
two feller debil-debil. ’E die along night. S’pose
boy ’e die, father b’long him makeim me feller pay
plenty money—twenty pounds, I tink. He feller no
gotim money. By-’m-bye five other feller boy he
die; altogether ’e gotim debil-debil alleesame. More
better you sendim some feller boy back along
Malaita, makim present along debil-debil [i.e.,
sacrifice pigs], or altogether boy ’e die.” I agreed
to this; but before daylight Faivo went west.
Before doing so, however, he stood up and thanked
us for what we had done for him.
* * * *
“Azac”: Reported that a flying lizard has
been “discovered” by Mrs. Adam Black of Pajingo
station (N.Q.). If this reptile turns out to be a
new discovery it will be the third known flying
lizard in the world. The lady says that she has, on
various occasions, observed the stranger parachuting
down from high trees to the ground. Its parachute
is a frill around its neck, which folds up neatly when
the flying stunt is not in progress.

* * * *
“Bill R.”: Let me advise Abo. readers
not to place dependence on “Toni's” theory (B.
8/5/ 19) that only non-venomous snakes climb trees.
On Oak Range, Monaro (N.S.W.), I once helped to
push a “tiger” out of a ’possum spout 30ft. from the
ground. And about the biggest black snake I ever
interviewed was one that lobbed among a company
of juvenile cherry-pickers at Parkesbourne. He had
been aloft lying in wait for parrakeets, and he dis-
gorged one when I walloped him with a pitchfork-
handle and great enthusiasm. Then there are the
snakes up the telegraph-poles which the homing
drunk pursues at midnight.
* * *
S. : Here’s another way to prevent a
new' pipe from burning or cracking. Sioke it one-
thin! full and smoke; then half-full and smoke;
next completely full. I have tried this with a score
of pipes and have never had one crack or burn. It
is a good idea also to let water run through a new
pipe before smoking, of course seeing that it is
thoioughly dry again before the tobacco is put in.
* * * *
“Jimsey”: Near Yawrie (N.S.W. South
Coast) I recently came across a rocking-stone. It
is about 9ft. high, of a rough spherical shape and
\\ou d weigh about six tons. The slightest push
is sufficient to start it swinging. It will move about
Sin. in any direction, but it would take a charge of
dynamite to rock it any further. The stone is
resting on another pebble and is balanced on its end,
which is curved and about five inches across. Its
address is about 11 miles from Yawrie, in the moun-
tains. I have never heard of any other in Australia.

THE OLD BUSH NIGHTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sit in a room that is twelve-by-twelve,
And the ceiling painted white;
On a bentwood chair in the straining glare
Of the pale electric-light.
And my thoughts go back by a winding track
To the nights I used to know,
W hen our arms were strong and our hearts were
strong
And our blood was all aglow.
When we never looked for sorrow
That might come along to-morrow,
And we didn’t seem to worry where the money
used to go.
A car sweeps by on the long white road
M ith a devil-may-care and scorch,
And its headlights play on the broad roadway
As I look through the open porch.
I can hear a train on the distant plain
And pick out a hundred lights;
And my thoughts go back by the winding track
As I think of the olden nights.
When the slush-light flickered dimly,
And we faced our troubles grimly,
And we battled for the union and the things we
called our rights.
There were nights we spent in the haunted camps,
\\ here the restless spirits bide;
Where the phantoms stalk and the dead men walk,
And the headless horsemen ride,
here the night-birds cry as the coach goes by,
And the horses plunge in fright;
And the headlights gleam on the plunging team
In the blackness of the night.
But they leave before the grey light
Lets you know that it is daylight,
And you laugh at midnight fancies when the sun
is shining bright.
And there were nights beside the Gulf
In the overlanding days,
Where rovers come and rovers go,
But the man is lost that stays.
here the cattle ring and the drovers sing
Through the watches in the night;
And the wagtails peep at the men asleep
Beyond the blazing light.
Oh! the nights of ninety-seven
When we camped right under Heaven,
When the wanderlust was on us and the Star of
Hope was bright!
There was a night when we rode alone
Where the plains were wild and wide,
And we travelled west on a bushman’s quest,
And the sheep-dog jogged beside. ;
And the squatter slept as we slowly crept i
With an eye for his fattest sheep,
Till we found a mob and did our job
Where the restless dingoes creep.
Ere the eastern sky was ruddy
We were satisfied—and bloody—
As we burnt the skin and offal and the parts that
wouldn’t keep.
And there was a time when the lure of gold
Had entered the hearts of men,
And a man and wife in the spring of life
Went off to the diggings then.
And one dark night a light shone bright
On the flap of their tilted cart,
And a child was born in the early morn,
And gladdened the miner’s heart.
And their spirits didn’t suffer
When they bottomed on a duffer—
And the man that writes the story was the baby
in the cart.
Arid he sits in a room that is twelve-by-twelve
That lie hates with a bitter hate
When his thoughts go back by the old bush track
And many an old bush gate
In the tranquil hush of the moonlit bush
To the call of the open land,
Bv hill and plain and shine and rain,
And the men of the open hand—
Out beyond the Never Never,
Where a mateship lasts for ever,
And the bush is big and peaceful, and’the bush if
always grand.
N.S.W.
JIM GRAHAME.

* * * *
“Entomos”: If “Cecil D.” (B. 13/3/T9)
continues to interest himself in insects, wasps especi-
aHy, he will observe much that is queer. Reference
to “Agricola’s” excellent note (B. 27/2/’l9) will
help him greatly. The yellow-antennicd blackish chap
is a wasp, with various relatives much like him—or
her, really - all possessing a penchant for spiders,
which they sting into quiescence. The paralyser then
elects to straddle the paralysed, but, the legs being
in the way, she bites them off, or most of them, then
proceeds homeward with her victim, which is to
serve as food for her' offspring. She has earlier
located a little nook, a keyhole being much fancied
(or a roundish mud nest on a twig, with several
comfy cells for her spiders, may have been prepared),
into which the not-dead-but-reposeful prey is bun-
dled, an egg laid on it, and the hole then sealed up
with clay. In time, a wasp, or several, according to
the size of the nest and number of spider-cells, will
emerge. One wasp will bite off all the eight’ legs,
another will consider six sufficient, but “Cecil” may-
be jumping to conclusions—and those erroneous—in
stating that the wasp sucks the juices exuding from
the leg stump. Different colonies, different insects;
but practically everywhere the wasps of solitary
habit embalm spiders and caterpillars, etc., to serve
as food for their young. I don’t think the species
regales herself upon the spider’s juices.
* * * *
E.A.\. ; Grafton (N.S.W.) Pastures
Protection Board has found a use for the fox, and
has stopped paying a bonus for his scalp. Its opinion
is that the red fellow is the greatest natural enemy
of the rabbit, which is threatening to invade the
North Coast, and that he will be more effective than
netting or poison in holding bunny in check. The
Board also advises protection of brown and eagle-
hawks, iguanas and carpet-snakes as valuable auxil-
iaries to the fox. After the bunnies are wiped out
it is the intention to send the foxes hack to Eng-
land by the first mailboat.

“Nick Bottom”: Re “Tarwin’s” query (B.
17/4/T9) anent the crab that resides in the back
portion of the mouth of the trevalli. This crus-
tacean is the well-known Legpullus kidstakesi. It
is used by the trevalli as a toothpick.
“Bouragi” : Bulletin mention of the
islands of Boigu and Saibai, which, though close
to the Papuan coast, are included within the boun-
daries of Queensland, recalls an old-standing grievance
which the Papuan Government nurses against Ryan’s
territory. William Macgregor, in particular, was
never tired of voicing it. When S.E. New Guinea
was first taken up, at
Australia’s insistence,
by. the Colonial Office,
Bananaland put in a
claim for all the
available pearling
ground she could get
control of. The claim
was allow'ed by the
C. 0., which hated the
idea of troubling
about the show from the
first, with the result
that the Queensland
boundary runs far
north of its proper
territorial limit; it
almost touches the
Papuan coast in
places. Boigu and Sai-
bai became “sanctu-
ary,” and evildoers
who were “wanted” at
Duru slipped over the
Queensland line and
were safe for the time
being. Macgregor
bumped this arrange-
ment in 1898 to
square matters up
with the Tugeri. These
were a savage people
who lived in Dutch
territory, and yearly
made head-hunting
raids along the coast
of British N.G., leav-
ing nothing but devas-
tation behind them.
Macgregor caught them
with their vast fleets
of canoes while they
were busy cleaning
up Boigu and other
islands inside the
Queensland boundary,
but as the raiders
dodged away on the
Papuan side, where
the Merrie England could not follow in the
shallow water, he had to drive them ashore,
leaving the Tugeri a 300-mile walk home
through an awful sago swamp. Later he
cut off a large body of the Tugeri on land,
and dealt so reverently, etc., with them that
there were no more raids by the cannibals.
One of the rarest medals in existence was issued for
this expedition by the Colonial Office. Six were sent
out by the C. 0., but only three of them w’ere dis-
tributed by the B.N.G. Administration—one to the
white commandant of the Armed Native Constabu-
lary and tw'o to native members of the force.
“Azac”: Re the suggestion (B. l/5/’l9)
that there should be established a travelling bird-
school in charge of a lecturing naturalist to spread
the knowledge of bird uses and habits, “E.S.S.” is
hereby invited to consider this. Recently the N. S.
Wales Department of Agriculture published a useful
book on the utility (and otherwise) of Australian
birds, and although its publication was advertised
far and wide, also the fact that it could be had for
the asking (post free), under 100 copies have been
distributed! When it is considered that out of
that number many curious city folk obtained copies,
and also that a few dozen were sent to the Depart-
ment of Public Health, one can easily gather the
amount of interest taken in this publication by the
people whom it really did concern—the farmers.
There’s only one way to impart knowledge of bird-
ology to the man on the land, and that is by making
it a compulsory subject in the schools. The coming
generation will then be sure to have a fairly sound
acquaintance with their bird friends and foes. The
present working generation can be abandoned as
hopeless.

“Barber”: The best way to prevent a pipe
from burning or cracking is to fill the bowl with hot
water and leave it stand over-night. Empty and
allow to thoroughly dry. If it cracks after that, pre-
sent it to a South Seas missionary society, which
will swap it to a heathen for half a year’s crop of
copra.
ti * *
H.B. : This is the real Mackay for pre-
venting a pipe cracking or burning. Rub the
inside of the bowl with black-lead or stove-polish.
You can get black-lead at any parsonage. The holy
man uses it to polish the top-hat which he sends
round.

“Melos”: The anvil-bird is not the only
chook that can imitate the village blacksmith.
Some of the honey-eaters, particularly the mock re-
gent and the white-fronted, have a metallic voice
which suggests the ring of iron. The dragoon uses
a block of stone whereon to hammer the stuffing out
of snails. If anything interferes with his business,
or the shellbacks happen to be tough, this fellow’s
language is kin to what you hear in a smith’s shop
when Bill’s moke refuses to stand on three legs, or
kicks on the kneecap the party who goes every Sun-
day to the church to hear his daughter twitter in the
choir.

“Sandfly”: I don’t want to go any further
than this. “Frank R.’s” statement (B. 27/2/’l9)
that Lee and Sons, of Proserpine (Q.), made
a crocodile leg it 12 miles is as false as
Japanese matches. I know the whole Lee family,
and, besides having been a next-door cocky to them
until quite recently, used to accompany them in
their fishing and hunting jaunts, and
I have never known them to attempt
to drove a catch overland. I have
seen every croc, they have exhibited
in the Proserpine township, and can
prove tha,t each one was hauled
either on a truck over the tram-line
or by horse-waggon. Dad Lee, the
noted croc catcher of N.Q., is a great
leg-puller. His weird stories of las-
sooing and handling - reptiles would
make a baldheaded man’s hair grow.
I have been amongst crocodiles in
the vicinity of Proserpine and on
the northern rivers of N.Q. —have
caught them alive, hunted for their
eggs and studied their habits gener-
ally—for years, arid never have I
seen or heard of them straying more
than 100 yards from the water’s-
edge. One has to do a good deal of
coaxing to get them away from the
creeks or rivers. It is done only
by first tying a bait near the water,
then increasing the distance from
the bank each day until the lizard
comes far enough to be trapped.
There are exceptions where croco-
diles have been seen miles away from
their usual haunts, but this is when flooded
ereeks or rivers have overflowed their banks and
on falling to their normal have left the saurians
stranded. One case of this kind was observed in the

“Jim Carew”: There is an interesting
legend attached to the big bare cliff near Peats
Ferry, Havvkesbury River (N.S.W.). A tribe of
Binghis had offended tlieir guardian spirit, and had
been foibidden, as a punishment; ever to camp for
more than one night at any place. Their debil-
debil followed them round to see that he was
obeyed, hunting them from place to place and
giving them no rest. Their ill-fame spread, and no
other tribe would have anything to do with them
Once they managed to evade their enemy, and had
been camped for nearly a month under a large over-
hanging ledge of rock. When the spirit discovered
them he was so angry at finding they had disobeyed
his orders that he caused the ledge containing thou-
sands of tons of rock to fall and blot out the whole
tribe. I’ll vouch for this, anyhow, that there cer-
tainly has been a terrific slide on the spot at some
time or other.

“FY7”: I see that the beautiful brolga
or native companion, Australia’s only crane, is visit-
ing the N. S. Wales North Coast cow country in
great numbers, having been shifted from the west,
no doubt, by the recent extra-dry conditions. Old’
hands reckon the birds haven’t been seen on the coast
foi 40 years. I m told that every yahoo in the
district who can borrow a gun is shooting the
strangers. Why, God knows, for the birds aren’t
good for cow-feed, and are just as unpalatable for
human beings. Also, the graceful creature’s feathers
are no use for a best girl’s Sunday hat, even sup-
posing the girls up that way can spare time from
cow V.A.D.-ing to wear Sunday hats.

at Moawhango, in the same district, which was owned

at country sports, was always attended by a big,
full-blooded buck Maori in Highland costume.
Dunno what clan the tartan represented, but some
of the men from Scotia who settled in Taihape used
to become whuskiously indignant at McHenare
appearing in the petticoat, and threatened to
brain him with a haggis. MacHenare’s troubles!
These prurry bagpipe prokes,” he used to sav, “all
wind!”

TO A STREET ACQUAINTANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Immovable you sit within your cage,
Like some old shiny--overcoated Jew;
Your hook-nosed face, expressionless but sage,
Facing the city side-walk’s frowsy crew.
Are there no thoughts behind those small bright eyes
Which look so preternaturally wise?
Is life to you a mere dull wooden blank,
A question of so many - seeds per day?
Or do you brood on times when, rank on rank,
The snow-white parrot-armies winged their way,
You in their midst, breathing the high free air,
And wild with utter joy at being there?
Do you remember, as you watch the street,
It 3 crashing trams and fleeting drab-clothed forms,
That other life, in which you used to greet
The dawn, the shining plains, the passing storms,
Jbe laden fruit-trees, and the gleaming creeks,
With eager, glad, discordant cries and shrieks.
The Lord forbid that I should think you do!
Life would acquire a lot of added gloom
If one were suddenly unable to
Believe that parrots like a small wire room
(Shut off from light and air and sea and sky)
In which to sit and see free fo'ks pass by.
N.S.W.

The Death Song of Murri
In the Rev. William Ridley’s “Camilroi
and Other Dialects” the author concludes
with a rather mournful “Parting word for
the Race of Murri,” the generic name of
the aboriginals. Herewith I give a literal
translation of what stern old Woorallajee
sang to me—who have been among abo-
rigines since I was seven —as the Death song
of his race; for afar off he saw that time
coming, and above the sound of wind and
waves he heard the tramp of doom, as did
the ancient Norseman
When saddled were the steeds of Fate,
That champ the bit by Asgard gate.
A. Meston.
THE DEATH-SONG OF MURRI.
No more the whir of the bird-like boomerang,
skimming the ground as a swallow or circling round
us like Warrawandi, the spine-tailed swift!
No more the sharp spear piercing the air in
silence to where it is parried by the narrow shield!
No more the clang of the nulla on the sounding
heelamin, the war-note of the Race of Murri!
No more the hand-to-hand combat of the young
warriors in the first of their battles !
No more the quivering of the womera and the
reed spear flashing past as if thrown by the light-
ning 1
No more does Yulu, the porpoise, drive in the
frightened mullet, where the young men surround
them with the tow-row net and offer Yulu his share
on the point of a spear!
No more are the tracks of the naked feet of Murri
seen on the white sands of the shore where the waves
have washed them out for the last time!
Overhead in the night I hear the wild swan call-
ing to his mate, and there is no answer when he
rsks, “Where are the fires and the camps of Murri ?”
The winds make no answer, and the waves have
none. And the old trees ask each other: “Where are
the people who once ascended our tall trunks and
dragged the opossum from his lair?
“Where are the swift feet, that ran the emu and
the kangaroo to the place where the unerring spear
or the nulla ended the long race on the plains or in
the valleys?
“Where are those eagle-eyed men from whose keen
sight no bird or beast could lie concealed, and no
foe could hide his tracks?
“Where are the youths to whom the old men
taught the laws of the tribe, and the secrets of
Biamee, in the Bora circles of the Race of Murri?
I sit on the great rock that overlooks the home of
;he waterfall, and see the sun go down in the West
towards the abode of Biamee, and I stand and call to
him to bring back my people when he returns from
his sleep in the morning.
The moon looks down at night and is silent when
I call, and only Echo answers from the lonely caves.
For the full moon beholds no more the white and
yellow ranks of the warriors in the corroboree, the
rising and falling arms and the quivering muscles,
and the earth feels no more the stamp of many
feet, and the silent birds hear not the song of
the women, and the loud beat on the hollow thighs,
or the chorus of the old men and the clash of the
resounding boomerangs.
But the Stars, which are the eyes of Biamee, look
down, and a voice calls from the clouds: “Never
more shall you see the Race of Murri among the
hills and on the plains they have left for ever!
“They are with Biamee, where the white man
troubles them no more; where their wrongs shall be
forgotten until the day of the white man’s own doom
shall arrive.
“You, too, 0 Woorallajee, shall come to Biamee
and mourn no more for your lost race!’’
The thunder shakes the mountains, and the red
lightning rends the rocks asunder, but no ear of
Murri shall listen to those sounds again.
When I walk among the trees to hear the voices
of the birds they are all silent.
Even at night the mournful dingo is mute, and no
wailing whistle comes from the sad curlew.
By the seashore the waves are silent when I pass,
and even the voices of all the winds are heard no
more until I am gone.
All things, save the white man, mourn for the Race
of Murri.
But the day of the white man’s own darkness shall
come, and none shall mourn for him.
Then the birds and the trees shall rejoice, for he
gave them to death and to the fire.
He sent the Race of Murri to the grave!
Our women and children fled in terror from the
sight of his face, for he had the snake eyes of
Ahvandi the Evil Spirit, and the look of red Cal-
moora, who ate our children in the white caves of
Mirramooma I
The curse of the Race of Murri has gone to
Biamee, the maker of all things, and Biamee, the
friend of Murri, will remember the white man in
the Day of Doom!
A Satchel of Books
What Not, by Rose Macaulay (Constable, London).
This satire on the multiplication of Ministries has a
history. It was bound and ready for distribution
in Nov., 1918, when it was found that “a slight
alteration in the text was essential to safeguard it
against one of the laws of the realm.” So it had
to be unbound and bowdlerised. The story mostly con-
cerns the Minister for Brains and the work of his office.
The often very clever satire will be best appreci-
ated by those who know something of the inner work-
ing of the innumerable new “Ministries” that Lloyd
George created, partly to do necessary public work,
but often, it seemed, to provide for politicians who
might otherwise have made themselves a nuisance.
And those who clon’t know anything of the inner work-
ings of the new Ministries can get a very interesting
lesson from these 250 pages.
Concentration and Character Building, by Dorothy
Grenside, teaches how to acquire “creative thought,”
“concentrative thought,” “consecutive thought,” “re-
ceptivity of thought,” and generally to build up the
muscles of the mind. Whether a course of it
would make, say, Holman, who is at least a consecu-
tive elocutionist if not a consecutive thinker, see
that £819,902 is rather much to add to the annual
interest bill of a small State in a single year is
another matter. (G. Bell and Sons, London.)
The Pillar of Fire, by H. C. Bailey (Methuen,
London). A story of the days of Lord Palmerston.
Garibaldi, Louis Napoleon and the Italian revolution
come into the yarn; and the heroine makes her
entrance by murdering an Austrian spy. In spite
of that, she marries the Prime Minister’s secretary.
Exciting in a rather melodramatic way.
Hearts Courageo-us, by John Oxenham (Methuen,
London). Advice in prosy and undistinguished
verse to “Look Beyond,” “Follow Me” and do other
print-worn things. There are lots of patriotic
piayers and the like; but the verse is commonplace
in thought and manner. Still, there is an occa-
sional flash, but never one bright enough to redeem
the book.
Special Performances, by W. Pett Ridge (Methuen,
London). Rattling good London yarns with plenty
of feeling and plenty of humor. About the best
thing m the book is “Photographic Proofs” ; and the
war stories are all good. Of course, there is the
note of exaggeration, but that’s in the game A
fine collection.

October 1, 1864) is a combination of four
newspapers-—The Examiner, The Yeoman,
lhe Weekly Argus and Bell’s Life in
Victoria. The first three were established
Edward Wilson. The fourth, acquired
in 1868, was founded in the mid-’fifties, and
in the early ’sixties the Levey brothers
(George, lias just gone west at 85) had in-
terests in it W. M. Akhurst did the
theatres; Graham Garrick the P.R. ; William
Levey (editor) the turf intelligence.

“0.K.”; Whereas Melbourne has never
known a Sunday newspaper, and is debarred
of the luxury bv a 30-year-old Act of Parlia-
ment, sinful Sydney started its journalism
with a Sunday rag. The first issues of the
Sidney Gazette wer-e published on Satur-
day, but in the fourth week it came out on
the Sabbath and continued to do so for some
years. There were no wowsers in Australia
at that time. But in Hobart Town, where
e very official was. qn immoral law unto him-
self, the authorities were whales for Sabba-
tarianism as regards other fellows. His-
torian Bonwiek quotes a Hobart Town mili-
tary order of February, 1810;
The detachment will attend Divine service at the
usual hour, during the performance of which the
patrole will apprehend all persons found moving
house tOWn ’ th6y WUI be taken t 0 the 8 '
In 1813 the unsober Governor ruled that all
persons neglecting to send their servants to
church should be deprived of assigned ser-
vants. Four years later a man was fined a
pound for driving a bullock-cart through the
streets on a Sunday, all such practices being:
solemnly described as “Profanations of the
s ,.Jr ly - vulgarly and improperly called
Saibath-breakmg.” Sales of wives at auc-
tion were permitted, but the rulers of the
laud insisted on Sunday observance for the
masses. Needless to say, journalism in Van
Diemens Land didn’t begin with a Sunday
newspaper. And it was from there, and not
trom N. S. Wales, that Melbourne got its
first settlers. The spirit of compulsory Sab-
batarianism came up the Yarra with, Fawk-
ner and Batman, and settled on the site of
that Parliament House where the late H«nry
\\ rixon, long years afterwards, carried his
rnti-Snnday paper Bill.

“A.R.” : One of the most hopeful signs in
Australia is the gradual stoppage of waste;
which is to say, the gradual using up of by-
products. Even in the cow industry, which
was for so long carried on under conditions
that killed intelligence in those connected
with it, great improvements are in progress.
In Western Vic. for some time past separ-
ated milk has been run through another pro-
cess and casein extracted. This by-product
is disposed of in bulk to manufacturers of
patent foods, or shipped to America to be
made into collar-studs and celluloid shirt-
cuffs. Now it is proposed to put the whey
left after the cream and casein have been
extracted through yet another process to win
the milk-sugar from it. What remains of
the original cow-juice goes back to fatten up
Blossom’s offspring.

“Leonardo” : After three days at Yanko I
came to the conclusion that most of the
settlers have had too much done for them.
I saw one patch of pumpkins, one acre in
extent, and what a crop! To-day this fruit
of the old Hawkesbury is fetching £2O per
ton. This very fact shows that Yanko
settlers don’t use their heads. When every
other farming centre is becoming a dust-
heap, that is Yanko’s chance. Even last
year, when pumpkins grew abundantly, they
were worth £6 10s. a ton. Yanko can grow
rock-melons second to none in the world; yet
I saw only two vines. Any season rock-
melons such as Yanko can produce are worth
anything from 12s. to 18s. per dozen. I
didn’t see enough carrots or p'arsnips or leeks
to make a decent pot of soup. The curse of
the settlement is the single-crop system—the
thing that kept hundreds of the okl Cumber-
land fruitgrowers on the brink of poverty all
their lives. But they, poor devils! had to
trust to luck and the weather. They got Id.
per lb. for their grapes; Yanko settlers aver-
age sd. They got 2s. 6d. per case for
oranges and lemons; Yanko growers get any-
thing up to 40s. The very fact that they
get only 14in. of rain a year is all in their
favor. They have the water and the sun-
shine co-operating to produce whatever is
desired. I will say this: that wherever I
saw a young farmer (they were few and far
between) I saw a prosperous mixed-farm —a
few pigs, fowls, a cow or two, and a decent
kitchen- and flower-gardc-n. But there are
too many older men with a grievance on the
area.

“C. 8.” : Linseed could be grown in Aus-
tralia in vast quantities; yet we annually
import thousands of tons of seed, oil and
fibre grown in India by coolie labor. In
1917-18 the Argentine seeded 3,233,609
acres; two years before India had 2,683,000
acres, the United States in 1917 cultivated
1,809,000 and Canada annually sows about
800,000. In Australia the plant is practi-
cally uncultivated. Last year the two
largest mills in the Commonwealth were idle
for some time because the Indian Govern-
ment had placed an embargo on the exporta-
tion of the seed. There is a duty of Bd. a
gallon on oil to stimulate the local manu-
facturer, but the producer of the raw goods
gets no Protection. As it is improbable
that Russia, Courland and Roumania will
resume production on the pre-war scale for
years, an adequate duty might enable the
Australian to not only supply its own re-
quirements but also work up a profitable
export trade. In 1916 Britain alone im-
ported flax, linseed and linseed-meal w T orth
£13,000,000.

BREAD, AND THE CIRCUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To prove its wholesomeness, N. S. Dales Premier
Holman had a sample of bread baked. The chemi-
cal analysis yields the following results:—
Moisture 43.2 p . c .
Ash . . . . . . . . . . i
Ash, insoluble in hydrochloric acid .02 ”
Acidity, expressed in cubic centi-
metres of decinormal sodium
hydroxide . . . . .. 1.9
These results are in accordance with regulation 20
(3) issued under the provisions of the Pure Pood
Act of 1908.
I am an artless swagman of the wilds;
I scorn the city and its gross devices;
My needs are simple as a little child’s—
A crust of bread with lots of beer suffices.
I like my damper to retain a dash
Of gravel, to prevent its being flaccid,
With point-nought-two of good tobacco ash
Insoluble in hydrochloric acid.
The right acidity must be supplied,
Expressed in simple cubic, centimetres
Of decinormal sodium hydroxide—
A one-point-nine suits scientific eaters
Sufficient moisture is essential, too;
A drought I loathe, and drynesa causes colic.
Say, cent.-per-cent, of something wet will do
(The wetness must, of course, he alcoholic).
The simple life for me, with none to chide,
Unvexed by sordid Pure Food legislation;
Matilda for my mistress, true and tried,
Free from political sophistication!
N.S.W.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

Mentioning the death of A. G. Ilotli, The
Bulletin said that he had missed getting
into the Baptist ministry. As a matter of
fact, he was in the ministry for some yeais,
but retired.
“D.F.”: Jervis Bay, the Federal port,
came into notice as a harbor as eaily as
1791 The Matilda, which came as a trans-
port'in the “Third Fleet,” took a run down
south to look for seals, the first attempt at
sealing by a vessel sailing out of Sydney.
She did not see any, but ran into a gale,
and put into Jervis Bay to stop a leak, lne
captain reported that there was exceedir g y
good anchorage and “room for the large
ships to work in or out with great safety.
Of the Forrest Ministries which ran
Westralia prior to Federation the only repre-
sentatives still in Parliament are Edward
Wittenoom and Henry Lefroy. The Leake
Ministry, which followed Forrests, has two
left, Kingsmill and Holmes. The James
Cabinet, which was the next to take control,
has a sole survivor, Gardiner. Ihe first
Labor Government, under Daglish, which
took control from James's party in IJU4,
has a couple of members still in the House,
Taylor and Holman.
“Cazna.”: The Repatriation Department
has turned down the hand-weaving of
“Anzac” tweeds as an occupation for. re-
turned soldiers on the ground that it is a
“dead-end” job. Quite right. The stuff
they were turning out in Melbourne wears
so well that it threatened, before the Depart-
ment kindly took a hand, to ruin its own
market by lasting too long. I know because
I have a suit of it, and no matter how much
knocking about it gets it won’t even start
to go to pieces. What’s going to happen to
the price of shoddy if the making of that
sort of stuff is encouraged? It has not
even the merit of being excessively high-
priced.
“U.P.” : S. Aus. rabbits are in for a bad
time, notwithstanding that the drought has
broken and feed and water are plentiful.
CO2 gas having been found effective in the
destruction of weevil in wheat, a machine
has been made for the purpose of conferring
the same favor on bunny. The gas quickly
finds it way through the largest burrows, and
means certain death to all inmates; further,
after the gas has been forced into the
burrows no attempt -is made to reopen
them. The process is very economical. The
cost of the gas is the price of the firewood
used —about 2cwt. a day for a machine capa-
ble of gassing 20 burrows —plus petrol to
work a 1-li.p. engine, a horse to pull the
machine around the paddock and a man to
read the funeral service.
“Club Bore” : Melbourne is a city of in-
appropriately named clubs. The Melbourne
possesses the smallest proportion of Mel-
bourne members, being the Mecca of the
woolly Western District and the Aus-
tralian address of the absentee land-
lord. The Yorick is a meeting place
for neither jesters nor actors. The Green-
room’s actors are easilv outnumbered by
persons who would not know a stick
of No. 2 from an eyebrow pencil. The
Athenaeum relies for its membership chiefly
upon the ancient lights of law, commerce
and industry. And opposite the Athenaeum
stands the exclusive Bohemian, the lair of
burghers of heavy financial tonnage, where
the odor of garlic would be as out of place
as a dog fight in a cathedral.
Allen Taylor’s timber co. has sold its fleet
of little boats to the North Coast S. N. Co.,
and presumably that vigorous concern will
now push harder into the coastal timber trade.
It is one of the oldest in N. S. Wales.
Cedar-cutters were working on the Shoal-
haven at least as early as 1812, seven years
after the first exploration of the river by
Lieutenant Kent. In 1812 six schooners —
the Geordy, the Mary Anne, the Endeavor,
the William and Anne, the Windsor and the
Cumberland —were bringing logs to Sydney.
Early in 3813 the Whale, the Mary and
Sally and the Speedwell went into the same
trade, and in the first three months of the
year' 272 tons of cedar were landed at
Sydney. Later in the year sawpits must
have been put in at the Shoalhaven, for
cedar then appears in the returns not as
so many logs but as so many feet.
“E. J.H.”: More than once I have been
present at the wine-tasting at the Sydney
It.A.S. Ground. There I have sampled
wines, (as I have done in Britain) and found
them Very good. But when I ordered wines
bearing a similar label in Sydney I did not
get anything like the Show liquors. This sort
of thing does not encourage a man tp take
to wine in place of whisky. One of the great-
est mistakes, to my mind, is the capping of
bottles with lead capsules instead of sealing-
wax. In France, for instance, if mine host
suggests “a bottle of Cambertin with the
yellow seal,” you know what you are going
to get. Here, lead capsules are slipped
over the cork, not even making the bottle
airtight in case the plug gives out. I was
in a hotel recently and asked for a certain
whisky. The girl pulled the cork, assuring
me that it was a new bottle and I would
get a whisky “true to label.” I asked to
look at the cork, and the one she pulled out
of the bottle and handed me bore the name,
burnt into it, of a well-known firm of French
brandy-sellers! The same thing can be and
no doubt is done with our wine.
“Smith”: It was my first time in the line
and Fritz was lobbing them over pretty
thick. Near me was a thin post, upright
in the ground, on top of which a piece of
silk gave us the office as to the way of the
wind in case of gas. A rat, which evi-
dently had the wind up, climbed the post,
and every time a shell exploded he jumped
about a foot into the air, landing on top
of the post again—the finest acrobatic act
I have ever seen. When things quietened
down a Digger murdered our friend; but
who could resist such a shot? Another
time I sneaked down to cooky to cadge a
drink and nearly dropped dead on finding
a platoon of rats lined up, with old Dad
Rat dishing out our rations. The only way
we could keep our tucker in that part of the
line was to stow it in our mess-tins. Rat-
shooting was permitted, and many a rat got
something that had been bought specially for
Fritzie.

DELCO-UGHT IS SIMPLE.
DELCO-LIGHT consists of an engine, generator,
switchboard and set of batteries. The engine
and generator are direct connected, which does
away with any transmission losses or belt trouble, in-
creasing the efficiency of the machine. The storage
battery is of the special Delco-Light type, consisting of
sixteen glass jar cells, delivering 32-volt current. Unless
the demand for light and power is very heavy, it is
unnecessary to run the engine, except when the storage-
battery requires recharging.
Electricity is stored up in the battery by means of
the generating unit. Wires lead from the light and power
connections to this storage-battery, from which electric
current is used as desired for light or power.
0U Delco-Light to be a model of simplicity.
When it becomes necessary to re-charge the battery the
engine is started by merely pressing on the starting-
switch located on the switchboard. This electrically
cranks the engine, just as the automobile engine is
EFFICIENT and DURABLE.
cranked by an electric self-starter. The engine starts
running on its own power in a few seconds. It can then
be left to itself, as when the battery is completely charged
the Delco-Light engine stops automatically.
The supply of kerosene is regulated by means of
a simple lever-operated mixing valve. Moving the lever
to the right or left regulates the supply of fuel.
There is only one place to put oil. The crank case
is designed to hold about three quarts of oil, and a rapidly
revolving gear carries a fine spray to all parts requiring
lubrication.
The reliability of Delco-Light has been proved through
actual service under trying conditions. It has been
thoroughly tested in every possible way, both in the shop
and in practical service on "the farm and in the home.
Nothing has been left to chance. The tests have been
more severe than any service an owner would require.
Delco-Light is made by the same Works which have
Delco-Lighted and started over one million Motor-cars.

THE Gisholt Turret Lathe is a positive profit-
maker for machine-shops because of the
many devices which it embodies for lower-
ing costs on all classes of work.
The use of the tool post ancl turret simultaneously is
one of them, and the illustration here shows this prac-
tice in connection with machining the back plate of a
24-inch cast-iron scroll chuck, which had to be
finished within very close limits.
The simultaneous use of the tool post and turret
enabled this job to be finished in 19 minutes. And
the job, when finished, w r as practically right for
immediate assembling, which is a true test of effici-
ency for such a machine tool. The Gisholt is so
perfect, and work is finished to such a degree of
accuracy, that hand w'ork is practically eliminated.

Yes, wages have risen. S. Australia’s |
first judge. John Jeffcott, received £5OO a
year, and Mann, the Crown Solicitor, £3OO.
“Ivaylee”: On the Gundagai-Tumut
(N.S.W.) line I met a man who had never
previously travelled in a train. Yet,
strangely enough, many years before, he had
helped to build the line, and the train that
went over the rails he assisted in putting
down was the first he ever saw. “But I
never thought the darn thing would run,”
he told me.
T. G. Jones, the alert young lawyer who
helped Tom Johnson conduct the inquiry into
the working of the Vic. railways, has thrown
up his job with the Crown Law Department
and nailec] up his own plate. Two or three
cases a week and he will have a better show-
ing than the £5 a week at which the Public
Service Commissioner valued him under the
regulations.
“Dug”: Wealthy Americans are showing
a disposition to turn to Havana for a drink.
But a young Cuban Congressman, Cecilio
Acosta, resents the home of cigars and cubebs
being turned into a relief depot for the
thirsty gringo; wherefore lie has prepared a
bill to make Cuba as dry as the U.S.A. At
present in Havana you can find cafes selling
joy-juice in every city block, and there is
surprisingly little drunkenness. Indeed it
has been argued that Havana proves that
unrestricted liquoring tends to make men
temperate rather than intemperate. Pretty
soon the Yankee hard-drinker will have to
try Mexico. And there the local populace
love him so that he is likely to be handed
poison.
“Y.Z.,” after paying a doctor’s bill: —
A little time back various contributors were
recommending certain South Sea gargles to ordinary
civilised humanity as a means of avoiding ’flu and
bronchial troubles of one sort and another. The
simple reader was called upon to fill his head with
sea-water and other concoctions, and convert hirnself
into a human syphon, squirting a Steady stream in
at his mouth and out via the nostrils. I hope I
am in time to prevent rash experiments in this
direction. Such violent exercises may be excellent
for tough products in the savage lands, hut the
average son of civilisation is not possessed of a
throat like an asphalt track, and the earliest conse-
quence of his efforts to convert his air-passages into
sluices for more or less biting chemical compounds
would probably he an attack of laryngitis. The ’flu
epidemic has led to an immense amount of pro-
miscuous gargling, with the result that when the
doctor is not paddling after a ’flu patient he is
busy over a sufferer who has got his throat and
tonsils into a pitiful state of inflammation and
ulceration through laving his breathing apparatus
with unfamiliar fluids.
“Petcrkin” (A.1.F., London) : America’s
challenge to Great Britain for the world’s
maritime supremacy should make Australia
sit up in bed and ask what time it is. Before
the war America’s merchant tonnage was
approximately 2,400,000 as against Eng-
land’s 18.000,000. British war losses were
about 9,000,000 tons, of which roughly
4.500,000 tons have been replaced by new
ships, giving a total of nearly 14,000.000
ions.. But America has increased her mer-
cantile marine to 7.500,000 lons, exclusive of
interned enemy vessels, which she appears to
intend appropriating. Her war losses were
very small. She is now building far more
rapidly than Britain, where industrial dis-
putes and military exigencies have done
much to cripple the output. At Win. Dox-
ford’s Sunderland yards, where they used
almost to build ships by the mile and cut off
lengths to suit purchasers, the keels laid
down are fewer than before the war. largely
owing to go-slow methods. The U.S. Ship-
ping Comptroller is now in these parts with
the avowed objecc of inducing European
shipowners to adopt a uniform scale of
freightage, wages, etc., and as the Morgan
merger already holds a controlling interest
in certain spheres in the White Star, Ley-
land and other nominally British lines, the
inference is that Wall-street may soon be
dictating how much a bushel or a bale it shall
cost to carry Australian wheat and wool to
Europe ; and when Wall-street gets a mon-
opoly. experience with oil, harvesters and
tinned ’ cow has taught ns what to expect.
Hughes has done many feebler things than
lay in a stock of Commonwealth ships, and
the more bottoms Australia has to carry
goods out and home the better for us all.
“Henry Horsecollar” takes the sky-road :
The first aerial attempt at crossing the North
Atlantic was always expected to prove a matter of
trouble. The North Atlantic is a cold and heaving
and sunless thing, where the sea is grey, except the
tops, which are white, but is seldom blue, and never
very blue. It is the place of the great wrecks. Even
whales break their backs balancing on two big
waves. When there are great winds there are no
fogs, and when there are great fogs there is seldom
much wind. It is generally cold on the surface
where the ships always go, and it is cold beneath the
surface where the ships often go, and it is beastly
cold high above the surface where the airships mean
to go. The first people who grew a religion along-
side the North Atlantic had a cold hell because cold
was the thing they saw most of and liked least. The
worst thing they wished their worst enemy was
plenty of their own weather. Some places along
the shores are thickly peopled, but only because
there is coal there to keep people warm. When the
coal runs out Glasgow will he dug up and taken to
Egypt, though hunters will visit the old spot in
summer. The Strait of Dover is a humble pup of
the big, mad-dog ocean, and the first flier who crossed
it waited many days for good weather. That cross-
ing has become a matter of course now, as the one
from New York to England will in time, but the
latter will always be liable to swallow a Titanic or
two every year, through collision with an extra-large
hailstone —the kind that is an iceberg dropped from
Heaven. Australia, partly by reason of its disad-
vantages, is likely to be the ideal land for air-travel.
Over a great part of it for a great part of the year
things will be pleasantly warm above because they
are a trifle hot below. There are no fogs to bewilder
the sky-pilot because there isn’t enough rain to make
fog, and one reason why there isn’t enough rain is
that there aren’t sufficient mountains for the pilot
to bump against in the dark. All the seas that
have to be crossed on the way to Asia, Africa and
Europe are narrow, and even on the way to America,
if anyone wants to go there, Providence has pro-
vided a fair number of stepping-stones, some of
them with very sharp points and very sharp brown
girla.

Bowser, Vic. Chief Secretary, who has
been on sick-leave, is returning to work, but
McWhae is to continue to be O.C. in the
campaign against influenza. He has ap-
pointed several adjutants, and the Ministry
wants to give him a reasonable chance with
his enveloping movement.
Mention of the Vic. members whose fathers
sat in either State House recalls that the
two Clarkes had a grandfather, Peter Snod-
grass, who was notorious in the Assembly as
a mover of amendments. Charles Jardine
Don, the first Labor member, who hated the
squatters and all their kin, became exas-
perated one night when Snodgrass was try-
ing to have a clause altered. “If,” Don
growled, “the honorable member had been
present when Moses brought down the Tables
of the Law, he wouldn’t have let a clause
pass without a challenge.”
“Hamer”: One or two of the Australian
V.C.’s have succeeded in annoying London.
The champion nuisance was the hero who
formed the habit of inviting casual pub
acquaintances to stagger along and see him
turn out the guard at the Horse Guards.
The turning out of the guard is an honor
a V.C. can claim; but this bird made a
cheap sideshow of the affair for the delight
of his boozing cobbers. At last, at the
urgent request of the War Office, he was
recalled from leave, and shot back to France
with great velocity.
Tudor’s bitter complaint that the amuse-
ment tax on picture-show admission was a
device far “robbing the kiddies” is knocked
flat by statistics. In Melbourne, where
Tudor lives, moves and draws his salary, the
attendances of children at Saturday-after-
noon performances have diminished, although
these performances are exempt from all taxa-
tion. As for the cruel wrong done to in-
fancy, the official report states that, outside
of Saturday afternoon shows, 90 per cent,
of the threepenny admissions are for adults
attending continuous pictures, which suggests
that quite a lot of Melbourne has stacks of
time to burn during the day.
“Melb.” : Acting Chief Secretary McWhae,
M.L.C., is Australian-born, of Dumfries
parents. His father was one of five sons,
all sailors, but dad went to the Ballarat
diggings and made a fortune, which enabled
him to revisit Scotland, with his family.
They returned to Australia when Jack was
13, and he started as a Union Bank clerk.
After eight years with the ledgers he went
on the Ballarat Stock Exchange. The sil-
ver boom caused his transfer to Melbourne,
where he rose to be chairman, and piloted
“the room” for six years, including the most
difficult time after the land smash. Twice
he received the thanks of Parliament for
negotiating loans at awkward angles.
While South Aus. went into the ship-
building business as early as 1803, when
Pendleton and his Yankees built the 40-ton
schooner Independence on Kangaroo Island,
the first permanent settler seems to have
blown in about 1812. This was an ex-
seaman named, or nicknamed, Abyssinia,
who went into the sealing business on Kan-
garoo Island. In 1826 Abyssinia was still
there, and new settlers had come in, making
about 40 persons with the women and chil-
dren—that was 10 years before the colony
was “proclaimed.” Once a year or so
a schooner from Sydney blew in and the
inhabitants bartered their sealskins, wallaby
and kangaroo pelts, oil and odds and ends
for rum, tobacco and coins, the last to make
ear-rings from, for they hadn’t any other use.
G. D. Delprat, the Dutchman who is
general-manager of the Broken Hill Prop.,
the biggest industrial concern in the Com-
monwealth, says in the first number of the
"Institute of Science and Industries’ Jour-
nal” :
I have had many scientifically-trained men of
various nationalities working with me, and have no
hesitation in saying that, generally, the British and
Australians had the poorest scientific equipment of
any of them.
But what else could you expect? Britain
adopted Freetrade in the generation before
last; and, though it has hung on to some of
its old industries—not all of them —the
newer ones have made far greater progress
elsewhere. Australia didn’t have any old
skilled trades which the father might
teach the son; therefore it has pot-
tered along at jobs which call for
nothing much besides physical strength.
There are no big manufactures to pay for
technical training. Metallurgy and mining
engineering have recompensed a few; but
the field is limited, and was long since so
over-run that even in this line trained men
by the score have had to seek jobs outside.
“Yedyah” writes:—
lie “Politician’s” letter (B. 17/4/’l9) in which
he says treating the sale of public land as revenue
is a commercial fraud; they are alienating capi-
tal. Is not this a fallacy ? The State gets a land-
holder whom it can and does tax. The landholder is
just as much capital to the State as the land alien-
ated. The purchase price, i.e., the fee paid by the
purchaser to the State for the right of exclusive use,
is thus fairly revenue.
The other way of putting it is as follows:
N. S. Wales (which is taken as a specially favor-
able example because it raises more land-sale money
than all the other States put together) collected
about £1,000,000 a year land-sale money in 1918.
It spent over £2,000,000 loan money on railways to
help open up land and keep the revenue going. Most
of these railways had to be subsidised out of taxes
because they didn’t pay expenses and interest. A
lot of money had also to be spent out of revenue for
roads in order to earn the £1,000,000. The new
settlers pay hardly any income-tax and very little
land-tax because they are small men and inside the
exemption limit, though they pay some trifle of
customs to the Commonwealth. Very often they
don’t pay enough to maintain the local bobby and
other essentials. Therefore it seems a fair thing to
apply the land-buyers’ money towards the expenses
(raflways’ etc.), of putting the buyers on the land.

vou and that the plaintiff by his writ of summons
claims a dissolution of his marriage with the
defendant Ellen Ann Croxford Avery AND by his
petition filed in this Court on the twenty-fourth
dav of April 1919 the plaintiff alleges that sometime
between the twenty-eighth and thirtieth days of
i October 1918 inclusive and the twenty-second and
1 twenty-third and twenty-fourth days of November 1918
inclusive and on other occasions unknown to the
plaintiff the defendant Ellen Ann Croxford Avery
committed adultery with the co-defendant Edward
Stewart Curry AND TAKE NOTICE that the Court
as by order dated the seventh day of May 1919
authorised service of the said writ and petition
on the defendant and co-defendant by service of
such writ and petition on the father of the defends -,t
Ellen Ann Croxford Avery and the insertion of this
notice once in the Australasian published at Mel-
bourne in the State of Victoria once in Thu
Bulletin published at Sydney in the State of New
South Wales and once in a Labor paper published
in the State of Victoria AND TAKE NOTICE that
vou are required within thirty days from the per-
formance of the last Act required by the aforesaid
order to cause an appearance to be entered for
each of you at the Registry of the Supreme Court

you or either of you so doing the plaintiff may
proceed in this action and that judgment may be
given against you or either of you in your absence.

“The Better ’Ole,” now at Sydney Tivoli,
looks like repaying all flotation expenses and
a wad over. Most theatre-goers are curious
to see how close living actors can get to the
three grotesque musketeers created by Bairus-
fatnet, the soldier-humorist with the curi-
ously crude style of drawing. Alf and Bert
on the stage turn out to be plain human
Cockneys with tunics unbuttoned and caps
askew. But Old Bill, of the walrus mous-
tache. is grease-painted up till In* resembles
a blotched and puffy Father Christmas. He
is a good-natured, honorary father to his two
younger mates and a recognisable likeness of
the reliable, middle-aged type of Briton; but
the author of the play makes him almost
bestially stupid at one stage so that he may
he discovered as a mute hero later on. Rank
melodrama is thrust upon him at another
stage, when, unjustly suspected of treachery,
he stalks out proudly before his colonel. The
play jumps from gag to gag, so to most
readers of “Fragments” every jest is signalled
like a train. However, the three principals
put them over skilfully, and the supporting
members of the co., especially the girls, help
the show along briskly. The incidental songs
and music are bright and cheerful, and there
is quite a lot of dancing. The mud and gloom
of war are only stressed in about two scenes,
the other glimpses being mostly sunlit spots
behind the line and cheery interiors. Harry
Whaite and Harry Grist are the scenic
artists responsible, and their work is uni-
formly good. Whaite’s “The Way In” is a
fine cloth showing a moonlight effect on a
oesolate, shell-churned landscape.
Arnold Bell, as Old Bill, is the show’s
capable fullback. He realises the humorist’s
ideal of a patient and wary old soldier who
is a generous and reliable mate. Also he
uses his mellow voice skilfully in various
songs, and in the mournful chant with which
he announces himself—“ Old soldiers ne-ver
die, they simply fades away.” Percy Cahill (as
Bert, the lover of womenkind) and David
Dallam (Alt' of the patent-lighter) get
through their steady jobs as typical Tommies
and keep repeating “blinkin’ ” till the last
curtain falls on them. Amongst the femi-
nme decorations, Clarice Ilardwicke and
Audrey Worth are sunshine in stockings.
Allan Wilkie raised “The Silver King”
trom the dramatic dead at the Royal (Syd-
ney) on Saturday night, and there was a
packed house to see him do it. The King
with the wide-brimmed hat will be hurried
back into his tomb on Friday night and

One of the consolations of drenched Syd-
ney is the “Goody Two Shoes” panto, at
Her Majesty’s. In a world of wet umbrel-
las and black rainelouds, the big show
sparkles like a girl’s first engagement-ring
or the promise of your rich uncle's estate.
“Nothing but the Truth” keeps the elec-
trics blazing at the Sydney Criterion. M.
B. Figman and Lolita Robertson are the
chief assistants behind the J.C.W. comedy
counter, and business continues satisfactory.
Dame Barry Lupino still falls from the
high stool in “Jack and Jill” at the Palace,
Sydney, with all the enthusiasm necessary to
distil mirth from his rowdy school. George
Moon and Daniel Morris are as sprightly as
over in their eccentric dances; and the don-
key, William A. Hassan, turns the pages and
balances on the edge of the well with an air
of wisdom almost too profound. Dance, song,
quip and caper improve as the show mellows;
and Dorothy Leigh dashes bravely to the
conquest of lovely Princess Violet Collinson.
The Taits and Bailey and Grant are doing
their level best to lift Sydney out of its
sombre gloom.
Wirths’ big circus is keeping up the repu-
tation of its press-agent at Sydney Hippo-
drome, and the complaint-book at the en-
trance had only one item on its pages when
last The Bulletin inspected it. It read:
“Good show; but it costs too much money
to come every night.” One of the most inter-
esting features of the circus is a matinee
performance (Wednesdays and Saturdays).
The outbursts of the children on these occa-
sions would cheer up a coroner.
Eugenie Duggan is to disclose “The
Secrets of the Harem” at the Grand Opera
House (Sydney), starting at the matinee
on Saturday. Three is a slave-market scene,
and in it the beautiful Christian girl
(Eugenie) _ will probably be sold into Orien-
tal domestic service for 14s. 6d.
. Cyril Monk’s introduction of the D’Ambro-
sio Concerto was warmly greeted by an
interested and attentive Conservatorium
audience. The violinist possesses a person-
ality that tinges all his work, and he de-
serves a special hooroo for pioneering
from the worn track. Fair Doris
E ass on at the piano, in her soft white frock,
charmed both the ear and the eye and Limi-
ley Evans gave his clever fingers a night
out. The D’Ambrosio Concerto is to °be
heard again at one of the Verbrugghen Syd-
ney pops.

* * *
“De Luxe Annie,” under the Tait manage-
ment, at Adelaide Royal, is triumphing over
the doubts of the more timid as to whether
theatre-going is healthy these times. The
play is remarkable for a tangled plot, which
takes one visit to grasp and another lo
appreciate the fine acting. One records with
regret that Emelie Polini’s noisy grief
brought some laughter one evening. Call it
hysterical laughter, unintelligent laughter;
but there it was, and a sound artist ought
to be able to check it. It all arose from the
lady’s breaking the first great commandment
for emotional actresses: "Don’t make a noise
when you cry.” The part gives Miss
Polini many chances, and she makes the
best of them. Harmon Lee made a first
appearance here as the detective, and the
character sketches of Maurice Dudley, John
Fernside and Georgia Harvey enhance a
strong supporting co. At the Tivoli, the
pleasant old “Squatter’s Daughter,” is be-
ing reproduced, with real wet water and
sheepshearing on the stage. Cappelli still
heads the bill at the Majestic; Sydnev
Jones and Alice Raines mix music with their
fun, while Joe and Vera White introduce
acrobatics with their joyful proceedings.
Among pictures is “The Forbidden City” at
West’s, featuring Norma Talmadge and
showing how an Emperor’s favorite can be
got rid of, when not wanted, by the spears
—a thrilling item. At Mondergraph "Pri-
vate Peat” shows some side-lines of a sol-
dier’s life; and Enid Bennett provides a
sentimental contrast. At the Pav. is a new
picture-actor, Bert Lyell, in one more devel-
opment of the good-natured-burglar theme.
Adelaide, which had the first Repertory
Theatre in Australia (it was called at first
a Literary theatre, which seems a more
sensible title on the whole), is giving a
warm welcome to the professional staging,
by Gregan McMahon, for the Taits, of Shaw
and Galsworthy plays. The first tried is the
familiar “Doctor’s Dilemma,” superbly
played. Souper, Bryant, Eardley Turner
and McMahon himself repeat their brilliant
differentiation of the great medical types;
Bristowe and Greenaway do their parts just
as soundly though less showily. Raymond
Melbourne King's.

In “The Days
of ’98,” at Mel-
bourne Princess’s,
Denis Kehoe is de-
fending the libera
ties of Ireland with
an army of seven
supers and the
support of four
pathriotic songs.
Colonel Foster and
half a dozen red-
coats represent, the
forces of tyranny and oppression, and on
form and discipline the stolid regu-
lars ought to make a mess of the oppo-
sition. But every time Foster has the
enemy at his mercy, Sir Kenneth Cowper,
the incredible English Secretary for Ireland,
drops in to veto the execution. The old
boy develops warm sympathies for the suffer-
ing Irish, concurrently with warmer feelings
for a beauteous colleen, and the pair gradu-
ally drift towards a happy ending, what time
the hero, having escaped from prison and re-
covered certain documents from a dirthy
informer, reaches the same terminus with
his own best girl. The play is artlessly
Boucicaultian, with loads of green stuff for
local consumption, but it serves as a useful
vehicle for Denis Kehoe, whose torpid nature
can easily pass for heroic nonchalance in
such violent surroundings. Would that he
had some of the ferocity of Colonel Foster
(Peter Melling), who could safely spare
some without damaging the suspicion that he
drains a bucket of gore at every meal. Stan-
ford represents the tottering monument of
English officialdom, whilst at the other ex-
■ tremity of the social scale lurks Barney
Egan with the traditional cringe and übiquity
of the common informer. Barney is any-
where and nowhere at one and the same
time, but his faithlessness is too humorous
to excite the execration of the populace. The
piece is pretty well staged, especially in
the opening scene, where the dawn is turned
on amid the presumable twitter of Irish
canaries.
$ $ $
At Melb. Royal the penny serial story of
“Common Clay” will be withdrawn after
Friday, therefore the fallacious reasoning of
the heroine may be allowed to pass without
further remark. But the crude melodramati-
cisrn of the plot deserves a parting protest on
account of its moral. It appears that sin
and sorrow dog the footsteps of Muriel Starr
as long as she accepts a poor, but virtuous,
old party as her mother. When she learns
that her real mother committed suicide
rather than blight the career of her illegiti-
mate popper Muriel’s luck changes. She
immediately joins the ranks of the wealthy
and develops into the world’s greatest
prima donna. The man who had previously
accused her of blackmail tactics becomes
obsessed with the idea of marrying her,
although he doesn’t know of her long lost
father and her great musical achievements.
Everything in the garden of Muriel’s life
grows lovely after a heavily-dollared parent
has discovered his relationship to the im-
penitent young woman. In poverty she was
distinctly naughty, but in a state of riches
she turns rather too good to be true. On
Saturday the co. will tackle “The Silent
U itness,” which is a “mystery drama” bv
the author of “Katinka.”

George Willoughby wants to meet some-

** * *
Hoyt’s, Melbourne, are introducing a new
pleasure to the eye and intellect in the per-
son of Lila Lee, in “The Cruise of the Make
Believe.” The film holds the mirror up to
a Tom Gallon yarn and fully sustains Lila’s
claim to a place in Hoyt’s gallery of favor-
ites. “The Girl I Left Behind Me,” an old
Belasco production, starts a fresh career in
celluloid with Robert Edeson and Claire
Whitney to carry the story to a graceful
conclusion. The Metro event for the week
is “To Hell with the Kaiser,” at the Strand.
W. Hohenzollern, senior, goes Below in this
film and finds the abashed Satan ready to
abdicate the chief grid in his favor. At the
Majestic the daintiest thing running is the
celebrated Marie Tempest piece, “A Pair of
Silk Stockings,” wherein Constance Tal-
madge has to explain the fatal hosiery to a
wistful husband. A new child star, Baby
Osborne, is also hitting the screen in a
rather tricky diversion called “Dolly’s Vaca-
tion.”
Harry Dearth’s easy smoke-night manner
added further relish to his singing at the
Melbourne Auditorium on Saturdav.
Squire’s “Sergeant of the Line” was the
apex of his performance in popular estima-
tion, but he gave Handel’s “Honor and
Arms ’ and a couple of old West of England
ditties with all the unconscious graces of
the accomplished interpreter. Nothing was
tasteful than the soaring of Ivy
Ta v T in a bit of “Samson and
Delilah, whilst Elsy Treweek took a short
section of, “La Tosca” and gave it about
three-penn orth of feeling. Leila Doubledav.
in the familiar “Hjre Katy” of Hubay, and
rancoeur s “Sieilienne et Rigaudon” never
showed a keen sense for the music of either
composition. The violinist seems to dissi-
pate her temperament in the energy of her
bow arm. Harold Elvins did the accom-
paniments like « diplomatist and an artist.

N. : Arab’s” yarn about the leading
lady who fell up instead of down, owing to
overweighted counterpoise of potatoes (B.
10/5/T9), recalls a similar accident to Maud
Williamson. With the late Charles Hollo-
way’s co. we were putting up “Queen’s Evi
dence” in a hurry at Newcastle (N.S.W.),
and Maud, as the poor blind heroine, had to
fall into a lock on the Thames. For the
plot’s sake the villain had previously tam-
pered with the lock gates. There was no
time to rehearse the fall and test the work-
ing of the piano-wire that was to modify
Maud’s tumble of 6ft. or Bft. Now Maud
was always one of the willowy sort, and being
very tall her weight was deceptive. Any-
how, the mechanist miscalculated Maud’s
tonnage. So when, in Act 11., the blind lady-
stepped off the lock into nothing, a convinc-
ing splash of water (rice) from below re-
sulted; but Maud sailed up to Heaven.
Charlie Holloway shrieked furious instruc-
tions to the mechanist up above, and that

a run to high-water mark. “No! No!
