over the fly-rail. But when Maud William-
sun, with murder in her eye, started upstairs
after him, he climbed out on to the roof, and
stayed there till the show was over.
“Peg-Leg”: “Gin Fiz” (B. 15/5/T9) over-
does it in stating that the firm which con-
trols the city picture-shows is freezing out
local producers by demanding unjust terms.
The trouble arises out of the producer’s
demand that an undue amount should be
spent in advertising his film —an amount
often far in excess of the drawing-value of the
picture. The success of any production de-
pends largely on the publicity it receives in
the city, and the theatre-owners—call them
the Combine if you like—have in the past
borne this advertising cost out of their own
percentage. The terms they now ask prac-
tically mean that the producer shall bear a
fair proportion of the advertising exes.,
which generally run from £lOO to £l5O. The
Combine further demands that if it shows
a picture it should also be allowed to re-
lease it. But the producer objects to this,
because he thinks he can get slightly better
terms elsewhere. He does not realise that
through a small exchange he reaches only
a small percentage of hirers. A commercial
equivalent would be for Jones to ask Brown
OVER THE ODDS.
“Why on earth are you so jealous ?”
“Well, didn't you go to the theatre last week
with that confounded husband of yours.”
to display a special line of shirts in his big
shop-windows, while only allowing Robinson,
a few doors up the street, to sell them. My
experience of the Combine has been that it
does not desire to kill local industry. On
the contrary, it fosters it by giving the pro-
ducer the loan of its studio and camera-men,
who would otherwise be far more profitably
employed in some of the Combine’s own
activities.
“Vaudeville Veteran”: Modern comedians,
with few exceptions, don’t pay for
songs as Harry Rickards did in his
performing days. Once out in Africa
he paid for a thing he liked with a handful
of small uncut diamonds. He could size up
a song very quickly. “Popping in at Pud-
ding Time,” and “Jeremiah, Blow the Fire,
Puff, Puff, Puff,” were good numbers for
Rickards that wouldn’t win a handclap for
a comedian nowadays. His descriptive ditty,
“That’s What the Country Wants to Know,”
was of a different type. At the time he
bought it there were petitions at every im-
portant London street corner praying for
the reinstatement of Colonel Baker (Baker
Pasha). Clement Scott’s verses, “Give us
back our colonel —the man on the old white
horse,” helped the flame of agitation, and
Rickards’s ditty kept the public fanning it.
When the affair had got on the nerves of
the old Queen and the politicians, Baker
settled the question of reinstatement by
dying. I don’t recollect any other very
serious attempt on the part of an acknow-
ledged comedian to make or mar a cause per
medium of song or talk, though about 1911-
1912 practically every red-nose in the busi«
ness was winning cheap applause by gibing
at Lloyd George.
“Ring Master”: The circus was pitched
near Bairnsdale, and the co. was disposed
about a camp fire, lazing, when the strange
sight broke in upon them. He was a tall
man with a Henry Irving droop and the re-
mains of a clean shave. He was in bad
repair, but there was no mistaking the old
act-or. And his feet had gone back on him,
which is the poor player’s final calamity.
He approached the boss. “Have you any-
thing an old pro. can do ?” he asked. “Well,
I dunno,” was the reply. “If you have, for
the love of Heaven let me in,” said the
ancient actor. “What can you do?” “I’ll
do anything—any mortal thing to get out
of this.” “Go into the lions’ cage, for in-
stance?” This was said playfully. “Well,
I have done a braver thing.” “You have!”
—this derisively. “Yes, I have played
Hamlet!” He got his laugh, and that was
the beginning of the career of “Old Odds,”
one of the funniest clowns that Victoria ever
saw. Unluckily he died a year after of the
lung trouble he had picked up while hoeing
Gippsland turnips.
“Busker” : An alleged chorus-girl, who may
reasonably be suspected of wearing trousers
instead of crgpe-de-scrim fal-lals, has written
to the Akgus about the Actors’ Federation,
declaring that “she” is resigning from that
newly-formed outfit because, after all, “the
federation is to become a union, along with
the coal-miners, wharf-lumpers, motor-drivers
and slaughtermen.” It doesn’t sound the sort
of junk that a really up-to-date chorus-girl
would write; but there are plenty of ambi-
tious Willy-willies, with white hands and
two-pun-ten a week, who would gladly he
guilty of such a screed if they thought the
boss might be pleased by their action. The
average Australian chorus-girl isn’t of the
kind to worry much about her association
with unionists, however humble or smelly
their jobs, so long as such a course may pre-
sently bring her an extra four or five half-
crowns each week of fagging stage-work.
“Peg-Leg” : It happened in the days when
film-boosting was a real science.
We had a Chinese picture on at
the old Colonial Theatre, Sydney,
and decorated the place with
yellow banners bearing a few
Chow characters, which the
signwriter had copied from a
Chinkie paper. A bunch of
Celestials gathered round the
show early, and appeared to
enjoy the joke, but that night
a howling band of Chows
descended on us, ripped every
sign out of the theatre, and
w-ere just tossing-up to decide
whether to abduct the girl in
the cash-box or slaughter the
manager when the police arrived.
They were Young China Re-
publicans, and the interpreter I
called in to decipher the signs
told me that they were blister-
ing comments on the integrity
and ancestry of all republi-
cans. Now when I want Chinese posters I
make the signwriter copy the characters off
a packet of crackers.
“Arab”: Stage-hands (give ’em their due)
often have ideas of their own. I was once
in an all-champagne-and-curlpaper produc-
tion, supposed to be the very acme of taste
and understanding. The third act wound up
with a dismal death-wriggle and three chil-
dren crying in a balcony surrounded by
double-lilac. One night somebody got drunk,
and the “rag” descended with a whack
reminiscent of guillotine days. “Garn ! That
ain’t no way for a curtain to come down on
a scene like that!” said my discontented
friend. “The Count ’as shot ’isself over the
corp’ of ’is poisoned wife, and Ermyntrude
and Cedric and Charles Worrall are floodin’
the stige wiv their tears. The curtain
should come down slowly and melancholilly.
Slowly and mel-an-chol-illy —wodger think?”
“Harrison O.”: The great man arrived
from—call it “abroad” —to “produce” a musi-
cal show which had run for one hundred-and-
umpteen nights. He brought the script,
copies of which were made here, and the
show was put into rehearsal. It was not
killingly funny, and the co. reckoned the new
“producer” nothing out of the ordinary.
They changed their opinion—twice. First
he took to acquiring inspiration. “Hold on
a moment, Miss Blank,” he would interrupt;
“I’ve got a gag that will fit in there. What
was it now? Hang it all, I thought of it in
bed last night!” Then would follow an
interval while the Great Man disentangled
his teeming thoughts. At last the elusive
thing was caught. Day by day fresh gags
were fished from the recesses of that wonder-
ful intellect, always with a casual exnlana-
tion to the effect that the precious thought
had burst upon him while he was under
the shower, or been suggested at that moment
by something else. The respect of the co.
increased as the dull script became interlined
with bright remarks and tricky bits of “busi-
ness.” Then one day somebody went into the
“producer’s” cubby-hole, and while waiting
glanced through a script which he had brought
from “abroad” and had carelessly left on
his desk. All the inspirations to which he
was supposed to have given birth were
there. The script he had handed to the
management had been villainously sub-edited
so that all might see how a Really Capable
I roducer was able to transform a lump of
dough into the puff-pastry the public is
alleged to want.

“Stargazer” : Never before did I see any-
thing quite so pawky as Harry Lauder on
his opening night in Melbourne, when a voice
in the gallery called persistently for “A Wee
Doch an’ Dorris,” while Harry was bent
with a resolute endeavor on delivering him-
self of quantities of patriotic sentiment and
much stale wisdom anent the war. Sud-
denly Harry turned on the frivolous mis-
creant appealing for the rhapsody on boose,
and said, with the air of a lank, dank elder
asked to outrage his holy conscience: “Hec,
moil, we dinna want onnythin’ o’ that sort
here !” The house was stunned into puzzled
silence for a moment, and then the man in
the stalls who was really convinced for the
moment that he had strayed by mistake into
the “Kurruck,” responded with oiliest unc-
tion “Ah-h-hmen !”
“John K.” : The Pointers are wrong when
they say that the late Wybert Stamford's
only appearance on the stage in these parts
was as Napoleon in “The Lady Dandies.”
He posed as Nap. in “The Duchess of Dant-
zig” at Sydney Majesty’s about 11 years ago.
“Melb”: Muriel Window, at the King’s,
does her quick changes behind a low screen,
which shows her bust above all the time.
Margaret Kokin, at the Tivoli, showed the
process of all her changes by cinematograph.
But Eva Mudge was the quickest girl of all.
Her rapidity was such that critics declared
it was impossible, and guessed there must be
two women, very much alike. To disprove
this Eva changed her attire behind a trans-
parent screen. A dresser immediately
stripped her to a shape, and in a movement
or two put on the other things. But I think
Biondi beat the lot of them. As orchestral
conductor he bobbed up, in quick succession,
as Gounod, Donizetti, Jullien, Meyerbeer,
Rossini, Verdi, Wagner, or anyone else that
might be required. Yet an imitator of
Fregoli, for whom he was said to have been
dresser.
“P.” : A recent Poverty Point par regard-
ing the much-used idea of such plays as
“Romance,” “Eyes of Youth,” “On Trial”
and “De Luxe Annie” might have gone back
farther still. It was in Wilkie Collins’s
“Moonstone” that an Indian poured ink into
a youth’s palm and gazing into the pool
revealed the future.
“0.K.”: One of the ambitions of Ethel
Irving during the long years when her talent
was being wasted in musical comedy, was to
play La Tosca. She hopes shortly to get
into management again and play the part
which Sara Bernhardt created. She will
re-create it. For Ethel, in “Dame Nature”
and “Lady Frederick,” was a greater and
more versatile actress than ever the tempest-
uous Sara was. But Sara, from almost her
earliest days on the stage, had great chances
and exceptional personal notoriety. Long
before she made her first grand tour the
world had heard that she kept a tame tiger
in her studio and a coffin in her boudoir.
A new play on the old “Judith” theme, by
Arnold Bennett, has been produced in
London, with Lillah McCarthy as the terri-
ble heroine.
MISS MODESTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To Jan I said: “How sweet you are,
All others here exceeding far.”
“Nay, nay,” cried she,
“That cannot be.
You saucy man, you flatter me.”
1 said to Jan: “No Helen fair
Was fine as Jan. Such eyes, such hair
Delong pecu-
liarly to you.”
She said: “That’s monstrously untrue.”
Said 1 to Jan: “Thy sylph-like form,
Thy limbs so sleek, thy lips so warm,
Are past compare.”
She said: “Stop there.
It’s false and fulsome, I declare.”
“But, Jan,” I said, “it is a fact
You positively cannot act!”
Her rage and pain
She did not feign.
“I’ll never speak to you again!”
I followed Jan. “My darling, stay.
I’ve told one lie to you to-day.”
“Since you admit
I’ll pardon it,”
She said. “My acting’s exquisite!”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
MICK
SIMMONS
for
SPORTING
NEEDS
Sweepstakes for the Melbourne Cup have
been raised this year to £5O. The only object can
be to exclude the weeds and wasters. But an
extra £2O isn’t going to do that. It would have
been better to boldly stick the fee up to £loo—-a
sum that wouldn’t have stopped the champions, but
which would have much discouraged the fifth-raters.
Randwick on Saturday had another deluge.
The Plate was the good thing it looked for Poitrel,
for the St. Alwyne horse was as hard held at the
end of the three miles as at the start. Odds of 9
to 4 were gladly laid on the winner. Kennaquhair,
runner-up, is a wonderful horse. He was trained to
the minute to race when the ’flu shut the courses,
practically since early Spring. Old-timers were pro-
minent. The Dangar Handicap went to the Fortune
Hunter, who had to stretch himself to beat IJiva’s
Isle. (By the way, the latter looks more of a race-
horse now than at any time previously.) The Steeple
was won by another veteran in Glenquinn, who out-
stayed the favorite, Old Mungindi. There were
several falls in the event, and these once more
showed the need for motor-ambulances instead of the
antiquated horse-waggons in use. Myles Connell only
required one win to register his thousandth, and
punters took even-money about him doing the trick
with Arch Marella in the Dangar Handicap. They
lost their silver.
Perhaps the difference between the
weather in November and April helps to explain the
success of English horses in the Sydney Cup, com-
pared with their failures in Melbourne Cup. Within
the past six years imported nags have scored three
wins (Lilyveil, Rebus, lan Or), three Seconds (Green
Cap, Harriet Graham, Shadowland) and one third
(Lanius) in the Sydney Cup. On the other hand, you
have to go back 10 years for the one and only im-
ported winner of Melbourne Cup, and he (Comedy
King) had been brought to Australia as a baby.
Three Britishers have been runners-un in the Flem-
ington event—-Voyou, Hallomas and Shepherd King
—-the first-named dating back 20 years, and one (St.
Spasa) got third place. But Melbourne Cup experi-
ences have been a warning against putting faith in
nags from England that looked “good things” for the
two-miles race. Three years in succession the hot
favorite—Shepherd Kirg, Lanius, King Offa— -has
been one of the imported lot. In each case the
alleged certainty went more or less amiss before the
day, and two of them became hopelessly infirm soon
after the race. Judging from what they have done
in the past half dozen years it seems that November
is a bad month for horses from the cold country,
whereas March, April and May are good months.
They show their stamina when the warm weather has
gone. The Australian Cup, the, longest-di’stance han-
dicap event in Australia, is always run in March, and
four times in the last seven years it has gone to an
imported stayer.
* * * *
Big money was dropped over Publican at
the A.J.C. meeting last week, one Glen Inues
punter supplying McNoses with £l2OO, and a
well-known Sydney beneficiary of the ring sacrificing
close on £lOOO. The horse is like Desert Gold : if
he can be got away he will win, but he won’t chase
his field. Arch Marella also let his followers down
badly in the Dangar Handicap. What better advt.
could be got of the folly of trying to pick winners
than the fact that Hordern’s high-priced gelding
should have been beaten out of a place in such a
field!
* * * *
J. Daly, the veteran A.J.C. handicapper,
is ill.
* * * *
S.A. Jockey Club- made a profit of about
£3OOO over the Adelaide Cup meeting. The amount
that went through the totalisator was £ 61,000-odd,
which has several times been surpassed, though not
by much. Of the £6524 prize-money, the largest
slice went to the Richardsons, who own the Cup
winner Dependence. Mr. “Fairways,” with the
Leger and a Cup second, and J. E. Brewer, with the
Goodwood winner, were the only others to collect
over £500; but Mr. and Mrs. R. M. Hawker did so
in fact, though they have a fancy to keep their
various horses in their separate names. The Adelaide
Racing Club meeting, which is practically a duplicate
of the other, follows at once (June 3 to 10). Blue
Cross, with 9st. 71b., heads the City Handicap (7
furlongs), and after him, down to 9st., come Ipoh,
Pistolarie, Lucknow, Haud and Dependence. At the
top of the Birthday Cup list (mile and a half) are
Nightwatch, 9st. 81b., Wee Gun and New Tipperary.
+ * * *
The steeplechase at Morphettville(S.A.)last
Saturday (won by Ferrignite with list. 131 b. up)
was about the closest on record. If the post had
been a yard further back Bundong would have won.
* * * *-
Cabled last week that Panther is a hot
favorite for the Derby to be run at Epsom this
month. He has already won the Guineas. The
Derby class is remarkably high this year. Grand
Parade (10 to 1 as against Panther’s 13 to 8) was
only once beaten as a two-year-old, and Buchan and
Lord Basil look like champions on their performances
and breeding.
They have the right perspective in parts
of M.L. At last Wanganui rates the chief event on
the programme was the steeple. It was worth
£7OO and there were 18 starters —winner Coalition,
carrying list. lib. For years Australia’s best
jumpers have been show performers—in short, circus
horses.
* * * *
A Lina ere was again successful in Q. on
Saturday, Delinacre (out of Debutante) taking the
Brisbane Thousand.
Ken Austin’s 14-year-old, Bibbenluke, who
failed to bring off the double in tile Second
(A.J.C.) Steeple, is a chitjken compared to some
good horses still jumping. For example, Captain
Percy Woodland’s Wild Aster (rising 19), mentioned
in this page recently. A well-conformed jumper
should be at his best at 12 or 13. The writer knew
a 30-year mare once that was still in work as a
hack.
Victorian Public Schools boat-race, held
on the Yarra last week-end, attracted the usual big
crowd despite cold and wet weather up till the after-
noon of the racing, when the weather cleared. Gee-
long Grammar got a “row-over” owing, to the Geelong
College crew being ’flu stricken. As Geelong paddled
over the course it was impossible to get a true line
on their form. The first heat saw a ding-dong go
between Wesley and Scotch, and though Wesley made
a bold bid at the Engineers’ Corner and passed the
enemy, the Scotch came at ’em again, wore them
down, and won by a short canvas. The heat between
Melbourne Grammar and Xavier was a demonstration
of good _ style and form against brawn and muscle,
and Xavier got well away, and, maintaining the lead,
won a very tough race over the mile and an eighth in
smin. 30Jsec. In the prior heat Scotch covered the
•distance in smin. 24sec. On time, style and form,
Scotch should win the final. The knowing ones
reckon this year is the first time Xavier have ever
brought to light anything that could be called a
crew. Both heats were won by the. south-side crews,
and in each case the first boat away captured the
race. The defeated crews, Melbourne Grammar and
Wesley, coached respectively by Jim McFarlane and
Charlie Donald, displayed the usual finished style
unequalled on the river. It is the old Albert Park
way of covering the water which Jimmy Mac taught
the Donalds and the Parks which invariably won the
Vic. State championship and 14 inter-State contests
till Jimmy went across to Sydney and taught the
New South Welshmen how to row. This self-same
style has been adopted successfully at Cambridge and
in Canada and U.S.A.
Were any sports, rather than partisans, on
the Melbourne banks of the Yarra last Saturday
they must have been sorry to see Xavier beaten in
the row-off. For the first time in the 20-odd years
that the head-of-the-river tournament has been giving
public-school boys something to yell about, the
Xavierian crew had won a first heat and looked likely
to carry off the final. A long, long record of utter
failures is a heavy spiritual handicap for a college
crew, and when the red-and-black lads had beaten
Grammar (last year’s winners) in the first round, and
had dead-heated with Scotch for the final, they fairly
deserved a bit of luck in the decider. As it was
they could only make so tight a thing of it that
many people hurried away under the impression that
Xavier had just done the trick. Probably the rowing
all round was not up to the average form of recent
years. The five competitors were much of a sort.
Xavier hadn’t a length the better of Melbourne in
the Friday afternoon tussle, nor did the Scotchies
beat Wesley without splashing a little at the finish.
Geelong Grammar was a mystery, in so far that the
visiting crew had a row over the course by themselves
on the Friday. With the worst of the positions in
the final they were only beaten a length by the
dead-heaters. If the tournament were repeated next
week, and if there were any allowance for lack of
weight—the important consideration of brawn behind
the oars —Wesley would win. Why, the Wesley
ligbtweights averasred B*lb. less per oar than the
Geelong, who were a trifle heavier than Xavier, the
second biggest crew !
McKay, who stroked the winners, is a stroke
of great promise. He possesses judgment, pluck and
form, and in his three finishes he kept a fine length.
Although the rowing generally was not quite up to
standard, this was not the fault of the coaches: an
inter-State eight would not get better attention than
that given to the boys by Charlie Donald, Jim
McFarlane and A. B. Sloan, to name the three best
known.
“M.P.A.”: Melbourne ’Varsity athletic
champioi ships and inter-College. contests held on the
“shop” ground on Monday and Wednesday last week
failed to reveal any talent up to before-war standard
except in the cases of proven athletes like lan Pen-
der, the crack hurdler, and Harvison, the tall, lean
high-jumper. Pender started three times during the
meeting, twice in level races and once in a handi-
cap, and won all three. His best time at the meet-
ing, 16 3-ssec., was good form, remarkably so con-
sidering that, he had not long recovered from a severe
operation. His jumping was perfection. The tall
Harvison, suffering from a strained leg, contented
himself with winning both high jumps, the best a
modest leap of sft. 6in.—4in. below his best. The
soundest running of the meeting was done by the
distance-man, H. C. Davis, who cleared away as if
pursued by a Bolshevik Chinaman in both the mile
cnampionship and the two-mile handicap. Davis did
4min. 45sec. for the mile, covering the half-distance
in as good time as the half-mile championship was
won in. The 220 yards, won by Ross, an ex-Wesley
boy, was a good run; but the sprints generally were
below par. The quarter and half were run in time
much below public-school form. Edmunds, the ex-
Scotch collegian, got a move on starter “Storky”
Adams in the 100 yards, and won by less than a yard
in 10 3-ssec. The ’Varsity has no J. L. Davis and
“Rusty” Hunter at present. On both days the
attendances showed a lack of enthusiasm in the
sports, considering that almost 2000 students are now
at the “shop.”
* * * *
The most cheering sporting news of the
week was that that great sportsman, Harry Hawker,
had been picked up. He was a genuine “indis-
pensable” during the war, meaning that he did
work no one else could have. He was England’s
most skilled aeroplane tester; a job that involved
as many risks as any in the front line. Hawker
really typifies, in skill, insouciant courage and in-
itiative, the best sort of A.I.F. sportsman now in
England. Did any country ever get a better 1 advt.
from its youth than Australia has?
“Jake B.” : “Autolycus” (B. 15/5/T9),
after going to the trouble of explaining the differ-
ence between air-speed and ground-speed of aero-
planes, cites an example of a machine whose air-
speed is 100 m.p.h. doing a ground-speed of 90
against a 20-mile wind. If “Autolycus” had been
in the habit of doing long
“cross-countries” on which a
doubt existed as to whether
the oetrol would last the dis-
tance or not, he’d have got
rid of such ideas long ago.
I’m willing to lay a small
shade of / odds that his
machine, under the conditions
quoted, doesn’t cover an inch
more than miles of
ground. In fact, I wouldn’t
be surprised if it only did
80., A fact which isn’t 1 so
widely known about the
“pitot” is that its accuracy
varies with the density of the
atmosphere. A machine at
10,000 feet, whose pitot is
registering 92, is . actually
doing an air speed of 100. Of
course, the speed of any
machine decreases the higher
the ’plane ascends, chiefly ac-
cording to. its type, so that a
machine whose pitot shows
92 at 10,000 feet would
probably be able to put up a
speed of 125 m.p.h. at sea-
level. A speed test should
always be made as close to
the earth as possible, prefer-
ably over a stretch of water.
Bv so doing vou get the maxi-
mum results, a horizontal line
of flight and, over water, an
unobstructed course. In the
event of the engine conking
you have something to perish
in without breaking your
neck.
BELCHER, captain for
Essendon (Vic. League
Football) .
The rush for N. S. Wales Rugby League
premiership has already a straggling appearance.
After three matches, Balmain is at the top, having
arrived there without defeat; Wests, Easts and Glebe
are next —-each with cne defeat; Souths have won,
lost and drawn a game ; while the rest string out in
the tail. On .Saturday the draw-card was the Glebe-
Souths fixture. Souths won after a see-saw struggle
by 18-14. Both sides scored two tries, but the boot-
ing of five goals by Oxford (Souths) did the trick.
In the other disturbances, Wests defeated Newtown
(11-6), Balmain chased out Annanadale (37-2), and
Easts jumped over (Siorth Sydney (23-5). An im-
provement in goal-kicking was thankfully noticed.
McPherson (Wests) and Messenger (Easts) each
kickpd four goals, P. Boys (Newtown), Robinson
(Balmain). and Bolewski (Glebe) booted three each,
and Fraser (Balmain) scored two.
* * * *
Balmain’s defence in Rugby League this
season is worth some ungrudging remarks. In three
matches no tries have been scored against the XIII.
The team has scored 72 points (16 tries and 12
goals) to 10 (five goals).
The Maoriland Rugby League team, which
pushed off for Australia on Saturday, was rubbed in
the earth by Auckland before leaving by 25 points to
19. This happening has occurred in other years to
teams getting ready for an over-sea tour, and hasn’t
much significance. The team, which comprises 12
backs and 10 forwards, is a sturdy band under Cap-
tain G. Bradley, and will next face an Australian
XIII in Sydney.
“R.J.A.”: Uni. have a useful Rugby
Union team this season; one that recalls the days
of the Futters, Hughes' and Bullock. And what a
specialised and scientific game it is as such men
play it! The League game, in the writer’s opinion,
calls for little more than “foot” and an
to “take” the ball. Sheehan, of Souths, is a case
in point. He has many of the qualities of Asher —
the Maori three-quarter who used to wear a sand-
shoe on one foot and a boot on the other. He
is a League top-notcher, and yet he would not be
nearly so successful in the Union game. The idea
is re-inforeed by the prominence men who were
very ordinary Unionists have gained, of late, as
Leaguers.
* * * »
Adelaide has seen the unusual sight of a
match under Australian Rules ending in a draw.
South Adelaide and Port, after a long even struggle,
finished with 6 gaols 10 behinds each. The season,
as far as it has gone, shows Sturt and North Adelaide
in the lead, each having won the three matches
played.
The Australian Rulers had their thrill of
the season on Saturday, when Newtown (last year'.-
runners-up) defeated East Sydney (last year’s' pre-
miers) by 31 points to 29. Newtown is the only
undefeated team in the campaign.
Under Soccer rules there is a lengthening
premiership table. Granville is the only team with- '
out a defeat in four matches; tally, 21 goals to one.
Otago (M.L.) University v. Zingari
(Dunedin) was a notable clash recently. The school
won by 50 points to nine—39 to nil in the second
half. One Campbell lifted seven goals through the
uprights.
* * * *
George Mendies tried to give Jack Green
close on a stone at Sydney Stadium on Saturday
night and found the job beyond him. With ban-
tams every pound tells. In four rounds Green had
the enemy tottering, and in the seventh the towel
was wafted in. Noel and Clarrie Smith (also ban-
tams) supplied the prelim., Clarrie being outed in
round two.
Three punches from Tom O’Malley (list.
61b.) were enough to settle the pretensions of Dave
Smith’s white hope, Jerry Hansen, who entered the
Sydney Stadium ring weighing 12st. 101 b. on Mon-
day night. The disaster occupied hut train.
3 7 sec.
Harry Stone, the cigar-chewing Yankee
Yid, who did so well as a lightweight—and, later,
as a welter —at Sydney Stadium before the war, ; s
now a promoter in S.A. As'he can’t find a little
man of his class in Adelaide, or thereabouts, he is
challenging the local heavyweight, Ern Waddy, over
the championship distance.
* * * *
The Australian Trench XI made hacks of
Cambridge last week. However, it has to lie
remembered that both University teams are miles
below par still. It will be another six months
before British demobilisation is far enough advanced
to give the ’Varsities back their athletic best.
* * * #
From the firing mound: —
A wise act of retrenchment would be the abolition
of the Federal vote to rifle-clubs, which runs to
about £60,000 a year. It is a relic of the Boer
War, which left rn impression that campaigning
was mostly a matter of men with rifles lying
behind rocks. And as the Boers called up young and
old, and between the extremes did great damage, flic
scheme was looked upon as being very effective.
So the retiring age of the Australian rifle-clubber
was fixed at 60, and the baby bonus was intro-
duced. Now war is rather a matter of tanks and
gas and airships, and is more than ever a young
man’s game. Also there is no money to subsidise
gun-bearing patriarchs with.
* * * #
A S. Aus. rifle-club sec., in his official
report put it this way: “An enjoyable time was
spent, and very poor shooting was the consequence.”
“75”: There is one Melbourne golf club
that appears to have lopsided views of sportsman-
ship and patriotism. A job was vacant at the club
for which a one-legged Billjim applied. When it
was discovered that he was receiving a pension of
225. 6d. a week he was told that another £1 would
keep him, and he could have the position at the
price. Billjames promptly refused and has reported
the matter to the Repatriation Department. It is
playing it pretty low down when those who golfed
while Armageddon blazed attempt the sweating of
men who made a continuance of the pastime pos-
sible.
“C.J.”: A few weeks before the ’flu
became a general nuisance a committee was at work
in Sydney making arrangements for a Peace Regatta.
Peace, however, has not arrived up to schedule, and
the proposed programme of sailing and rowing events
has been hung up. Open-boat sailing representatives
asked that cash prizes be granted in view of the
disabilities under which they would sail in winter,
but the request was refused. Now, winter has prac-
tically set in, and most sailing crews have donned
football jerseys; so the Peace Regatta seems to be
off, so far as sailing 1 in open boats is concerned. As
it will probably be some time before all the peace
details will be adjusted, wouldn’t it be better if the
regatta was postponed till the sailing season opens in
September ? Crews will be plentiful then, and all
the boats will he fitted out.
Back to Sydney from the war the other
day came George Drysdale, well known in aquatics as
the skipper of Myra. Previous to sailing the 10-
f'ooter he was notable amongst the six-footers. When
he with a number of other harbor boys enlisted, the
10ft. dinghy club shut down.. Racing was resumed
towards the end of the war, when most of the boats
had got into younger hands. Myra, however, remained
in her shed, but will no doubt be afloat next season.
Drvsdale himself intends to sail an 18-footer.
* * * *
At no sport does the amateur cut such a
futile figure compared with the pro. as at billiards.
Pry has been amateur champion of England,
oft and on, for 20 years. He has ample leisure to
practise—he is the wealthy son of the once-famous
bookie-—and yet in the qualifying round of the
chamnionship contest, last month, he could do no
more than average 13. Boxing is the other sport
at which the amateur takes a back seat. But ft
front-ranker sometimes appears from amongst the
amateur pugs; from the billiardists never.
Some Gunning (N.S.W.) lads have been
fined for playing cricket on Sunday. As a. matter
of fact, under the Lord’s Day Observance Act, half
the inhabitants of Australia are lawbreakers. Golfers,
tennis players, motorists, etc., and all those who
minister to them: rail and tramway employees,
postal officials, hotel and restaurant servants and
many others, including those who sell the Sunday
paners and print the Monday issues of the weeklv
ones. The original Act dates from Charles ll.'s
reign. It was amended and made somewhat less
stringent in 1871.
SOME PROBLEMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The third act of the great equine drama will be
staged on the metropolitan heath to-day. The bill
of fare is an appetising one.”—S. M. Herald.
Is it drama, with grandstands and tracks as a stage?
Or a feast, or—more vulgarly—feed ?
Is it Art, when the bookmakers bellow and rage,
Giving peerless portrayals of Greed ?
Does the Governor stand for the bird or the joint?
Or is one who believes it a dupe?
Does he represent “lead,” that is really my point,
Or a species of dignified “stipe” ?
Are the ladies who saunter through paddock and shed
The equivalents charming of wine?
Not improbably, Yes, since they go to one’s head,
And they’re costly, like most things divine.
“No,” my friend interrupts. “They are heroines all,
Pure as Heaven’s immaculate snow.”
“My mistake,” I reply, feeling painfully small,
“But, now, how could one possibly know ?”
Do the jockeys provide all the comic relief,
Or do trainers and owners assist?
Should one look for the name of the Joker-in-chief
On a menu or race-card or list?
Coming now to the hill —to the question of cash—
Ts there any fixed price for the show?
But the expert here burst from the room, with a
crash,
Using language deplorably low.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

The hearl-of-the-river carnival last week
turned part of Melbourne into a turmoil.
From Pvineos Bridge up round Collius-
street and hack a continuous stream of deco-
rated motors packed with schoolboys and
girls kept passing and hooting. Generally
a delighted young matron was visible on the
hack seat Mothers of college children have
the time of their lives at boat-race season.
They live in a whirl of flapper entertain-
ment, amid a gale of buns and ice-
cream. The school colors fluttering from
the cars looked like the pennons of an army.
Mrs. Leslie Stuuit, Mrs. Billy Williams,
Mrs. Glenlotli Peter and Mrs. Gatehouse
were important matrons in the social doings
during the noisy revels. Their box-theatre
parties, dinner feasts and flapper dances
were the social features of the week, and
their young guests are still cheering.
Her Majesty’s celebrated the boat-race on
Saturday with much hunting. Flags fes-
tooned the theatre entrances and hung on
clothes-lines all over the auditorium. The
big banners of the competing schools smoth-
ered the proscenium arch, whilst most of
the mummers sported their favorite rosette.
The boxes bulged with blue-blazered youth,
parents and sundries, and the gallery was
packed with shiny juvenile faces. Alfred
Frith and Co., especially Frith, did their best
to keep the infants aloft smiling. The house
was more than half-full of flappers. Boxes
were taken by precocious young things in
their partiest frocks and elaborate hair-
hows. These young people entertained col-
lege athletes during the evening while
a smiling chaperon or two took back
seats in a shadowy way. There was
nothing simple or suggestive of the nursery
about these little parties. Each box was
decorated by florists. Two youngsters not
yet in their teens sat in a pen completely
outlined with pale blue hydrangea and had
special blue boxes of sweets. They were
hostesses to some pale blue Geelong Gram-
marians. Tallis and his wife were eclipsed
in a box by a small son in dazzling white.
The Wenmans were in a lower roost. As
each crew strode in to take its reserved
seats a roar went through the theatre. The
crews were all late, as they had been ex-
tensively dined. The performers on stage
grew quite huffy about the noise at last.
Gaud Mayor Cabena is alleged to be prac-
tising knee-drill deportment, so that he will be
able to get down and up with becoming grace
when the Governor remarks, “Rise, Sir Bill,”
or words to that effect. This is due to a
rumor that wearers of the Gaud Mayoral
rabbit-skins who have not already had knight-
hoods thrust upon them will be operated on
in connection with the Peace celebrations.
The Baldwin Spencer sale of pictures, pot-
tery and furniture, at the Fine Art Gallery,
ODened well, drew its best house on the
second day—when some beautiful water-
colors by Streeton, Hilder and Heysen
brought good prices—and slumped badly on
the third day. The heavily-gilded Mrs.
Barrett, of Armadale, was the most con-
sistent buyer, her purchases including Lam-
bert’s “The Bathers” (175 guineas) and
several canvases of Hilder, including the
ravishing “Golden Glow” (110 guineas).
Willie Kelly annexed Streeton’s “Royal
Barge at Eton” for 100 guineas. Donald
Mackinnon, Captain Hall, the Russell Grim-
wades and others also opened their cheque-
books ; but many of the Best People who
came to bid remained silent when they noted
the prices, contenting themselves with an oc-
casional titter when the auctioneer made a
“break,” as, for instance, when, bringing the
merit of Lambert’s work under notice, he
mentioned that the artist had already made
a name in Australia and would yet be heard
of in England ! But he was most amusing
when bidding became slack, and. addressing
the Very Best People as though they were
naughty school-children, he observed, more
in horror than in anger: “Really, I don’t
know tohat Sir Baldwin will think of this!”
When John Madden was Lieut.-Governor
he received half the vice-regal wage, as well
as full union rates as Chief Justice while
the imported Cocked Hat was on leave draw-
ing his whack from Australia. It is
taken for granted that the same system will
he “available” when Governor Stanley goes
on “leave” (queer name for the stunt!)
for six months prior to his retirement in
August. 1920. and Irvine, C.J., takes charge.
No official statement has yet been made on
the subject, and there is some speculation as
to whether or not Stanley and Irvine will
touch the £2500.
Governor Stanley “proclaims” Williams-
town a city. The place was named after
King William, because early tipsters ex-
pected it to be the capital. Melbourne, up
the river, was only named after the English
Prime Minister. ' The difficulty of getting
water settled Williamstown iu its cradle. All
the needful had to be brought from Mel-
bourne, the Yarra being salt up to the
Flinders-street falls. The place has a very
poor stony beach, and many of the early
settlers must have cut their feet ana lost
their tempers scrambling ashore.
The George Armstrongs have slanuned the
front door of Coombe Cottage, and are off
by the Euripides to London, where they will
spend the strawberry season at Melba’s
Dover-street house. They intended leaving
earlier, but the Dame wrote advising that
there wasn’t much in the larder, owing to
war-time food restrictions. Now, however,
square meals are obtainable in London on
demand —at a price, of course.
Published statements that the London
press is shocked to its dear old soul by the
jazz dances now the rage, make the cynics
smile. This has been its attitude to every
new dance craze —the tango, bunny-hug. fox-
trot, and even the waltz. In 1796, when
the waltz was something new, the Times
made some well-chosen remarks anent the
opportunities it afforded “for squeezing, hug-
ging, etc.,” and mentioned that “more than
one lady has actually fainted in the middle
of it.” Ginger has beeu added to the waltz
(which is of Hun origin) since then;
even so it is now considered much too
decorous for au up-to-date flapper to waste
her legs on.
In the present rush of hospital appeals
there is no worker
so missed as the late
Mrs. J. T. Davey,
who died suddenly
from influenza. Mrs.
Davey was tireless,
earnest and charm-
ing. She could col-
lect more money for
a charitable cause,
quietly and without
fuss than any other
woman in Mel-
b o u V n e. M r s.
Davey had a term
as Lady Mayoress,
and since then has
many a time given
a helping hand and
valuable advice to
a new reigning
municipal dame. By her death Melbourne
has lost a very lovable woman. This photo-
graph is by Talma.
Is there a “something” in the new jazz
dance, or is it an innocent form of recrea-
tion? This question will, in other words,
be submitted to the audience at the Town
Hall on Anzac Night. The Ilyams sistern
are to run a palais de danse in aid of the
fund, and 20 couples will demonstrate the
new craze. Meanwhile Jennie Brenan, as
counsel for plaintiff Propriety, has declared
that the American dance is only what might
be expected from its name.
Mrs. Colonel James gave a party on
Friday at the Lyceum, with Harry
Lauder's wife as chief guest. It was a
crowded show. The Lyceum is always like
a beehive or an anthill on a party day, and
if any woman wanted to swing a cat (four-
legged variety) she would have to take that
cat outside.
When Canon Evelyn Snodgrass left St.
Paul’s at Geelong and accepted the poor
parish of St. James’s, East St. Ivilda, it
was understood that- the shortage in salary
would be made up by giving him a supple-
mentary office. The wardenship of the
Community of the Holy Name has now been
handed over, with headquarters in Spring-
street. It should be a congenial billet
for the tactful bachelor parson. He will
have to direct the efforts of a small army
of Sisters, who run a variety of enterprises
—House of Mercy for girls, a pair of
hospitals, homes for babies and older chil-
dren. work at gaols and public hospitals,
and a bunch of lesser activities. Until
lately the work has been carried on by Dean
God by, now bowed by the weight of years
and unable to produce the energy demanded.
Canon Snodgrass’s step is still springy, and
he l-eckons he is good for at least 10 years’
hard going.
A storm burst in one of our big hotels one
morning recently. Newcomers on the scene
of action found a large, flushed pastoralist
threatening death to some villain who had for
the moment escaped him. It was presently
gathered that the party temporarily off the
land had come on a depraved wretch at the
door of a bathroom with his eye to the key-
hole. Now, I he young and attractive wife of the
man of acres was in that bathroom. Peeping
Tom had been taken red-eyed, and the out-
raged husband insisted on a personally-con-
ducted homicide. Two barmen, the manager
and three volunteers were holding him. At
this juncture a timid housemaid arrived with
a message from the guilty wretch, who was
hiding under his bed. He wished to apolo-
gise. They brought him down, and he con-
fronted the rampant pastor-alist. “I trust,
sir,” h p said, “you will understand when I
explain that my action was purely profes-
sional. lam a private inquiry agent!” And
now the squatter is more eager than ever to
wash the insult out with blood. But the
agent is a great runner.
Mention of Redmond Barry’s ancient
dwelling still standing by Goldsbrough,
Mort’s mass of bluestone in Bourke-street,
recalls the fact that the distinguished Judge
had a charming week-end retreat on the Bay
View-road, at Burwcod. It is still in use
to-day, and very much improved in its .sur-
roundings since Barry had it. The place
lies near the southern fringe of what is
now Melbourne’s most productive orchard
areas, and is planted on every side with young
trees, served by an immense irrigation dam.
Utility is to-day the watchword of the
neighborhood; and the simple, cow-raising,
apple-growing life of the community con-
trasts sharply with the merry-and-bright
atmosphere of the house in Barry’s day. The
learned bencher used to drive into the city
behind a pair of fast horses, after a walk
around the garden and an early breakfast.
John MeWhae, Acting Health Minister,
has decided to issue an honor-badge to those
nurses who have attended ’flu patients for
three months or until they took the disease
themselves. A bar will go with the badge
in cases where nurse has been on duty ever
since the Department recognised the epi-
demic as worthy of its official notice. A few
nurses have flatly refused to attend ’flu
patients. Those who haven’t shrunk from
the danger deserve every bit of credit that
can be given them.

she had been classed as a contralto; but the prim i
donna was emphatic that Nature intended Doris for

A drear tale of the desolation in Belgium and
Northern France comes from Mrs. H. G. Reid, who
before her marriage was a Miss Brock. She motore d
from Paris to Brussels with her soldier husband in
February, and saw graves everywhere. She describes
Belgium as “one vast quivering wound.' 1 At
Avesnes “it was like a glow from a warm flame
to see Australians everywhere.”

Dr. Price, the good-natured Town Hall
organist, was touched on a sensitive spot
at last week’s municipal concert when a
party of gabbling women insisted upon chat-
tering while he was playing. Price tried
hard to ignore the disturbance, but it was
more than his artistic temperament could
stand. “If you don’t stop talking I can't
play,” he announced at last, and the shamed
gasbags collapsed.


FIGHTING THE INFLUENZA
THE FOUR PRINCIPAL FACTORS EMPLOYED.
In fighting the menace that has been
threatening the people of Australia
during the last few months, four weapons
have been principally employed, viz.:
The MASK, to cover the face and keep the germs
out; a VACCINE, with which the public have
been inoculated to increase resistance; many kinds
of DISINFECTANTS, to kill floating germs; and
lastly, but by no means least, various REMEDIES
to keep the system free from germs, clean and in
order.
It can be said that, in this latter particular, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
have played an important part without any booming, such as was done by
many to use the influenza epidemic to attract attention to their commodities,
for which they were of little use. Straight announcements were made
advising the public to keep their systems in order by taking an occasional
dose of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills together with 10 grains of Aspirin or
Quinine, but on no account to try to doctor themselves should they show
any signs of the real Influenza, but to send for the doctor and to go to bed.
This advice has been followed to a wonderful extent. Tens of thousands of bottles of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills have been sold since the epidemic started. One retail company alone has passed across.its counters
over five thousand bottles every thirty days. Many wholesale houses have exceeded this quantity in Sydney,
Melbourne and Adelaide. More than a ton of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills was sold during each of the
epidemic months. There are about two thousand two hundred pills to the pound (anyone can work it out),
which makes over five million pills sold during each month in Australia, or at the rate of about a pill for every
man, woman and child on the continent. The wonderful character of this sale is further emphasised when it is
known that no salesmen were employed whatsoever. Orders came through the mail, or over the telegraph and
telephone wires, and were in response to the genuine demands of the trade. It can, therefore, be seen that
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have played their part, as already stated, with the Mask, Inoculation and
Disinfectants, in fighting this epidemic.
It is not claimed they are better than anything else made for similar purposes, but it is claimed that they
are far better than most and equal'to the best made in this or any other country —and why notP The
Proprietors have been making pills for nearly a century, and for twenty-five years they have been in constant
use in Australia. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are like Ford cars. It is quantity production that makes
them possible at the price. If the formula were taken to a chemist he would charge five shillings to
make it up, and there would be no guarantee that the ingredients had not lost their efficiency. That cannot
happen in manufacturing Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. The ingredients are bought in large quantities and
rapidly used up, and the activity of this pill is certain. They are sold to the public at a shilling or a little
more, subject to local conditions, and in any case the public gets good value. These are the reasons for their
immense popularity, which can be summed up in the two words, “Absolutely Dependable.”

On Thursday Sydney shook off her sulks
and trimmed herself with golden sun-
light to commemorate the unforgettable
Landing of 1915. All thronged and
beflagged ways led to the Town Hall, where
four padres led the congregation in prayer
and hymn, encouraged with a wonderful
heartfulness by soldier and sailor, maimed
and sound, who were there in hundreds.
Many were carried in the arms of comrades;
now and again a cheerily-grinning chap came
riding pick-a-back, with his own poor useless
legs . held stiffly out. There was a great
burst of applause when the colors were being
draped around the back of the stage—greens
and golds, blues and violets —and the pulse
of the crowd of khaki-clad men leapt again to
the flags. There was wave after wave of cheer-
ing. Matron Cameron, who was injured in
1915 in the Aegean Sea, was borne in by
four soldiers, and then the cheers swelled
again. A big official with a heavy Scotch
burr and the stars, who seemed to have every
seat in the hall tabulated in his brain, stood
stiffly to attention. Under cover of this ex-
citement a little woman in black stole in. By
her side trotted a golden-haired youngster
wearing daddy’s colors on his arm: the green
and white topped by the A. While the
imagination was still stirred by the echoes of
the Last Post, she went on to the stage,
almost t’oe last of the quiet looking men
and women who ascended the steps to take
ribbon and medal, in honor of those who died
for the nation.
All Sydney’s thorough-paced party-goers
were at the Garden Island At Home to the
Fleet. The evening began with theatricals
and the sail-loft was filled with rows of
collapsible chairs that required careful navi-
gation by the audience. “The Rest Cure”
and “The Playgoers” were the two playlets.
The former seemed to conceal a warning nut
to have “nerves,” and the folly of a man
losing sight of his leg envelopes upon enter-
ing a nursing home. Harold Watch, with a
strong support of female beauty, was the
“star” in the hospital scene; but Captain C.
Keith Cohen had the house in “The Play-
goers”—which was evidently written by
Pinero in a moment of dejection. M rs.
Roy Buckland shared the lead with
Cedric Cohen. Others were Mrs. Burrows.
Mrs. Salter, Mrs. Bromwich, Doris Rook
(who promises well in low-comedy parts).
Lena Blaxland and Daisy Fell. After the
final curtain the collapsible seats were
banged together, and dancing commenced in
earnest. Commanders Wilkinson, Burrows
and Glossop and their wives were present,
the tall Mrs/ Burrows draping her shoulders
in a jade-green scarf. Mrs. Rolin, in black,
chaperoned one pink and one buttercup
daughter, and Jean Curlewis in pale blue;
the Macarthur Onslow miss brightened her
black raiment with a blue sash : the tiny and
fair Toto Beith flashed her emerald-green
frock through every dance; Mrs. Venour
Nathan had the sleekest head and the only
black panne frock in the room; Mrs. Roy
Buckland contrasted her fairness against
reseda green ; Cecily Cattrell and Mrs. J. K.
Osborne chose brocade with its old-world
•suggestion; while Mrs. Henry Hill Osborne
wore old-rose velvet; and black was the
raiment of Mrs. Owen Dibbs. There were
yards of gold braid and a khaki suit or two
to dance with the pretty girls, so everybody
was happy.
The night before the arrival of the Mel-
bourne and the six destroyers, various Syd-
ney pressmen who had to do the “introduc-
tions” to the papers’ naval welcome looked
at the sky and decided that to-morrow was
going to be fine. Then they turned to their
desks and wrote beautiful stories about blue
skies and sunshine. One, with anthill
ideals, guessed that the sailors would perish
of disappointment “if every headland were
not black with people.” As soon as some
of his rhapsody was in type, the rain clouted
down again. When morning came several
wild journalists looked out on a dripping
world and saw that they should have
selected Scotch mist or shower-bath over-
tures for their overnight studies. When the
wet cruiser and the six damp destroyers
slid up to their moorings about 10.30 a.m.
the crews saw the Domain slopes speckled
with black umbrellas and glum citizens in
goloshes. The morning scribes having
crashed, the naval experts of the evening
papers splashed to their offices and rattled
out yards about “long grey ships of war
silently stealing to their moorings through
the mist.” That afternoon the city was
full of sailors silently stealing to their well-
won refreshments—and getting plenty of
them.
Cinderella Sydney, with her slipper
just returned to her by Prince Billjim,
who has taken nearly five years in outwit-
ting the German knave that had stolen it,
has fitted it on, and is simply crazy to try
all the new steps she's been learning. So in
addition to the Peace Ball and the Grand
Rose Ball, the Queen's Club is giving a ball,
and there’s talk of a Matrons’ Ball—the very
pick of the matron’s basket. As a dead
secret, it’s going to cost them £l5 each, but
for that little lot they may invite eight
guests.

ney last week, to welcome our jaunty little

As though she resented her good humor
of Thursday, Sydney turned a petulant face
to Randwick on Saturday, and the Autumn
Cup meeting ended with savage sweeps of
rain. This meant the stand shelter for
the furs and tailorings. Nevertheless, Cor-
rectly turned-out flappers were quite a
feature of the day. Mrs. Gordon Wesche
flung a marabout cape over her black
frock, while a cavalier cape gave Mrs.
Rabett’s navy suit quite a dashing look;
Madame Shimizu smiled serenely over blue
fox and the impeccability of embroidered blue
charmeuse; green cloth and black fringe
clothed Helen Macarthur Onslow ; the Alec
Dalgleidhs, from Goulburn, were there, Mrs.
Alec in dark tailorings and skunk; the
Ranee wrapped her sealskin about the last
word in coat frocks; the two pretty Rolin
girls brought mother in dark blue and white
check; Madame de Beaupuis was with
daughter Mrs. Osborne, who added slash-
ings of cerise to her navy suit, and wrapped
her hat and throat in pink veilings; Mrs.
Bertie Sheldon aired a magnificent diamond
brooch and a smart top coat; Minister Ful-
ler's daughter wore Wedgwood blue under
musquash ; Cissy Jamieson pulled a blue
hat over her gold crown and brought with
her Primrose Caryll, who is helping to tell
“Nothing but the Truth”; Commander Bur-
rows's lady supported smart tailorings;
pretty Una Barton was a picture in pale
grey tones: Mrs. Scott brought her black
and white checks from Newcastle, and had
with her sister Belle Rankin in brown ; and
Captain Roy Forrest’s brand new wife
smiled above rose jersey cloth.
Will Edgar has
been in the fleet in
Australian waters
since 1906. In 1910
he went to England
in H.M.S. Gibraltar,
under command of
Captain Grant,
erved in the Nep-
tune under Admiral
Callaghan, gained his
commission and served
on . the Australia,
during her building
and till 1918, when
he was appointed
chief engineer of the
Iris. He was in the
Zeebrugge and Os-
tend adventures.
The little job of the
Iris was to land a storming party at the
Mole and participate in the blocking opera-
tions. Edgar now wears a D.S.C. for
bravery. For 27 hours continuously he
stuck to his post. The Iris was brought
home safely to port in a badly-damaged con-
dition. The young man is now Engineer-
Lieutenant Edgar of the JeVvis Bay Naval
College.

Billjemima learned quite a lot of things
during the war. One of her was billeted for
a while near a Digger battalion in France.
Later, she mentioned the fact to her fond
mother. “They were splendid fellows, ma;
but they used such funny language.” “I
hope, my dear, you didn’t pick up any of
their ‘funny’ language.” “Oh, no, ma ; just a
few words like and and .” “For
Heaven’s sake, child, be quiet!” shrieked the
horrified parent. “Suppose your father heard
you!” “Oh, d —n father!” replied the un-
dutiful one.

All Saints’ College, at Bathurst (N.S.W.),
is about to close down, after 40 years work.
The fine building is on one of the most pro-
minent hills on the plains. The property
belongs to a former headmaster. Rev. Canon
Tracy, now resident in England. Amongst
old boys are C. G. Wade, Arthur Jose, W. H.
Kelly, M.H.R., and C. E. W. Bean, war
correspondent. The fighting record of the
college can be disclosed with pride, Two
hundred ex-students enlisted, and one of
them, Corporal A. Hall, won the V.C.
Claines, the R. T. Carter home just pur-
chased by R. B. Orchard, of the Federal Min-
istry, was simply packed from attic to cellar
with art gems that make the Arabian Nights’
treasures less like fiction. The Sevres and
Chelsea collection was possibly one of the
most unique private collections in the world ;
while the Royal Worcester and Wedgwood
certainly had no rival in Sydney. There
were innumerable fine specimens of Georgian
silver, among which was a tea-caddy that
Doctor Johnson would have adored. It was
of George 111. design elaborately embossed
with figures of Neptune and Diana with a
host of cherubs in attendance. One priceless
treasure was a porcelain cameo of Napoleon.
Before this was gathered to the Carter fold
it belonged to the Moore family who claim
that the Little Corporal gave it to their
grand-dad on St. Helena’s far isle. Book-
lovers focussed upon three original etchings
by Thackeray for “Vanity Fair,” and the
original xmlume- (first edition) of the work
that gave birth to the inimitable Becky.
There were paintings by wizards of color,
and carpets that might have accompanied
Cleopatra on her unconventional visit to
Caesar. Six thousand pounds of the proceeds of
the sales go to the Carter banking account, and
connoisseurs think that Christie’s would have
paid tlii times that figure for the collec-
Hj n 'c< j r " '^ arter was one of the figures of
old Sydney, and his antique shop at the
corner or Jamieson-street had a renown that
was world-wide. Thirty years ago he put up
the shutters and transported cases of the
treasures to the Darling Point house which
had been the Jack Want home. Some of the
cases, they say, were never unpacked until
the sale.
The Empire Concert on Saturday night
failed to attract Verbrugghen’s usually
large audience. Alfred Hill’s Maori Rhap-
sody was a deserved encore. Frank Charl-
ton, a tall young man from Maoriland, was
found guilty of having a rich baritone. Mad-
ame Verbrugghen sang a group of folk
songs, and “Land of Hope and Glory.” for
which her voice proved too light, but the
orchestral background made the contribution
charming.
Brisbane joins the tea-party:—
Mrs. Langbery, younger daughter of Justice Chubb,
brought her two young daughters over from Korea
the other day. They are staying at St. llalo, the
Chubb home. Dad is looking after the interests
of the Standard American Oil Co. in Seoul.
Ex-Senator Bob Sayers, who died last week, came
to Australia in ’63, at the age of 19, and started
to chase gold. It was in ’7B that he first entered
politics and got into Bananaland Assembly for a
five years’ spell. He afterwards went to the Fed-
eral House, from which he resigned about five
years ago. His home at New Farm was a kind
of caravanserai for old pals from the North.
The John Macdonalds are waiting for their son
Roy, who is a flight-lieutenant. Macdonald senior is
the managing-director of John Bridge and Co.
The members of the Soldiers’ Comforts Fund
farewelled their president, the Cowley dame, last
week, and thrust silver candlesticks and a silver
clock upon her.
Edna Raymond Jones, daughter of A. R. Jones, of
Indooroopilly, and a cousin of the pretty Sydney
Jones girls, is to be married in a few days to
George Darvall.
Influenza has killed Walter Williams, the popular
manager of the Queensland Trustees, Ltd. He had
been only 18 months in Brisbane, and was a solici-
tor of the Supreme Court of N. S. Wales.
Brisbane slapped the Australian Navy on the back,
begged it to drop in at the Club, and ’insisted
that it should dine and dance every night. The
boats were swarmed over by visitors, and the fare-
wells (some of them at least) were most pathetic.
Mrs. F. K. Brodribb hostessed a cheery dinner-
party on —-'.rday night in honor of pianist Eddie
Cahill and singer George Brooke.
■Last Saturday the remaining Sydney Jones girl
met at the altar rails Lieut. Dundas Gore, R.F.C.,
son of the Fred Gores, of Goondiwindi. The bride-
groom was an original 2nd L.H. man, who, after
doing his bit on Gallipoli, got into the Air Service,
was taken prisoner and sent to Austria. The bride
is the eldest daughter of Mrs. J. T. Bell and the
late Sydney Jones, solicitor, of Rockhampton. The
only bridesmaid was Margot Bell, the bride’s half-
sister. Major and Mrs. Arnold Weinholt (the latter
is a sister and has only been married a few weeks)
were at the wedding.

A Splendid Repair by the
Oxy-Acetylene Process,
NOW, more than ever, experienced engineers pin their faith in the Oxy-
Acetylene Fusion Process in time of breakdown trouble. Users of
machinery on the land and in the workshop find the Oxy-Acetylene
Process a wonderful factor in saving money by abolishing the necessity for
buying new parts of machinery and new tools.
The illustration shows a Steel Acid Container belonging to the Ammonia
Company of Australia, Sydney. The section outlined by the chalk marks
was eaten away to a mere shell. The job was taken in hand by J. Paterson
and Co., Expert Oxy-Acetylene Operators, of Chippendale, Sydney. The
corroded section was cut away with the Oxy-Acetylene Cutting Blowpipe, and
a new section welded in with the Welding Blowpipe, a perfect fusion, return-
ing the container to its original homogeneous state for a trifle compared with
the cost of a new one.

This is one example
of Motor-car Repairs.
THIS motor-car charged a wall, with
the result that the channel frame,
on two sides out of three, was
badly broken. Under ordinary circum-
stances it would mean considerable dis-
mantling and ugly patches.
The adaptability and convenience of the Oxy-
Acetylene Blowpipe obviated expense, trouble and
delay. The repair was effected with very little
dismantling of the ear, as you can see, because the
Oxy-Acetylene Operator can use the blowpipe in
any position, in any direction.
There is no comparison between Fire Welding and the
Oxy-Acetylene Welding Process, which is a perfect fusion
of the parts to be joined.
There is good business for garages operating an Oxv-
Acetylene Repair Outfit, which enables broken axles,
crank and gear cases, steering gear and every broken tool
or metal part composed 0 f cast and wrought iron, mild
steel, brass, copper, aluminium and gunmetal to be per-
fectly repaired for a trifle compared with the cost of
new parts.
Factories, Workshops, Shipyards, Ironfoundries
and all who use machinery will find the Oxy-
Acetylene Process a money-saving one.

“D.F.”: Sydney's first steam-engine was
set going by John Dickson, who arrived in
October, 1813. Writing on April 28, 1814,
Gov. Macquarie states that he had given
Dickson “a most Convenient and Eligible
situation in the Town of Sydney, having a
run of fresh water through it, for him to
erect his Mills, Steam Engine and various
Machinery on.” Dickson used his engine
to run a flour-mill, and he seems to have
had a sawmill as well. In 1819 he put in a
tender for supplying at a lb. all the
flour needed for the Government stores in
N.S.W., but he failed to get the contract.
In April, 1815, Walter Lang, a settler at
the Derwent, was given permission to take to
Van Diemen’s Land the materials for a
steam-engine which he proposed to erect at
“Hobartston.” He also tried to get, on the
strength of the steam-engine, a grant of
3000 acres; but the deal did not come off.
It does not appear that he ever set up the
steam-engine. Twenty years later, though,
Hobart was far enough ahead to have one
built locally.

SAFEGUARD THE CHILDREN.

It is only a little cold, you say; still it often
means a lot of suffering for the little folk and
worry for mother, so be wise and get the best that
can be had. Dr. Sheldon's New Discovery is a
wonderful remedy for all Coughs, preventing Influ-
enza, Pneumonia and Bronchitis. It is the safe-
guard of many a home. See that your children
keep bright, healthy and rosy with the help of
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery.
Dear Sirs, —At two years of age my little girl
Phcebe suffered with an acute attack of croup.
I tried the usual remedies, and evidently they
did not get at the root of the ailment, because
the attack left Phoebe a severe cough, which
sometimes became chokingly croupy at night.
A friend of mine, in whose house I was stay-
ing with Phcebe, was awakened one night by
one of these frightening coughing seizures,
and she brought in to me a bottle of your
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery, and we gave
the almost choking child a dose of the mix-
ture The effect was so immediately beneficial
that I have kept Dr. Sheldon’s wonderful rem-
edy bv me ever since. I feel that if I had used
it at the first, instead of trying other so-called
curative medicines, Phcebe would not have
suffered so with her cough. I send you her
photograph, which will show you how well she
keeps now.
Hoping that my little note of appreciation
of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery may help
other mothers to rely on your good, simple,
soundly-healthful remedy.—l remain, yours
faithfully, (gg d .) OLIVE K, TUBB.

The Miser.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f'PHEY stood and whispered
'I I heard their dismal, hateful brea. .
xheir feet were muffled on the floor
And rustled in the Room of Death.
They watched me lie and struggle there
Against the last eternal Cold
Thev waited for that glassy stare
1 That told them I had left my gold.
Thev listened with their greedy ears
TI Sd Sought Of all that wealth can .f.
They brought their sweet pretence of tear.
And waited for the Rich to die.
And each one clutched his sweating hands
And tried to call himself my friend,
And stood as one who understands
Each cough is nearer to The End.
I thought of how tjiey’d hunt my wealth,
And never find its hiding-place. ~
I watched them move their eyes with steaitn,
And laughed, and let them see my face.
And one of them with loathly guile
Laid shaking fingers on my head.
He said. “The dying always smile;
Another hour will find him dead.
And then they gathered round in prayer
While silently I rose and left
My grinning body lying there
Within its circle of bereft.
The laughing sun beat, in and shone
Upon the mouths that spoke no word.
“I’ve gone, you fools!” I shrieked. "I’ve
gone!”
But no one answered —no one heard.
I left them there to croak and weep
Around that corpse upon its bed —
A corpse that smiled within its sleep;
And then I went —for I was dead.
I wandered all around the earth
And left them in the room of Pain
Beside the dead that died with mirth
To know the quick had wept in vain.
N.S.W.
L. MAXWELL.
With All Faults,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CAPTAIN THOMAS MISSION, master
of the Star of Samarai, waiting for
orders at Seattle, anchored until the
weeds grew on his ship and trailed in the
tideway like the hair of drowned women.
He and his officers went ashore not often ;
their pay was too low for dissipation,
although the smallness of it had made them
in matters of money as clever as rats in time
of famine.
I knew that the Star of Samarai waited,
not for freight, but for sale, and that Shigoku
and Co., of Yokohama, were after her. But
she wasn’t sold when last I saw her master,
and the story was told to me by Billy
Hauberk, who was given a passage to Yoko-
hama when she was sold, and was the only
honest man working on the ship, or owning
it. At least, he says that. Billy Hauberk is
a master himself —but out of a job through
temporarily losing his ticket by permanently
finding a reef.
We sat in Bombay flirtation chairs on the
balcony of the Queen’s at Townsville —very
comfortably, with the leg-rests well extended,
the pipes drawing well, and the big whisky-
pegs standing by in glasses, so thin and with
ice so fragile that the creak of a chair made
a musical tinkling of ice against the rim;
Magnetic Island the blue ghost of a blur in
the starshine, and the red lights on the
breakwater purpling in the violet haze. And
Billy Hauberk talked.
“Y’ know the Star of Samarai? Rattle-
trap engines; weary boilers; carried jute,
niggers and derelicts —and should have made
a good sale by sinking at her anchors at
Seattle. Y’ know the owners; head office in
Liverpool to disguise Welsh navigation and
wowser ownership.
“Tom Mission’s her skipper, and although
he talks with that Oxford and Cam-
bridge accent tuat makes him sound like a
curate who’s half-sister to a suffragette, he’s
the most able-bodied cumshaw merchant,
between Durban and Vladivostok. If he
puts a coat of paint on her there’s a bit for
him in the paint, as long as she isn’t in a
home port; and if there’s a new towel bought
for the bathroom Tommy Mission cuts a bit
off the fringe for agency charges. She
stripped her propeller at Kobe, and Tommy
pulled a commission out of the dockyard;
and when she broke her tail-shaft near
Hongkong there was a Chow engineer cough-
ing up a bit for Tommy at Kowloon. He
chewed commission out of coal and water,
and on the beef and every banana in the
ship; but whenever she had a passenger he’d
fill that passenger with cigars—which was
decent, although they were given him for
nothing. The dhoby man for the ship did
the captain's white suits for nix, and pipe-
clayed the shoes he hadn’t paid for; and so,
with one thing and another, including pier-
head jumps from ports he was unlikely to
see again, Tommy managed to make ends
“He couldn’t have done that if he’d been
in a regular trade; because people would
have got tired of him, and creditors would
have caught him second time round. But he
practically never touched the same port twice
running, inside the Statute of Limitations.
It was down to Australia with rice; to
Bombay with horses; over to ’Frisco with
Indian" goods; to the Philippines with
machinery and notions; to New York or
London or Liverpool with sugar, copra,
cigars, sapan wood and hangmen’s goods.
Tommy Mission didn’t think that carrying
Manila rope was personal either. He was
very hard-cased, was Tommy.
“His owners made him so. His screw was
£24 a month ; and they expected seamanship,
responsibility, honesty and the virtues of an
archangel for the eightpence an hour they
paid him; and between whiles they drew his
attention to the fact that many tramp-
owners make the master buy his own charts,
but that they didn’t, because they were
Christians. Of course, they could have got
plenty o’ young fellows with captain s tickets
to take the job at less. You know pride of
the first command, and dam-fool romances o£
the sea; outside of hell being fine when
you’re twenty-five or so.
“When hard luck brought Tommy Mission
up to Liverpool to see his owners, he went
like a man going to execution —and he didn t
feel any better because there was enough
hemp in the after-hold to hang a million
harder cases than Tommy. First day they
were very nio;e to him ; glad to see him back
from the perils of the sea, and all that
sort of rot, the only unsafe place tor
Tommy in the world being in Ins owners
office. By the second day they were cooling
off, and by the third day they’d been through
his disbursement sheets, and every time they
read ’em over their yelps of agony were
louder.
“The boss owner would say to him, moving
about as if his cnair was made of barb-wire:
‘Captain Mission, these mess-accounts on
the disbursement sheets are ruinous ruin-
ous! Why, I see here that the men get a
chop, and more; and a potato —some of them
more than two.’ . , . , ,
“ ‘Board of trade, sir. Board of trade,
says Tommy. , ,
“ ‘But, captain, why not one chop and one
potato?’ ,
“ ‘They must be —ah —fed to work.
“ ‘And I’ve been taking the average of
eggs consumed by engineers. It works out at
one egg and a fraction per diem.’
“ ‘I got to feed the blighters —er —gentle-
men,’ says Tommy.
“ ‘And d’ye know, captain, that the engi-
neers’ mess had pickles?’
“ ‘The bli —engine-room staff —ah —lead a
fairly monotonous life, and they say they
ah —can’t keep awake without —-ah—pickles
—so endangering the safety of the ship!
“ ‘Nonsense, captain ! Nonsense ! It mast
not be repeated! Why, I don t permit
pickles on my own table except-—perhaps—-a
walnut for each person with Monday s cold
joint. See that it doesn’t happen again,
captain.’
“‘Very good, sir,’ says Tommy, and goes,
out swearing to himself, and buys a case of
pickles first port, and gorges the engineers
mess on ’em. But the meanness made mm
get revenges in other ways—ways more pro-
fitable to himself. Meanness like that stifles
conscience, and throws remorse overboard.
Tommy Mission would pull up his pick, and
go to" sea, prepared to wipe the owners
eye every time he got a chance. He dreamed
commission, and all his waking thoughts were
eumshaw; and he got as expert as a church-
rat at getting things for nothing ; and at
nosing out new ways for safe bribery and
tracking profitable corruption he was cleverer
than a New Jersey senator. He could beat
commission out of everything but an Angli-
can Synod and a sewing-bee; and the only
people he didn’t exact tribute from were the
widow and the fatherless, because they never
have any money; or if they do a public
trustee company guards it jealously against
all the thieves who haven’t a certificate of
joint-stock incorporation.
“And, as I tell you, he could get his dues
out of painting, docking, coaling, providormg
passengers, parcels, exchange, flotsam and
jetsam, beachcombing, barratry, simony, the
king’s enemies, and the act of God. Every
time he dropped the pick he opened a new
account; and he didn’t lift the pick until
he'd been paid his tribute —cash or kind,
money, clothes, wine, washing, jewellery or
cigars. God sent his owners on earth to
make the world look mean, and He made
Tommy Mission as a counter-irritant, and to
prevent owners becoming too rich.
“So things go on until they’re where you
saw ’em at Seattle eighteen months ago, with
weeds as long as the hair of a girl at
Mariquina. That’s where her last run had
been —hemp from Pasig River; and then no
freights going, and the owners throwing fits
by cable while she lay at Seattle growing
vegetables on her bottom. Meantime Tommy
Mission and his deputy-bribees and assistant-
secret-commissioners are hauled to blocks.
They can’t make any money, and of course
they’d saved none, not being mean enough to
think of a cold day coining in the tropics.
Anyhow, what comes by the wind goes by
the water; and that’s the way theirs went —
water with Scotch in it, and Gage-road, and
the little house in Samnaloc, and short-
legged Jap girls pretending to dance the
Balitao. And they’re so flat broke they’d
take down a Capuchin monk for all the socks
he has, which aren’t any. There they are—
poor as church mice, miserable as a Jew in
Hell without his cash-box, and watching the
long weeds on her bottom swing to the tide,
some of them trailing as long as a pennant.
You live on an anchored ship for months,
with nothing to do but look overboard and
see the weeds grow and the tides change;
and by-and-bye you’ll be as mad an’ des-
perate as they were.
“Just when they’re about giving up all
hope, and thinking about eating all the
pickles at a sitting to spite the mean cuss
who’s throwing fits by cablegram, the chance
comes. A shipping agent at Seattle says he’s
got a client in Yokohama who wants to buy
a ship like the Star of Samarai. • Tommy
Mission won’t put the agent wise to his
owners, because he thinks he might get a bit
of eumshaw out of the sale; and the agent,
after backing and filling for a week, came
over with the blood-money; and Tommy puts
the agent alongside the owners, and they got
to grips. The long and the short of it is that
the Star of Samarai is sold to Shigoku and
Co. of Yokohama, and at Yokohama she is
to be delivered. The owner doesn’t know
whether he’ll clean her up before she starts,
or try to save the dock-money and lose it on
the coal-bill. Tommy doesn’t care much,
because his eumshaw on the dock charges
wouldn’t be so much bigger than his cum-
shaw on the extra coal; so at last they com-
promise. They clean her up as well as they
can without dockinv. and the owner throws
more fits by cable when he hears the price of
the coal; and so at long last the Star of
Samarai hauls its pick out of the mud —
where it thought it was going to stay for
keeps—and pushes out westerly to the Bund
and the Hotel Nectarine.
“While they’re on that run, Captain
Tommy’s that busy scheming you can hear
him think. She was in light-ballast trim.

OUTDOOR workers, who brave all weathers in rough work,
generally have skin trouble of one kind or another.
Perhaps the skin gets hard, and cracks most painfully and
dangerously; cr it may be that the cold or dirt gets into some
trivial injury, inflammation and disease almost certainly
f jllowing. Cold=sores round the mouth and nostrils are also common
just now, and so are chapped hands,chilblains,chafingof the face, etc.
Zam-Buk is equally soothing, healing and curative for all such conditions of the skin, and
is consequently a real boon. This pure and powerful balm takes.the “smart” out, kills pain,
“cools” inflammation and allays itching. Continued use of Zam-Buk ensures perfect healing
with new, healthy skin, and “tones up” the tissue to resist cold and disease. Every outdoor
worker should carry a pot in his pocket.

BUSHMAN’S CUTS AND WOUNDS.
Mr. John Brown, 17 Dix Street, Chippendale, Sydney, writes:—“During mv
work in the country at fencing, clearing land and scrub-cutting I have met
with many accidents from axe or knife, some of which have been very
severe. But I always had Zam-Buk in the camp for emergencies, and have
found it splendid. My hands would get terribly knocked about, but Zam-Buk
always healed them in a very rapid manner.
“I have found Zam-Buk absolutely necessary in my bush life, where it is
so far removed from any chance of medical attention.”
BUILDER’S SLICED FINGER.
Mrs. E. Fisher, of 845 Street, West Perth, W.A., says:—“My
husband is a bricklayer, and while trimming: a brick with a trowel he sliced
his finger open. The flesh was laid bare to the bone. Some lime got into the
wound, which inflamed and festered in an alarming manner. Zam-Buk was
applied, and after a fortnight’s treatment the cut was completely healed, all
inflammation being drawn out, and he was able to use his hand.”
