A MINER’S
PRAISE.
“Whilst following my occupation
at the Great Cobar Mine,” says Mr.
.T. Farnsworth, of Becker Street,
Cobar, N.S.W., “I find nothing to
equal Zam-Buk for the cuts and
bruises I am constantly receiving.
No matter how bad the cuts and
bruises —and I sometimes get soma
nasty ones —Zam-Buk takes away
the pain almost instantlv, and in
a day or so the wounds disappear.”
and the ironmongery she called her engines
rattled and raced and made Hell’s delight.
The day before she gets to Yokohama
he’s got ‘-he plan of campaign. The otters
are making the best of it —the end of the job
and damn little money ; but enough to make
’em want to get a berth quick. And so she
comes to Yokohama, and is anchored late in
the afternoon—and she could have been
there by eleven o’clock, but Captain Tommy
had slowed her down at daylight.
“ ‘What for?’ asks the mate.
“ ‘Save coal,’ says Tommy.
“The mate doesn’t see how that matters,
as the ship is to be handed over- to Shigoku
with all faults, just as she is, without one
flea. Coal in her, or no; but just as she is
when they accept her. Yet he sees that
Captain Tommy has a plan, and hope springs
eternal in the Roman beast. So he passes
the word to the second mate, and tells him
to keep his lights burning brightly and be a
careful navigator of all these shoals and
back-washes of cumshaw and corruption.
“At about half-past three he drops the pick,
and at four o’clock lie’s back from shore
with a pretty little Jap in European rig who’s
representing Shigoku and Co. I’m at the
rail, looking down from me little house that
I’m to be evicted from by Shigoku, and sud-
denly I hear Captain Tommy say to the
chap as if he’d known him for years:
■ “ ‘lki,’ he says, ‘you catch ship now—take
delivery and all that, and if it’s any use to
you, it being too late now to send a new
crew aboard to-day, my lads ’ll stand by till
to-morrow.’
“‘I see,’ says Iki. ‘Take of delivery now,
and you are on the ship for us all of the
night.’
“ ‘That’s it—save you sending a new crew
till to-morrow.’
“ ‘You ask no money for stay aboard?’
“ ‘Not a sen. Just you take formal de-
livery, and we’ll have a whisky over it in
my cabin.’
“Iki is flatte-ed all the way at this splendid
Britisher wit! the Oxford and Cambridge
manner. He goes to Tommy’s cabin and
has a whisky—not one, either. You know
they call the Jap a hard-headed cove ; but it’s
a lot like British thoroughness and Ameri-
can hustle and French emotionalism—tre-
mendously exaggerated. A Jap on the
water front, anyhow, thinks life is a joke.
He can’t buy a shipment of coal or sell a
freight without a spree. He carries his
booze cheerfully; and if he once sees a man
quarrelsome in his cups he avoids that chap
—except in the way of profits.
“Well, Captain Tommy had already half
captured Iki by his condescension, and his
offer to guard that ship for a night for
nothing, and the whisky and the cheerful
drunkenness of Iki fixed the lot. Tommy
carried his liquor like a bronze Buddha.
The only effect it had was to make him
more polite, and speak more beautiful Eng-
lish than he'd ever heard at the Oxford and
Cam hr idee boat-race. He pours out the
whisky for Iki; and he tells Iki how clever
a business man Iki is, and Iki takes another
drink, and goes one better in compliments
than the white man. They open the last
box of cigars and smoke there till all’s blue;
and at 6 o’clock Iki tumbles into his boat
and calls the captain his dear friend, and
tells him he must come to a geisha party to-
morrow ; and so goes ashore leaving the
ship, taken delivery of, signed, and sealed,
and all correct.
“Then Captain Tommy started business at
the old stand. He pulled out of the Star
of Samarai the napery and the cutlery, the
glassware and the copper-pots in the galley,
the butcher’s steelyards, his own cabin fit-
ting# and the cushions of the saloon tran-
soms. Wherever brass showed up, or nickel
or electro-plate, he scored. Damask and
other textile fabrics were all one to him,
and if he’d seen a primrose by the river’s
brim it wouldn’t have been a primrose—he’d
have called it loot. If water had been sale-
able he’d have looted the river too. By 9
o’clock he had three boatloads of loot, and
he smuggled it ashore, and came back drunk
and happy about an hour off daylight, with
nine hundred yen in his kick, having sold it
to a junk-shop at a ruinous sacrifice, and
regardless of cost.
“Well, you know if the office-boy knows
the accountant is lifting the boss’s money to
put on a sure thing with the ponies, that
office-boy will pinch the petty cash. As
soon as Captain Tommy goes ashore the
first-officer gets to his with a boatload, and
is off the ship by half-past 10. You can
still hear the oars in the rowlocks when the
second and third start looting, and the boats
the captain took come back to help them.
They get a boatload of cabin stuff —wire-
mattresses and curtain bars and rings, nickel
taps and fittings; and whenever a bit of
metal saw them first it hid itself. And they
eo ashore to look for a junk-shop about
half-past 11. %
“The engineers are up to their dodges,
too—pinching spanners and hammers and
loading the quarter-boat on the port side.
They take any fittings they can, though they
have respect for the engines, which limits
their bushranging; but the propeller is
outside the ship and they have no respect for
it at all—only a grouch, because it’s under
water and they can’t sell it. They can’t
get at the funnel either ; and the capstan is
a fixture, and a winch takes time to change
its address. They wander about the ship
as mad as hatters because the other thieves
haven’t left them anything to steal. In the
end the second engineer comes to the chain
locker, and he gets a brilliant idea.
“ ‘Look here, chief,’ he says to the first
steam-peddler. ‘How many fathom o’ chain
in one o’ these cables?’
“ ‘Hundred and twenty,’ says the chief,
cocking his eye at the cables like a fowl
getting the measure of a grain o’ corn.
“ ‘Hundred and twenty fathom on the port
s;de’s enough,’ says the second; ‘and that
’d give us a hundred fathom on the starb’d
side.’
“I forgot to tell you they’d dropped the
port anchor that afternoon.
“ ‘Good enough,’ says the chief. ‘Open
two links, pinch a hundred fathom o’ chain,
and who’s to tell while the anchor’s peaked
how much cable’s in the locker?’
“Well! They did. They shackled the
anchor, opened a link, subtracted a hundred
fathom o’ chain, linked up again, and cast
off the shackle. They get it to the boat
when the third sings out from the fo’c’s’le
head. He’s looking over the side, and he
sees the starb’d anchor, fastened up against
the hawsehole, and looking superfluous.
“ ‘See here, chief,’ he says. ‘They got two
patent anchors, and one’s perfectly sufficient
for a Jap. They won’t use it,’ he says.
‘Let’s pinch it.’
“ ‘They’d miss it as soon as they got
aboard,’ says the chief. Lord knows why
he thought other things wouldn’t be missed.
By this time the ship looked more like the
Ruins of Pompeii than the Star of Samarai.
“ ‘How can they miss it?” said the third,
‘if we get the carpenter to make a wooden
one, and paint it. They mightn’t use it
for a year.’
“The chief and the second gave three
cheers. They pinched the anchor, and set
the carpenter making another, promising
him 25 yen out of the junk; and they went
ashore at about 1 o’clock in the morning,
and about two inches of freeboard was all
they had. They went to a new junk-shop, so
the stuff was well distributed, and some-
where about daylight they ran into one
another in an all-night saloon, off Main.
The engineers had the bos’n with them,
and they meet him in the all-night joint,
and he’s .well over the Plimsoll.
“ ‘Why didn’t you stay with the boat?’ the
chief said to him. ‘Somebody might shake
it.’
“ ‘No fear o’ that,’ says the bos’n. ‘I
see there was nothing left for me, so I’ve
pinched the boat and sold it.’
“Well, they got aboard a little after day-
light and they find Captain Tommy aboard,
raving in his worst Oxford and Cambridge
manner.
“ ‘Who’s destroyed my beautiful ship?’ he
said. ‘Where are the men who’ve looted the
Star?’
“The chief engineer is well loaded, and he
looks him in the eye and says: ‘The scoun-
drels went ashore just after you took your
three boatloads to the junk-shop.’
“Then Tommy laughs—and tells them to
get their gear together, as the ship is already
delivered. They’re only there as a favor to
the new owners; and incidentally he re-
marked that there’d be the devil of a row. The
bos’n was glad he had not missed the port
quarter boat yet, and the engineers looked
hard at him to see if he grieved over the
loss of the starb’d anchor, but he didn’t know
it—the imitation was so lifelike. There it
was—warship grey, with a bit of painted
rust showing cunningly on the flukes—the
most artistic forgery since William Henry
Ireland wrote Shakspeare.
“All the hands got their dunnage together,
and I was glad I wasn’t in the looting—
though it looked safe enough, even then.
About 9 o’clock comes a chief clerk of
Shigoku’s, and Captain Tommy seemed a bit
relieved when he found this was Shigoku’s
first ship, and that the chief clerk didn’t
know a steamer from, a cumquat. He rati-
fied the delivery taken by Iki and produced
the bank receipt for the payment of the
purchase money to the mean old cuss in
Liverpool; and then he told his captain and
crew to take charge. After a whisky the
ship’s old company, including me. put our-
selves in shore-boats, and you bet we were
mighty glad to hit that dock. We left the
most cock-eyed, incomplete thing in the way
of ships you ever saw, broken-winged on the
port side by the missing boat, and the star-
board anchor looking down to see if the
water was too deep to wade in.
“There was steam in her, and Shigoku’s
new master was as proud as a Pyrmont billy-
goat and in a big hurry to get a move on.
They were moving when Captain Tommy and
the mates and engineers and me ’riekshawed
round the Bund to the Grand Hotel. After
a peg we came out on the water-front, and
there’s the Star of Samarai with the white
flag with the cherry-plum on it floating from
the stern flagstaff; and she’s settling down
to six knots.
“ ‘He won’t clear that shoal,’ says Captain
Tommy. Apparently the Jap master thought
the same. He gave an order. We heard the
chain rattle and then—that damned starb’d
anchor floated —floated till the weight of the
chain pulled it under.
“Shigoku and Co. got her off the shoal at
high water, and we all cleared out as soon
as we could find a railway.”
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Singing Shepherdess.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sister earth and brother sea,
Dancing weed and laughing tree —
All the world is melody !
Airs are full of light and wings,
Bell to bell the blossom swings;
For my love, my true-love, sings.
She is fair and very wise.
In the valor of her eyes
Holy 'purpose dreaming lies.
Music with a long caress
Fondles the white loveliness
Of my singing shepherdess.
Down the scarp and hillside creep,
To the thoughtful waters deep,
All her words, like happy sheep.
They shall drink and they shall climb
Through the clover and the thyme,
Clashing little bells of rhyme.
Sister earth and brother sky,
You shall hear the song go by
To the dappled meadows high.
Woodland myrrh and magic spice—
All the world makes sacrifice
At the gate of Paradise !
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
